O, children, since I have completed this book for you, which is the final one in the science of medicine, by completing it, by its exposition, and by its declaration, it seems to me that I complete it for you with this treatise, which is a part of the manual operation, that is, surgery.
Because the manual operation is widespread in our region and our time, completely neglected, to the point that the science of it is in great danger, and its practice is lost; and only a few small descriptions remain in the books of the Ancients, which have been distorted by the hands of others and have fallen into error and hesitation, so that the understanding of it is closed and its strength and art are diminished.
And it seems to me that I will revitalize it [the manual operation] by the arrangement of this treatise, in accordance with the method of exposition, declaration, and abbreviation; and so that it will be provided with the forms of cautery instruments and other instruments of the craft, as is necessary by adding a declaration and by preparing it.
But the reason why a skilled artisan is not found with his own hands in our time is that the art of medicine is long, and that its practitioner must previously be versed in the science of anatomy, which recounts the body's parts, their shapes, their junctions and separations; and be aware of the bones, nerves, and tendons, their number, and their origins; and of the pulsating and still veins, and the places from which they come forth.
And therefore Hippocrates says that with one name is little, but in the work little, and more so in the art of the hand.
But we have already spoken of this in the introduction to this book, for whoever is not knowledgeable about what we have said about anatomy, is not spared from falling into error, by which men will die, just as I have seen many such, for some are trained in this science and throw themselves into it without knowledge and experience.
For I saw a foolish and unskilled doctor lance a scrofulous abscess on a woman's neck, and cut some of the neck's arteries, so that a great deal of blood flowed until the woman died in his hands.
And I saw another doctor attempt to remove a stone from a man who had suffered for many years, and the stone was large, and he came to him and removed that stone with a piece of the bladder, so that the man died on the third day.
And I myself warned him to refrain, and I saw that because of the size of the stone and the patient's condition, I knew beforehand that he would die.
And I saw another surgeon, who took a stipend from a duke in our land, and came to a dark-complexioned man whose thigh was fractured; and the surgeon, through his ignorance, straightened the fracture, pressing on the wound with pads and splints with excessive force, and did not allow the wound to breathe.
And then he unbound it according to his desires, and then he bound it again, and then he left it for several days and commanded that we unbind the ligament; and it remained like this until the thigh and the leg became abscessed, and it perished.
And I was called to him, and I proceeded to unbind the ligament, and tranquility was achieved, and thus he was relieved of his pain.
But meanwhile, the corruption had already been established in the limb, so that I could not restrain it, and the corruption continued to spread in the limb until the patient perished.
And I saw another physician who pierced a cancerous abscess, and it ulcerated for several days, until the evil of it, with the patient's own body, was greatly magnified.
The reason for this is that the cancerous ulcer is pure melancholy humor, so it should not be addressed with iron in its entirety, but rather the limb should be afflicted by being wholly removed.
Therefore, children, it is necessary for you that the surgical operation be divided into two phases: one is the operation associated with health, and the other is the work that carries significant risk in the most vulnerable conditions.
And I have already shown this throughout this book, where operations carry error and fear; therefore, it is necessary for you to avoid this and abandon it, lest the leaves (or chapters) find a way to speak and bring dishonor upon you.
Therefore, take your tools with diligence and God's protection, and your patients with skill and firmness.
And use the best approach, by God's grace, to achieve health and commendable success, and abandon the dreadful illnesses with difficult cures, and remove from your tools whatever makes you fearful, so that your zeal and your name are not lost. For greater glory awaits you, and even higher in the world and beyond your holiness.
Gualia has already said in some of his teachings: Do not treat a serious illness, lest you be called bad doctors".
And I have divided this book into three chapters.
The division of the book begins, and first of the first part. The first chapter, ordered from head to foot, discusses the types of instruments and the tools for cauteries, and everything necessary for the operation.
The division of the second part. The second chapter, on cutting and piercing, cupping, poultices, and the extraction of arrows and similar objects,  is fully described and ordered by the types of instruments.
The third chapter, on restoration and dislocation, and the treatment of twisting and similar injuries; ordered from head to foot, and detailing the types of instruments.
It is necessary for me to describe the benefits of this cautery and its harms, and in which constitution cauterization is forbidden.
Therefore, I say that the discussion of the benefits and harms of cautery is a long, detailed, and subtly scientific discourse, a secret hidden from view; many wise men have spoken of it, and they have disagreed, but a small part of their discussions will suffice for me, fearing to prolong the writing.
And therefore I say that cautery benefits all constitutions universally, whether with or without matter, except for two constitutions, which are hot constitutions without matter.
In the hot and dry constitution with matter, there is disagreement; for some say that cautery is beneficial for it, and others say the opposite, that cautery is not suitable for an illness which is caused by heat and dryness, because the nature of fire is heat and dryness, and it is unsuitable for a hot and dry illness to be cured with something that is also hot and dry.
And those who say the opposite claim that cautery provides benefit in all hot and dry illnesses affecting the human body, for when you compare the body and its humors to the nature of fire, you find the human body to be cold.
And I say, concerning that matter, that my own experience has revealed this to me several times.
However, this should not be adopted by anyone who has not already been thoroughly practiced and trained in the chapter of cautery through prior exercises, and has grasped the diversity of human constitutions, the nature of illnesses and their various characteristics, their causes, their symptoms, and the timeframe involved.
But in other constitutions, there is no need for such hesitation, and especially in cold and humid illnesses, for all physicians have agreed upon this, and there is no disagreement about the benefits of cautery in those cases.
And know, my children, that the secret of healing through cautery with fire, and its efficacy, lies in contrast to cautery with medicinal agents.  The action of fire is direct, affecting only the part that is cauterized, and does not harm other, nearby, parts except to a minimal degree.
However, the action of a burning medicine extends to adjacent parts, and very easily causes a difficult-to-heal illness in a nearby limb, and often results in hearing loss.
But fire, by its intensity and beneficial nature, does not do this unnecessarily. This is known to us through experiment and prolonged study of the art, concerning the truth of causes.  Therefore, I excuse myself from further lengthy discourse.  If it were not that this topic, concerning the secret of fire's use, is not included in my written text, and if the method of its operation in the body, and the expulsion of illnesses through it, were not already discussed in a detailed philosophical demonstration, which could enlighten your understanding.
And know, children, that they disagree about the best time for cautery, and they suggest the springtime as the most suitable time.
And I say that cautery is appropriate at any time, because the potential harm from a delay is outweighed by the benefit of the cautery itself, and especially if the cautery is needed for intense and sudden pain, pains which cannot tolerate delay, as the potential for greater harm from the disease is feared more than the slight harm of a delay.
And this is not for your understanding, children, or it is not considered by sensible doctors; this is, that for an illness cured by cautery, there is no single best time, and they suggest that conclusion.
And the reason is not as they think about it; for cautery is not just any treatment, but a method that alters the temperament and dries the moistures that cause the pain.
But nevertheless, cautery is superior to other treatments because of its power, efficacy, and the greatness of its effect.
And it is possible that their illness will return at some time, depending on the patient's temperament, the location of the illness, and the strength of the illness itself, and the accumulation of superfluities within them, and what they have consumed in food and similar things; however, if the illness for which cautery is administered is a minor illness in a limb with few superfluities and moistures, like toothache and similar ailments, then it is possible that the pain will not return, but this is done gradually.
And it is a common saying that cautery is the last resort in medicine, and it is a true statement, not in the way they understand it, for they believe there is no cure that is as effective as cautery.
And the reason is the opposite of that; and the understanding that cautery is the last resort in medicine is only true when we have tried various treatment methods for certain illnesses, and those treatments did not work, and then, at the end of the effort, cautery is used, and it proves effective for that specific case. Therefore, the statement that cautery is the last resort in medicine is not true according to the understanding of the common people and many foolish physicians.
And the ancients said that cautery with gold is better than cautery with iron, and they did not say this except due to the purity and superior substance of gold.
And they said that the location of the cautery doesn't cause harm, although this is not entirely true, for I have tested it and found that this only happens in some bodies and not others.  Cautery with gold is good and better than with iron, just as they said; however, when you perform cautery with gold-fueled fire, it's not clear to you how much gold to use due to the gold's tendency to absorb cold.  If you add too much gold over the heated area, the gold will melt in the fire, causing the artisan to lose control of the process.
Therefore, cautery with iron is done more easily and more directly towards the correct procedure.
And I have organized this chapter on cautery according to sections, and I have arranged them from head to feet, so that the inquirer may find what they want.
The first chapter.
This cauterization is beneficial for dampness and cold affecting the brain, the causes of which are twofold: one is the cause of a large flow of rheumatic fluid, called "corissa," flowing from the head to the parts of the eyes and ears, and a multitude of sleep disorders, toothache, and sore throat.  Generally, all illnesses caused by cold, such as paralysis, stroke, and similar afflictions.
The method of this cauterization is as follows: first, you must order the patient to be purged with laxative medicines, cleansing the head, for three or four nights, according to the patient's strength, age, and constitution.
Then you instruct him to shave his head, and have him lie on a smooth bench, placing his hands on his feet.
And then, place the blade of your palm (or, the blade of the cautery tool)  near the bridge of his nose between his eyes. However, when your middle finger has reached that spot, mark that place with a probe, and then ignite the olive cautery, of which the method is this:
And then place that [cautery] upon the spot marked with the probe, so that it descends gradually, your hand being tightened little by little, and you turn it.
And then lift your hand slightly and examine the spot.  If you see a significant amount of bone exposed, roughly the size of a grain of millet, then lift your hand; and if not, return your hand with that iron, with another of those [irons], until the bone is visible as I have told you.
And then take a little salt and dissolve it over the area, and leave it for three days.
And then place a cloth soaked in vinegar on it and leave it until the scab from the fire is destroyed. And then treat the area with a four-herb ointment until it is healed.
And if the wound remains open, the arm should be raised, because this is better and more helpful.
And some of them say that the bone should be cauterized down to the bone, and the cautery should be held until some part of the bone's tissue has adhered.
And then remove what is adhering to the bone, and then it should be treated.
And others say that with the cautery, one should penetrate the back, to strongly press on the bone until the bone breaks, and this is the method of small amputation: And they believe that vapors from the head escape from that location.
And the wound should be kept open for a long time, and then treated until it is healed.
But I have not seen these two types of cautery in all cases, only in some individuals, according to a method of fear; and in my opinion, it is better with healing, when it is; for the head is weakened by the interruption of its continuity, as we have testified in other limbs, and especially when the patient's head is of a frail nature.
And the first type of cautery is safer and better in my opinion; and by that use and method, healing occurs.
Second Chapter.
Of the cautery of the head.
When chronic and long-lasting pain occurs throughout the head in a prolonged illness, and treatments using leeches, cupping, head purges, plasters, poultices, cauteries, goose-fat, and similar remedies have been employed, and especially if the head has already been cauterized with a cautery which has proven ineffective for the patient, then consider that if the patient's head is of strong constitution, and not frail, and endures considerable coldness, then cauterize with another cautery slightly below the initial one. And then cauterize that area on the corner of their head with a cautery until the affected area is broken open. Incise the affected area, and expose the bone to the extent that we have described, and cauterize that area on the back of the head, in the location called the 'vas' (or vein) — which is the spot where a cupping device is placed. And ease your hand in this, and do not expose the bone, for the patient will experience very great pain, different from the pain caused by other cauteries on the head.
And I would place this cautery in its proper location, because the cautery used to cauterize the temples and the back of the head should be a finer instrument than the one used to cauterize the middle of the head; and this is the procedure.
Chapter three.
On the cauterization of non-chronic migraines.
When there is persistent pain in the head, or spreading pain to the eye, the head should be treated with purifying medicines, and the other treatments mentioned in the descriptions of illnesses should be administered. If none of this works, cauterization in that area can be done in two ways: either by cauterization with a sharp, burning medicine, or with iron.
However, cauterization with a burning medicine involves taking a piece of garlic, squeezing it, and cutting off the tips of both ends. Then, with a sharp lancet, cut the painful area on the temple until a small cavity is made under the skin, and it is hidden.
Then, apply a compress to the area to firmly constrict it, and leave it for three hours.  After that, remove it, and discard the garlic.  Leave the wound for two or three days, and then apply a piece of cloth soaked in wine until the area heals.
And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, do this with some burning medicines, which I have written about in the second treatise on burning medicines.
But cauterization with iron, according to this method:  with fire as a cauterizing agent, of which this is the form:
and it is called "claus clavilhs," because the head of it is shaped like a nail, in which there is some curvature, and in the middle of it there is a small eminence.
And then place it on the painful area, and hold your hand — so that the surrounding flesh is not burned — and rotate the small iron slowly and swiftly.
And if the amount that is burned by the force of the cauterization is as much as half of it, then lift your hand so that the artery beneath is not burned, lest there be a flow of blood.
And then soak a cotton pad in salt water and place it on the area, and leave it for three days; and then place a cotton pad soaked in broth, and then treat with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, cauterize with the sharp edge of the knife-like cautery, and beware of cutting an artery in this particular, not-ancient, procedure.
Fourth Chapter.
Of the cauterization of an old migraine.
When you treat a migraine with what we have said about the previous treatment, and what we have said in the divisions of illnesses, and the treatment does not work, and sometimes because the illness does not respond to what we have said about the first cauterization with medicine or with fire cauterization, then you should heat the knife-cautery until it is white hot, then apply it to the area of pain in a line the length of half a finger, or that much; and hold your hand steady once, and push until you cut the artery and reach the bone.
However, it is necessary for you to avoid damaging the continuity of the jawbone, which is strained by chewing, and to ensure that the ligaments and nerves that move it are not injured, and that a spasm does not develop.
And if it is about the care and caution of the flow of blood from the cut artery, because there is fear in cutting it, and especially for someone who is ignorant of what they are doing and is not skilled and experienced; and leaves the operation unfinished at the beginning.
And I will come back to the treatment of accidental blood flow from an artery in its own manner, in its own section of this book.
And if you see that this illness is not helped by this cauterization and you see the patient's condition as tolerable, cauterize that middle part of the head as we have described, and treat the wound until it is healed.
And if you wish, administer the cautery that we have described in the chapter on cutting arteries with a cautery having two blades, and this is a better and more beneficial cauterization.
Chapter 5.
Of cauterization of ear aches.
When pain in the ear comes from cold and is treated with laxatives and other treatments as described in the previous section, and the pain does not subside, then apply the cautery, and this is the one called a *puncta*, and this is its shape:
And then apply the cautery to the affected area, and let it be moved around the entire ear in a circular motion, or in a circuit over each affected spot if the pain is in several places. Move the cautery along the ear from the base slowly, after you have marked the area with a probe.
And if the cautery is applied with ten points to the entire ear, then cauterize those spots, and then treat the areas until they are healed.
Chapter 6.
Of cauterization of tumors.
Tumors which are cured by cauterization are only of the types that arise from phlegm, as we have said in the divisions of illnesses.
Avoid cauterization of the type that arises from dryness or spasm of the nerve.
When you treat that type of tumor with poultices, emetics, and remedies, and your treatment does not work, then you must cauterize the patient with three cauteries: one at the root of the ear, a second a little below their temple, and the third at the juncture of the two lips.
And place your cautery opposite the affected area, for softening occurs only in the area that appears healthy.
And the method of cauterization is to cauterize with the cautery at the right end of the ear, slightly above the corner of the head, and another at the temple, the length of which should be according to the length of the finger, and you hold the cautery until the quantity of half of the affected area is burned.
And this is the method of cauterization, and it is a type of surgical procedure, the method of which is described below. However, it is more subtle than what you have seen; and it is necessary to cut away some of the superfluous flesh.
And then treat the area with whatever was previously used until it is healed.
Chapter 7.
On cauterization for old apoplexy.
When apoplexy is old, and you have treated it with what we have described, but your treatment isn't working and the patient has no fever, then cauterize with four cauteries: on all the corners of their head; and with one cautery on the middle of the head, as we said before; and with a cautery on the temple. And the method of cauterization is done according to what has been previously stated.
And it has already been cauterized on the opening of the stomach; and further back; and then it should be treated with what follows.
Chapter 8.
Concerning the cauterization of forgetfulness caused by flame.
It is necessary to give the patient at the beginning large jars and pills, clarifying the brain.
And then wash the entire head of the patient and place on their forehead a plaster of the kind described in the treatise on plasters.
And apply it several times, for this is a method of cauterization. And do this according to the same order which I have already stated.
And so, if this treatment is effective, good; but if not, then apply three cauteries to the back part of his head, descending from the highest part of the head down to the lowest part of the neck. And place the width of the finger between each cautery, and then treat the cautery with what has been previously described.
But if the application and the patient tolerate it, then apply a cautery in the middle area; and if you wish a greater application, then cauterize it at both horns.
Then treat it until it is healed; and let the cautery be olive-shaped, following the previously given form.
Chapter 9.
Of cauterization for paralysis and softening of the whole body.
You must proceed with cleansing the head with herbs and as we have said; then shave the patient's head, and then cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head, and with a cautery on all the horns of the head, and with a cautery on the back part of it, and with three cauteries on the vertebrae of the neck.
And if in the illness of softening of the body, the patient is enduring it and the illness is severe, then cauterize the patient with four cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, and continuously with a cautery until the bone is loosened somewhat, and lift your hand. And then treat it until it is healed, and this should be an olive cautery.
Chapter 10.
On the cauterization of epilepsy.
The epileptic is not cauterized unless the epilepsy is due to phlegm.
Therefore, it is necessary to treat the brain from the beginning with herbs and the other treatment we have mentioned in the division when the patient is severe and tolerates the application of medicines.
And if it is a child who does not tolerate the medicines, then administer poultices and masticatories that cleanse the brain, doing so for many days, with a beneficial diet.
And then, bind the patient's head, and then cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head according to the previous method, and with another cautery on the back of the head, and cauterize on each temple. And if the patient is strong and tolerates it, then cauterize with the cauteries mentioned, having paralysis and softening of the body over the spinal vertebrae of the neck and back.
Let the cautery be olive-like, as previously described.
But if the patient is a child, then apply a small cautery according to this method.
Chapter 11.
Concerning the cauterization of melancholy.
When the causes of melancholy are corrupted moisture and thick phlegm, then cauterize that with the cauteries which we have mentioned in the previous discussion of paralysis.
And if the cause of melancholy is an excess tending toward melancholy, and the patient's body is moist, then give him to drink that which cleanses his brain, according to what has been previously stated in the division.
And then shave the patient's head, and then apply a properly made cautery to the scalp, and then place it in the middle of the head.
And the patient should be seated upright, and held steady on all sides.
And then apply a liniment; and the best is an old sheep's wool. And warm that over the fire with moderate heat, as much as the patient can tolerate when it is applied. And then place that in the middle of the patient's head on the scalp, and leave it until it is cooled.
And do this to the patient once a week, with the other treatments, until he is healed; or if you wish, cauterize that place with small punctures according to the patient's tolerance, but keep your hand with a cautery so it is adequately heated: this is a type of cautery that moistens the brain evenly.
And then place that cloth soaked in wool or hen's dung.
Chapter 12.
Of cauterization of descending water in the eye.
When you have observed the beginning of water in the eye, according to the signs I have mentioned in the previous section, then begin giving the patient drinks that will strengthen his head and guard it from all moisture.
And have him sweat in a bath for several days.
And then order him to shave his head, and cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head; and then cauterize with two cauteries above the two temples if the beginning of the water's infection is in both eyes, or with one on one side if the water's infection is in one eye.
Cut with a cautery all the veins and arteries that are below the crown; and let the cauteries be in that line, at the width of the temples.
And beware of blood flow when cauterizing; and if you see blood flowing from him, stop it with some medicine, as is possible for you.
And I will come with knowledge that is in the extraction of arteries and with the excision of them and the caution of blood flow.
And sometimes it is cauterized in the neck below two bones with two cauteries directly applied.
Chapter 13.
Of the cauterization of old sores.
When sores on the eyelids are old, and persistently through the veins and arteries, which are in the visible area of the head's exterior, and it is verified that this is superfluous cold phlegm, then cauterize it with the cautery that we have recounted in the chapter about initially applying hot water to sores, and with a cautery in the middle part of the head, and with two cauteries on the neck below the two temples, and with two cauteries on the neck below the two bones. And if you need additional treatment, then cauterize that along the entire eyelid edge on the eyebrow's end with a small cautery.
Chapter 14.
Of the cauterization of nasal foul odor.
When you treat it with what we have described in the division, and your treatment is not effective, then prepare and give the patient potions to drink for three nights; and afterwards, shave the patient's head and cauterize with a smaller cautery and an olive-oil cautery. Then cauterize the area with a clove-shaped cautery, using two cauteries on the two eyebrows below the hair, a small one each.
And be careful of the arc, so you don't set it on fire.
And this is the form of the clove-shaped cautery.
Chapter 15.
Of cauterization for softening the eyelids of the eyes.
When the eyelid of the eye is softened by illness or moisture, then cauterize that eyelid with a cautery using this lunar cautery; and this is its form:
And if you cauterize that area above the two eyebrows slightly with two cauteries on every part, and let them be elongated towards the temples. And let the length of each cautery be according to the size of the eyebrow.
And do not press your hand with the cautery too hard, but according to the amount that the third part of the eyelid is adhered.
Chapter 16.
Of cautery of the eyelid when the hairs are turned inward and pierce the eye.
When the hairs of the eye are turned inward so that they pierce the eye, cauterization in those cases is in two ways: either with burning or with a burning medicine.
If with a cautery using fire, command the patient beforehand to remove their own hairs in the same way that barbers do, until they are drawn out and plucked; and if you pierce the skin near their roots, then you must cover the eyes with a bandage so they are not irritated until the hairs are grown back.
Then, when they have grown back and are plucked, place the patient's head in your lap, and then gently press on the eyelid of their eyes with a probe, using a marking that resembles the shape of a myrtle leaf, and have the beginning of the marking near the hairs.
And then apply cotton soaked in egg white or a ptillium mucilage to the eye.
And then apply the cautery, which has this form.
And then cauterize along the marked area, little by little, in many applications, until the surface of the skin, which is similar to a myrtle leaf, is completely burned.
The sign of successful treatment is that you see the eyelid contracted and the hairs raised toward the puncture in the eye.
And then lift your hand and leave it for three days, and then apply cotton with a bandage until the scab is removed. And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
However, if any cause returns to the hair follicles and the eyelid softens, then do the cautery again in the same way as you did initially.
But if the hairs are beneath the eyelid, then cauterize it until it is raised to its natural position, healed, and the hairs no longer prick the eye.
But the cautery with burning medicine is what you instruct the patient to do to the hairs until they are elongated and healed, and then a leaf-shaped piece of cloth is made resembling a myrtle leaf.
And then take known medicinal herbs and fresh uncooked lard (or something like that), and thoroughly mix these ingredients quickly so they don't get cold.  Then spread this mixture on the leaf-shaped cloth, and place it over the affected eye(s). Place a cloth soaked in egg white under the eye.  And the patient's head should be supported by you.
And place your finger on the medicine and press it gently, and do not remove it until the patient feels the medicine's burning sensation, for the burning should be like that of fire.
And thus, when the patient feels the burning sensation, remove the medicine and gently wipe it away with your finger; then, when the burning subsides, remove the medicine and wash the eye with water.  Then see if the eyelid has been raised, just as if it were cured by cauterization with fire or incision; if so, it is good. If not, put more medicine on the area where the previous medicine did not reach, and do not stop until the wound is healed, and the eye is closed.
Then apply a bandage over it until the burn is healed, and then treat it with a final ointment or other remedy until it is completely cured.
You must, during your treatment, diligently ensure that no medicine gets into the eye.  If, after a few days, the eyelid becomes softened and something from the lashes enters the eye, then repeat your application of medicine to the softened part of the eyelid, just as you did at the beginning, and continue until it is healed.
And this is the method for treating eye injuries.
And know that human eye injuries vary in size and severity, and therefore your treatment is tailored accordingly.
And the path to healing in this art should not be hidden from the student.
Chapter 17.
Concerning the cauterization of fistulas in the tear duct of the eye.
When you treat a fistula, as described in the divisions of diseases, and your treatment is not effective, then you should cauterize it in this manner: instruct the patient to rest their head on your lap, and have an assistant hold the patient's head between your hands, so that it is neither moved nor shaken by the patient's head movements.
And then place a piece of linen soaked in egg white or gum arabic over the patient's eye.
And then apply the cautery, which has this shape:
which is concave, like the shape of a reed or a hawk's wing, on the one end where the cautery is made.
And if you wish, it should penetrate into the other end. And if you wish, it should be shaped like a probe.
However, the concave one is better for your work.
And then press the fistula if it is open, and extract the pus and dry it out. And if it is not open, then open it and extract the pus from it.
And then place the cautery, which is well ignited, over it. Hold your hand a short distance from the cautery over the area of the nose, so that neither your hand nor the patient is disturbed. Then place the cautery on the ulcer and cauterize it.
And so, if at the beginning your cautery reaches the bone, it is good.  But if not, then repeat the cautery once or twice, if necessary.  Leave it like that for three days, and then apply a compress with wool and surround it with a drying ointment until it is healed.
But if several days have passed and it is not healed, then apply a sharp, corrosive medicine until the bone is exposed and is smooth according to what is suitable in its chapter.
And there is another method of cauterizing a fistula, which some earlier doctors said:  Apply to the area of the fistula and make a hole there. Then, place in that fissure a fine, thin piece of material, such as: and pour into it a quantity of molten lead. Hold your hand firmly on the instrument and prevent the patient from moving, so that the molten lead does not flow onto the wound.  It is necessary to place a compress soaked in egg white or water over the patient's wound.  For molten lead adheres to the fistula area and heals it through a remarkable cure.
And if the fistula is healed by what we have said about the cautery and treatment, it is good.  But if not, then it is necessary to administer a trepanation of the nose and reduce the fistula to the nasal cavity, according to its proper place.
Chapter 18.
Of the cauterization of the fissure of the pot.
Often fissures occur in the lips, and more often in the lips of children.

Chapter 21.
Of the cauterization of toothache.
When toothache is caused by cold, or there is cold or a worm there, and you do not consider medical treatment appropriate, then the cauterization in that case is done in two ways: either cauterization with cow dung, or cauterization with fire.
Cauterization with dung is done by taking cow dung, boiling it in an iron container, then taking a piece of cloth and wrapping it around the end of a probe, immersing it in the boiled dung, and quickly applying it to the aching tooth until it cools down.
And then repeat this a few times until the effect of the heat reaches the root of the tooth.
And if you wish, immerse wool or cloth in the aforementioned dung, place that on the aching tooth, and place the heated iron on top of it until the iron reaches the opening of the tooth cavity.
However, cauterization with fire is done by holding a heated iron or bronze rod, ensuring that it is thick enough so that the heat doesn't reach the patient's mouth.
Then heat the cautery iron to the desired temperature, place it on the tooth and hold it there until it cools; do this several times until the pain is gone either that day or the next.
And it is necessary that, with the application of this cautery, the patient fill their mouth with good dung and keep it in for an hour, and then spit it out; and this is the method of cauterization.

Chapter 28.

And it is necessary that the cautery for children be subtle, according to their size and measure.
And then treat the cauterized area for three days with a poultice until the scab from the fire is gone.
And then treat it with other ointments until it is healed.
And the patient should lie on their back for forty days until the area and the wound are sealed.
And it is necessary to regulate the patient's nourishment according to the length of their treatment, which will support their stomach and prevent the intestine from coming out due to the effort of healing.
And then, when approximately 40 days have passed, administer a properly made bandage, and let them wear that for another 40 days.
And abstain from work, overeating, and strong drink, for if they follow this regimen of complete healing, they will be cured.
And I will now describe the treatment for ruptures in other parts of the abdomen, and if, at the beginning, you want to prevent them from emerging, cauterize the rupture with a round cautery, according to its size, and let it dry out as much as one third of it; then treat it with what we have already described, and do not continue further.
And some early practitioners would say that a rupture should be cauterized with a triangular cautery, following this shape, once it has been marked with a probe:
And a line is drawn along the widest part of the rupture, and another line above it; and you use a wedge-shaped cautery, but the first, lighter cautery is better.
Chapter 46.
Of the cauterization of torsion.
When pain arises in some limbs due to injury or impact, and that pain persists for a long time and you find no cure with medicine, then it is necessary to cauterize with a pointed cautery.
And let the cautery be appropriate to the size and smallness of the limb, its strength and weakness, the location of the torsion, and the pain it causes.
So if it is healed by the first cautery, well and good; but if not, then repeat the cautery; for it is customary with such pains that they are located in a limb adjacent to the one that is affected, so you must treat them with cauterization until the patient is healed.
Chapter 47.
Of the cauterization of leprosy.
Some cases of leprosy are greatly helped by cautery, especially if the type of leprosy is caused by the putrefaction of phlegm and melancholy.
And therefore, when you wish to cauterize it, consider if the leprosy is recent and curable with what we have said in the division, and if not, and if you fear that the corruption has spread throughout the body and affected the whole constitution, then cauterize that part of the head with five cauteries: one in the middle of the head, and a second below that, along the hairline; one on each temple; and one at the back of the neck or nape of the neck.
Finally, hold the cautery on the skin until it creates a small indentation in the bone, so that the release of thick vapors is relieved from that area, and cauterize over the spleen as necessary.
And if the leprosy has already spread significantly and is clearly visible, then you should cauterize with the cauteries mentioned for the head; and with a cautery on the tip of the nose, and with two cauteries on each cheekbone, and with two cauteries on two vertebrae of the neck, and with six cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, and one large one on the vertebrae near the hips, one on the hips, and two on the ribs, one on each rib, and two on the elbows, and one on each elbow, and two on the thighs, and two on the muscles, and two on the tendons of each foot; and cauterize on all the joints of the fingers and toes, and on each heel of the feet, and on the hollows of the hands.
And be careful of the nerves that are over the heel so that you do not burn them.
And sometimes, it is cauterized with one cautery on the hip bone, another on the mouth of the stomach, and another on the liver.
And know that the more cauteries you apply, the more helpful and beneficial it is; and know that the patient does not feel pain from the cautery, as they do from wounds, because the body is already numbed.
And the cauteries should be large or small, according to the limbs and joints, in accordance with the described types of instruments.
And then treat the cauterized area with flour of barley cooked with honey and with the other treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 48.
Concerning the cauterization of numbness.
When a limb is numb or asleep and is treated with medicines, oils, and plasters, and is not healed, then cauterize that numb limb with cauteries, according to the size of the limb.
And let your cautery enter the skin slightly. And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And sometimes, numbness affecting the hands and feet, and the spinal column, as well as the nerves that supply that limb, is cauterized, and the numbness is removed.
However, do not presume upon this unless you are knowledgeable about the anatomy of the limbs and the course of the nerves throughout the body.
Chapter 49.
Of the cauterization of ulcers that are morphea.
When an ulcer is old and no medicinal ointment helps, then cauterize over it with a cautery, applying a little heat according to the severity of the ulcer, until the whiteness and discoloration are removed, and the colour of the affected area is changed.
And then treat it with flour of barley and lentils mixed with rosemary oil and leaves of arnica, and with a portion of pigeon blood or swallow blood (each), all mixed together.  Then bandage the area with a cloth and apply it until healed.
Chapter 50.
Of the cauterization of cancer.
When cancer is starting and you want to remove it, then cauterize it with a circular cautery around it, in the same way it is wrapped.
And the learned say it should be cauterized with the back cautery in the middle area; but I do not see that, for I fear it will be ulcerated, and I have seen that happen several times.
And it is more correct that it should be cauterized around it with a circle, as we have said, or with many cauteries.
Chapter 11.
Of cauterization of tumors.
When a tumor is delayed in maturation, and this is due to the excess of its formation, or the age of the patient, for they are old and frail, with little blood, or due to time, and you want to accelerate the maturation of the tumor; then cauterize around it with many small, pointed cauteries; and then leave it alone, for it will hasten its maturation.
But if you want to pierce it with a cautery, then you heat the cautery, of which this is the shape:  and place it in the middle of the tumor until it pierces through the skin.
And if the cautery is in a lower location, so that the arm's condition is relieved; and then treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 52.
Of the cauterization of corrosives.
A corrosive is a wandering corruption in the limb, which corrodes it as if a fire consuming dry wood.
Therefore, if that corrosive is in a location suitable for cauterization with fire, then you should use cauteries with small, or large points, according to what is appropriate for the location where the corrosive is.
And then cauterize it from all sides until you eradicate the entire corruption and no trace of it remains.
And then leave it for three days, and apply a dressing of oil mixed with lint to the cauterized areas until the scabs are all eradicated and all corruption is gone.
And then treat it with a flesh-regenerating ointment.
But if after three days you see healthy flesh growing where there is no corruption, it is good; but if not, then repeat the cauterization on the remaining corrupted areas.
And sometimes a corrosive medicine is used, for it acts as a substitute for cautery. But cautery with fire is a more immediate remedy.
And I have already described the treatment with a sharp medicine in the division, so use it when you need it.
Chapter 53.
Of cauterization of ulcers and other similar afflictions.
Many times, in the lower parts of the diseased foot, a disease called an ulcer forms, and is a large, festering sore, which afflicts the foot; and cauterization in those cases is done in two ways: cauterization with fire and cauterization with hot water.
But cauterization with fire is to ignite a concave cautery, resembling a kind of small vulture, which is made of iron, according to the size required to contain the ulcer completely, and made of fine edges.
And then place that ignited cautery on the ulcer, and then move your hand with the cautery in a circular motion over the ulcer until the cautery reaches the depth of the ulcer. And leave it there for three days until it begins to discharge pus. And then apply a poultice of crushed wild mallows with salt, and leave the poultice on it for one night, for that will uproot it from its roots.
And then treat the area with a healing ointment until it is healed.
And if this doesn't work, and this often happens on the surface of the body, especially in the hands and feet, then you must apply cauterization with water.  Take a cannula of bronze or iron or the wing of a vulture, and place it over the ulcer.  Then pour a small amount of hot water into the cannula, hold your hand, and move it in a circular motion with pressure all around. Make sure the edge of the cannula is pressed against the root of the ulcer, and the hot water will reach the root of the ulcer.  Wait a little for the patient to feel the effects of the hot water for about an hour, and then remove it, for the ulcer with its roots will be eradicated.
And thus do with those and then with the others until you have treated all those affected on the body.
And then treat those wounds, since the roots of the ulcers have been eradicated with these ointments, which cause the flesh to regenerate.
Chapter 54.
Concerning the cauterization of tremors.
When someone has tremors due to cold in the nerves, or due to a cold fever, or another similar cause, then you should cauterize that person with four or five cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, one cautery between each vertebra; and use a cautery on their feet, and an olive-oil cautery on their stomach.
And the tremor in him subsides, and hastens the health of the cold illness.
Chapter 55.
Concerning the cauterization of white spots (or blemishes) on the body.
When white spots, or pustules and fissures appear on the body, caused by corrupted thick matter; then, at the outset, as soon as they appear, it is necessary to cauterize the entire head of the fistula with a thin cautery made of myrtle wood, the tip of which is heated; or with the roots of long-leafed herbs; or with a lens-shaped cautery.
And sometimes a cauterizing agent is added at the beginning of their expulsion in this manner; and if they are not believed and the superfluity is broken up by these means, they are cured by these means.
However, this must be done alongside bloodletting.
Chapter 56.
Of cauterization of accidental blood flow by arrow incision.
Many times there is blood flow from an arrow wound that cuts toward the affected area, or toward an incision of abscesses or cauterization of the limb, and similar cases, and it is difficult to control that.
Therefore, when this happens to someone, you should hold the arrow wound opening with your hand, and place your index finger over it, pressing it repeatedly until the blood flow stops under your finger and does not come out again.
And then place the olive cauteries, small and large, in a number of them, in the fire, and blow on them until they are very hot.
And then take one of those, small or large, according to the location and wound where the artery is broken, quickly pressing it onto that vein after you've stopped the bleeding with your finger, and hold the cautery until the bleeding is cauterized and stopped.
And if the blood is expelled from the artery's opening when you remove your finger and lift the cautery, then take another cautery carefully from the cauteries prepared in the fire, and keep doing this repeatedly until the blood is stopped.
And be careful not to burn the nerve that is here, and to cause further harm to the patient.
And know that when blood flows from the artery, it is not possible to stop the bleeding, especially when the artery is large, except in one of four ways: either with a cautery as we have said, or with extraction if it is not already extracted, for when it is extracted, the patient's extremities are more easily burned and the bleeding is stopped, or it is bound with a thread or bandage by firmly tightening, or medicaments whose virtue is to stop the bleeding are placed on it; and they are tightly pressed with a cushion with appropriate constriction.
However, whoever administers constriction with bandages or by applying burning substances, does not achieve success quickly.
But if this happens to someone and the doctor is not present nor is there medicine, then stop the bleeding by applying pressure with the index finger over the opening of the vein as we have said, and apply great pressure until the blood is stopped in the primary part of the wound, and the finger should not be removed from over the wound; and over the wound, sprinkle with very cold water until the blood has congealed and thickened;  and more care should be taken where cauterization or medication is needed.
Of incisions, perforations, leeches, phlebotomy, wounds, and similar matters.
We have already stated in the first chapter every illness in which cautery with fire is appropriate, along with burning medicines, and the occasions and causes of it, and the instruments necessary for those cauteries, along with the forms of those cauteries; and I have organized the chapters from the supremacy of the head down to the feet.
And I will proceed in this chapter in the same manner, so that the seeker may be relieved of this, concerning whatever he has inquired about.
And before I begin this, children, it is necessary to understand that in this chapter the content is similar to the first chapter on cauterization.
And for that reason, great caution is needed, for in the operation described in this chapter, many times evacuation occurs, which is with blood, the lifeblood, from the incision of a vein or an incision over an abscess or the opening of a wound or the purification of a sore or the removal of an arrow or the incision of a stone, and their similar cases. And with all of those [actions] are associated fear and trembling, and in them often comes death.
And I warn you that this, which brings forth danger, should not befall you, for sometimes in the treatment of men with numerous illnesses, [dangerous] situations may arise. And of these [situations], some will be angered by their illness, and death is easy for them, and this is due to the intensity of their illness, and the duration of their illness, and the illness itself is a fear that signifies death.
And among those is the one who gives you their possessions and says, "You hope for their health," and their illness is harmful.
Therefore, you should not consent to the one who comes to you with this kind of treatment; and your caution should be greater than greed and your rashness; and do not presume any cause for it except for certain knowledge that verifies it for you, and that leads to a praiseworthy outcome.
And in the treatment of all your patients, use prognosis and indication of the way to health, for in that is help for you in acquiring fame and memory, praise and glory.
Oh, children, may God inspire your understanding and not withhold direction and protection from you, for that is in his hand.
And I have ordered this chapter, distinguishing it by sections, as preceded in the chapter on cauteries, concerning the treatment of the head to the feet, so that what you seek and require concerning this may be made easier for you.
First chapter.
Of the treatment of water which is contained in the head of children.
Know that this illness often occurs in children after birth and when the nurse tightly holds the child's head without support.
And sometimes it arises from a hidden cause that is unknown.
However, I have only seen this illness in children; and in all those I saw, death resulted, and therefore I leave the treatment of it to others.
And I have seen a child whose head was filled with water, and its head was always thus enlarged until it could no longer support itself due to the size of its head, and that the moisture increased until death.
And this moisture is either accumulated between the scalp and the bone, or accumulated beneath the bone, on the skull.
When the operation is on this part, if the moisture is between the scalp and the bone and the abscess is small, then it is necessary to cut into the middle of the head with a fissure, and the length of the fissure should be about two inches until the moisture flows out.  This is the method for treating the swelling/abscess.
And therefore, if the moisture is more increased and the abscess is larger, then there are two fissures to be made in this manner:
And if the moisture is beneath the bone, a sign of this is that you see the seams of the skull opened on all sides, and the water is squeezed out when you press on it with your hand in those areas and it's not hidden from you. You must cut into the middle of the head with three fissures in this manner:
Then remove all the moisture from the incision.  Then compress the fissures with cloths and bandages.  Then apply wine and oil to the wound until the fifth day, then loosen the bandage and treat the wound with dressings and ointments, and support the head evenly; and have the patient eat dry foods with little moisture until the limb is strengthened and healed.
And there is another method of incision, considering where the size of the abscess and collection of water appear, for sometimes it is more in the back of the head, or in the front, or on the right, or on the left; and therefore, make the incision where the abscess and the fluid collection are most apparent, and do it in the manner that seems best to you.
And be careful not to cut the artery, so that blood does not flow and kill the patient through that blood flow with the evacuation of moisture.
Second Chapter.
When there is a persistent cold or inflammation in the eyes or the feet, and the usual treatments with medicine are not effective, then the final cure in this case is the incision of those two arteries.
And therefore, when you want to incise those (arteries) if the illness affects the head, first rub the area with a rough cloth so that the arteries are made visible, and then tie the patient's neck with a cloth; then, consider where the vein pulsates; and mark the locations of each of them near the ears, for they are hidden in some individuals.
And then, mark the artery with a probe, and then incise each one with a sharp instrument with an incision that reaches the bone, and this is done over the width of the head.
And if you wish, insert the instrument under the artery and cut it at the incisions on either side.
And the length of the incision should be about two fingers together, for when any vein is cut, the blood is released with a pulsating outflow, which spurts out with frequent spurts.
However, if the artery is not visible, then you must measure from the ear a distance of three fingers, then mark with a probe, and cut to the bone.
And the amount of blood that needs to be drawn is about a wine cup, and more or less will be drawn depending on the strength and filling of the patient's artery.
And then consider the wound, and if any cause of inflammation remains on the bone, then cut away that so that a festering abscess does not develop.
And then press the wound with lint and strips of cloth, and then treat it with ointments until it is healed.
Chapter 3.
Of the extraction of the arteries which are in the temples.
When you encounter a persistent headache or a sudden severe pain, due to acrid humors and the quality of the tissues of the temples, or a similar severe and persistent pain, and it is not treated by the usual methods of medicine, then in these conditions we have found effective the extraction of the arteries in the temples, or the cauterization of those, as we have shown.
And the method of the operation and extraction of those is that the patient shaves the hair in the temples; and identifies the artery appearing in the temple, because it is visible to you through its pulse, and is hidden from most people due to the severity of the cold.
And if it is hidden from you, then the patient's head should be firmly wrapped with a cloth; and then rub the area with a rough cloth or warm water to make the artery visible. And then you take the instrument, of which this is the shape: And then you carefully scrape the artery with that instrument until you reach the artery; and then you cut it in parts to remove it from the artery, and expose the membranes beneath it, from all sides.
But if the artery is delicate, then wrap it with the end of the lancet, and then cut it in a portion that is proportional to the lengthening and shortening of the extremities, and a flow of blood will not occur, for if it is not cut completely the blood will not all flow.
And then drain the blood by a quantity of one to three ounces.
And if the artery is large, then you should tie it in two places with a strong double thread of silk or with ropes, and do not loosen it before the wound is healed, otherwise there will be a flow of blood.
And then cut the excess that is between the two ligaments; and do this at that time or at another time.
And if you wish, cauterize that with a cautery, going down to the bone with a scalpel cautery, until its extremities are cut, and the same operation will then be done.
And it is better if the patient does not have a fever or is not of a hot temperament; for cautery is of those things that help to eliminate harmful moisture, and therefore it is better in a beneficial way.
And it is necessary after the extraction of the arteries, that the area be filled with old cotton, and powders made properly be placed on it, and afterwards be treated with drying medicines that make flesh grow and with certain dressings until it is healed.
And if during the operation, there is a bleeding from the artery, at that time you must try to stop the blood, either with cautery, or by filling the area with cotton, and pressing your hand until the bleeding is stopped. And if none of those things are available to you, then place your finger over the area until the blood is coagulated, and apply water of intense coldness to the area until the pain subsides, and it is tightened as needed.
And the easiest way in extracting an artery is that you cauterize the vein with this cautery, having two knives, since you have marked the two places with a probe, and placing each one, very hot, until they reach the bone and cut the vein in two places, so that what is between its extremities is stretched, because it is not completely consolidated by this cautery.
And this is the form of the cautery:
And the sharpness of the two knives is sufficient. However, if the sharpness is less than that of two knives, it is better, because if it is sharp like two knives, it is near to each other, and it does not cut the flesh quickly.
Therefore, since there is some thickness in those areas, the heat of the fire is held in those places and the flesh is cut easily.
And this method is best of all and the easiest.
And the space between the two knives should be the width of a finger.
Fourth Chapter.
Of the treatment of bodies suffering from acute and acrid humors of the oils.
When the humors are acrid, and the vessels on the top of the head are affected, and you find no medicinal cause for the cure, and sometimes the patient's face reddens and feels a crawling sensation on their forehead, as if ants were crawling, and the oils are weakened, dampened to the point that their skins are apparent, and the eyelids are inflamed by the acridity of the tears, the ancients, experienced in the treatment of such cases, used a specific technique for this treatment, and called that treatment "spassa".
And you command the patient to shave the hair on their forehead, and then make three equally spaced incisions on the forehead, following the length of the forehead. And if the length of the incision is two fingers, the first incision should be equidistant from the nose's length in the middle of the forehead, the second should be a little further along the movement of the muscles around the tear ducts, and the third on the other side.
And be careful not to cut the arteries which run along both sides, and keep your hand away from the connection of both jawbones. And the space between each incision should be about three fingers wide.
And if you have a prepared sponge or very dry cloths with which you can absorb the blood.
And then insert the spatula with two pointed ends, of which this is the shape of the incision: which is from the temples to the second incision. Scrape away with that all the flesh that is between the two incisions, using the chisel which is on the bone, and then do this from the middle incision to the second incision. And then remove this spatula and insert into the first incision again another instrument called a scalpel, sharp on one side and blunt on the other.
And place the sharp edge of that instrument against the flesh adjacent to the tissue, and the blunt edge against the bone, and push it in until it reaches the middle fissure, and sever with it all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes.
But continue the incision to that part of the tissue, and then do this in the middle fissure until the other fissure.
And when a quantity of blood flows, squeeze the places of the congealed blood clots.
And then place in each fissure a piece of old cloth, and place on those powders mixed with wine, oil, and vinegar, and make sure it does not become a hot abscess.
And on the third day, loosen the ligament and administer a large infusion of warm or lukewarm water, and then treat it with basil ointment with roasted oil and others until the wounds are healed.
Chapter 5.
Of the treatment of sores and ulcers descending from the parts within the head.
When a patient has sharp and persistent sores, and the ulcers on the patient's body are small and weakened, and their appearance is poor, and the eyelids are ulcerated and hair loss is occurring, and there is sharp pain and subsequent exhaustion on the scalp, then know through these symptoms that the sores and abscesses originate from numerous veins deep within the head.
And the best treatment for this is this: order the patient to lie down, and then make a small incision in the center of the forehead, from the left temple to the right temple (and begin with an incision from the left temple to the right temple; and make the incision down to the bone; and keep your hand away from the temporal lacerations which are commonly sore near the chewing muscles), until the bone is exposed.
Dry all the blood with a sponge or a dry cloth, and then fill the gap between the two edges of the incision with old cloth or linen bandages. And then apply a poultice made of powders mixed with wine and oil so that a painful abscess does not form.
And when you have removed this and see that the abscess is reduced, then it is necessary to clean the bone until it begins to grow flesh, and then treat it with a drying regimen that promotes flesh growth, just as if you take two parts wheat flour and four parts colophony, mix them, and from this create a plaster to be applied to these wounds to stimulate flesh regeneration.
Chapter 6.
Of the treatment of that which affects the ears.
Every ailment affecting the ears belongs to one of four types: either it is a mineral or something similar to a mineral, like iron or glass; or it is plant-based, like cedar or beans; or it is a flowing substance, like water or sour wine and their like; or it is some kind of animal.
And therefore, when a stone or other similar substance that does not belong nor grows in the ear enters it, and you place the ear in the sun, and the stone is loosened, then instill into it some kind of perfumed oil or balm, and then apply an extraction method, and make it sneeze with medicine, and plug its nostrils to prevent the expulsion of the sneeze. And then place some pieces of cloth or wool around the ear, and extend the ear outwards, for often it is forced out by these methods.
And if it is not forced out, then administer an extraction with the instrument called a *gesti epicicarios* [small spoon-like instrument for ear extraction], of which this is the shape:
And if it is forced out with the instrument, good; and if not, then administer an extraction with a small, slightly curved spoon.
And therefore, if it is not forced out by that, then make a copper tube and insert the end of the tube into the ear opening, and seal around the tube with softened wax and oil so that only the tube remains open. Then extract with your breath with strong suction, for often this is effective with what we have described.
But if it is not forced out in this manner, then take a heated, or pre-warmed, piece of cloth, a small amount, and place it on the end of the ear, then gently wrap it around that cloth. And then gently insert that into the ear opening, and then dry the ear of moisture.
And therefore, if it is not forced out by all the methods we have described, then you must consider cutting it out before it becomes a hot abscess or spasm.
And the way to cut it is that you first bleed the patient from the cephalic vein and extract blood according to its effect, and then place the cut between your hands, and turn the ear towards the upper parts, and make a small incision at the base of the ear towards its cartilage, in a well-pressed location. Let the incision be shaped like a crescent moon until it reaches the stone, and, once the stone is found, remove it if possible with appropriate instruments; then close the incision immediately, and treat it until it is healed.
And if the cause of the swelling in the ear is one of the growths that swells and becomes inflamed, then administer the cure and extraction of it; but if you do not comply with this, then take a thin, subtle substance in this manner: And administer with that the excision of that growth which has fallen into the ear.
And do not do this when you think that it has already been moistened by the ear's vapor; instead, put it into small pieces, and remove them with tweezers or with a subtle technique, or with a small incision, as we have mentioned; for this makes the removal easier.
However, if water has entered the ear, the patient should initially use sneezing, filling the ear with some old cotton, and lying down on the affected ear. If the water is thus expelled, it is good; otherwise, take small, smooth stones about the length of a finger and heat them slightly in the fire.  The patient should then insert one of these stones into the ear canal, and then tap the stone with another stone on the part of the head above the affected ear. Therefore, continue this with the stone until all the water is expelled.
Therefore, continue this with the stone until all the water is expelled. And when the water is thus expelled, take some paper or linen, place one end of it in the ear and hold the other end with a fire until the majority of it burns.
And then put in more linen and repeat this as many times as necessary until all the water is expelled, and remove it with a tube, as was described above for the stone extraction.
However, in the removal of a small animal (or similar) that has entered the ear, treat it as described in the section on smaller objects; but if it is a large object that appears to be in the ear canal, then extract it with instruments and forceps, as this is easier than other things that enter the ear.
However, in the removal of a worm that has grown in the ear, if you treat it as described in the section on smaller objects and in the treatise on distillation and your treatment does not improve it, then you should expose the ear to the sun's rays.
And thus, if any sign of worms appears, remove them with delicate instruments or forceps; but if no sign appears, then take a narrow-mouthed, wide-mouthed tube, of which this is the form: Insert the thin end of the tube into the ear according to how much the patient can tolerate; then, strongly suck with it, repeating this many times, until all the worms are expelled.
But if this does not work, then you should seal and close the end of the tube with wax, as previously described.  Then apply distillations that are known from ancient treatments for worms, and these can be found in the treatise on distillations.
And use the method of pouring medicinal oils into the ear using this instrument, of which this is the form: Make it of silver or brass, narrow at the bottom, with a small hole at the top, and flat on the surface.
And so it should be used to push what is in the cavity of the tube, wherever necessary. And if you wish, take a reed and wrap its end suitably with wool.  Then, pour oil into the cavity of the ear, or the juice of an herb, or whatever medicine you want, into the tube.  Place the reed covered with wool inside the ear, and press gently with your hand until the oil is forced into the cavity of the ear, and the patient feels the oil inside.
And whatever is poured into the ear should be slightly warmed by fire. And be careful that the substance you distill into the ear is not too cold, for ear medicines do not tolerate that.
Chapter 7.
Of the treatment of ear congestion.
Sometimes children inherit this from their mothers' wombs, and sometimes it occurs in adults,  a congestion in the ear canal caused by a plaque or a growth; and this congestion sometimes occurs at the opening of the ear canal, preventing the passage of fluid, and sometimes it's in the outer parts.
And that which occurs at the opening of the ear, which does not allow the passage of fluid, is in the most difficult situations to cure.
And in that which allows the passage of fluid, you should place the patient's ear towards the sun and observe it. And therefore, if congestion appears, open it with a very small instrument, of which this is the form:
If its extremity has a small width, with one part sharp and the other not; and the instrument has two thin ends so that it does not damage the ear.
And therefore, if the congestion is due to flesh growing in the ear or in its canal, then remove it with small hooks and cut it out with vigorous ease until all the flesh is removed.
And if the congestion is in the front of the ear, then take a thin, slender rod and warm it slightly with fire, and then place it in the ear canal.
And therefore, if you have detected congestion with which the patient finds difficulty in hearing, ease the pressure on it gently; and be careful not to damage the nerves of the ear.
And then place on the ear a cloth, fitting the width of the opening that you have wrapped in Egyptian ointment; and do this for some days until you are certain of the wound's healing; or that you take some and soak it in water and powder over it, thoroughly crushed, and use it; and if the ear seems to be already abscessed, then you should extract the pus and replace it with another pus-covered cloth wound in a cerate made from rose oil until the abscess is subdued; and then treat it until it is healed.
But if bleeding occurs, then soak a sponge or cloth in cool water and place it over the ear, and use another treatment that constricts the flow of blood, which is described in the treatise on distillation.
Chapter 8.
Concerning the treatment of the swelling which comes to the eyelids.
Swelling that comes to the eyelids of the eyes sometimes is moist, sometimes is dry.
Therefore, you must hold them with forceps or tweezers, and separate them from their roots with a spatula, and then apply a poultice to the affected areas.
But if pain with blood flow comes, then you cauterize them with a thin, lens-shaped cautery.
And the best cauterization is close to the incisions, and often times they return when some cause remains of their roots; and when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and do not return.
Chapter 9.
Treatment of swelling that comes to the eyelids.
Often, in the eyelids, a cause resembling swelling in its strength and hardness is present, and therefore it is called swelling.
And it is a collection of thick moisture in the eyelid above and below.
Therefore, the procedure for them is that you should consider if the swelling is originating in the area visible on the eyelid and is spread in all directions; and the cause of it is slight.
Therefore, make a cut across it (the swelling) with a scalpel, and then scrape it away from all sides easily until it subsides, and then suspend it (the eyelid) with a forceps and cut it away.
And if the swelling is large, then you should close it with stitches; and heal it until it is cured.
And if it is small, no harm will come to you because the ointment will restore and heal it.
And if the swelling is growing inwards from the eyelid's surface, then lift the eyelid, suspend the swelling with forceps, rather than making an incision, and press it from every side.  And if the eyelid is stretched by the incision, that is of no consequence.
And after the incision, wash the wound with salt water; and heal the area with something that promotes healing until it is cured.
Chapter 10.
Of the treatment of a cyst on the eyelid above the eye.
A cyst is a swelling that is generated in the eyelid's tissues, and it is more common in children, it aggravates the eyes of those afflicted and causes them difficulty in the morning; and they cannot look at the sunlight because of it, because tears accumulate around it.
And because of this, you see them lying on their faces constantly, and they sleep on their faces; and the eyelids below their eyebrows are wet, which has already become swollen, and the appearance of a cyst is imminent in the eye; and when you press the area with your fingers, you feel a cyst there.
And a method of operation is this: have the patient rest their head on your lap, and then place a cloth over them and heat it, and make a circle around the size that contains the whole cyst, and then place that over it and press with your fingers to make the cyst gather in the middle of the circle, then pierce the middle of that wetness with a small incision with a slit, and the slit should not be larger than the slit that is made in bloodletting.
But in the process, you must cut through the skin completely until you reach the cyst, because in most cases the cyst proceeds through a slit like a white grease.
Then remove it with cloths that you have wrapped around it with your fingers on the outside and twist it between your right and left hand until it is gone.
And be careful not to press too hard into the slit, so that with the incision you don't pierce the eye and damage it.
But if the cyst does not appear at the beginning of the incision, but you gently press into the slit until the cyst proceeds; then remove it as we have said; and then soak cloths in vinegar and warm water and place them over the area, and press them down with powders.
And some crush salt and place it in the cavity of the incision to absorb any remaining moisture.
And then treat it until it is healed.
But if there is a hot abscess in the area, then treat it with cooling poultices until it is healed.

Chapter 12.
Of lifting irritated skin with a needle when the skin is hard.

And if the hair is too short, then you take a long hair from the patient's natural hair, and lift it with that, or you let it grow until it is a suitable length, and then you lift it.
Chapter 13.
Of the treatment of an injury that occurs on the upper eyelid.
When an injury, a laceration, occurs in the eyelid, and it is a contraction of the eyelid, when it is contracted upward, either on the right or left side, due to poor healing from a wound, incision, or cauterization, and is called "leporine" (hare-lip shaped), and this laceration or injury is either natural or accidental, and is caused by poor healing from a wound, incision, or cauterization, or by similar poorly treated ailments.

And this operation, which we have described, is nothing other than a correction of a healed injury that does not restore the area to its original form.
Chapter 14.
