Often an ulcer forms at the extremities of the nose, and it grows and worsens over several days until the sight of it is most distressing.
And therefore, you should incise it at the beginning of its appearance, and remove all of it. And then apply a cautery or a burning medicine to the area.
However, if its incision is delayed until it has grown, then consider if it is hard, dark-coloured, and insensitive. If so, do not touch it with iron, for it is a hard, deep-seated abscess; because frequently, those who cut into this abscess suffer great harm from the resulting spread of disease.
And if the abscess is soft to the touch and not dark in colour, and if incision is possible throughout it, then drain the patient and cut it open without fear, and treat the area with things that dry and constrict until it is healed.
Chapter 26.
Concerning the suturing of the nose, lips, and ears when their continuity is broken by plague or similar afflictions.
Know that when you encounter a break in continuity in one of these cartilages, you will achieve little in that operation except in skilled hands.
Therefore, when encountering any of those causes, consider if it's a fresh plague wound with its own blood, and if so, then you should rejoin the two lips of the wound with stitches, and then treat it until healed.
And if the lips of the broken continuity are already separated, and each is made whole, then you must scrape each part of their apparent edges until blood comes out. Then rejoin the two lips with stitches, and tighten them.  Powder the stitches with dragon's blood and crushed emery.  Place a piece of diaphanous plaster, or any other consolidating plaster, over the powder.  Leave it bound for two or three days, then remove it and replace the plaster.  Leave it until the pus has cleared, then treat with ointment until healed.
However, the method of stitching is that you join the break either with needles as we have described in abdominal sutures, or join the break with glue, as you have been taught.
Chapter 27.
Of extracting those things that are found between the two lips in the inner parts.
Often, in the inner parts of men's lips, some hard abscesses appear, resembling the seeds of grains, and some of these are smaller, and some are larger.
Therefore, you must turn back the lips and cut away each of those hardnesses from every side, and then fill the wound with a crushed herb until the bleeding stops, and then wash the area with sour wine and treat the area with a styptic agent, until it heals.
Chapter 28.
Of the incision of the flesh adhering to the gums.
Often, flesh adhering to the gum, which the ancients called albulus, forms; in that case, you should hold it with tweezers or forceps and cut it at its base, allowing the pus or blood to run out.
And then apply crushed herbs or other styptic or drying powders to the area.
And if that flesh returns, then cut it again, and cauterize it, for with cauterization it will not return.
Chapter 29.
About removing teeth with iron.
When on the surface of the teeth, both inside and outside, and between the gums, rough, hard, and sometimes black and yellowish or greenish scales form, causing gum decay and making the teeth sore.
Therefore, you should have the patient sit between your hands, and place their head on your lap.  Clean the teeth where those scales and similar gritty substances appear, until no trace remains.  And do this to remove the blackness, yellowness, and other such things until they are cleaned.
If what is at the beginning of the procedure is removed, that is good; but if not, then repeat the procedure the following day, and again on the second and third days until you reach your desired outcome.
And know that teeth require many different kinds of tools and shapes, according to how you prepare for the operation. And therefore, the scraper used to scrape the inner portion of the tooth is different from the scraper used to scrape between the teeth in other ways, and these different scraper shapes you should prepare yourself, which you will see shortly.
Chapter 30.
Extraction of teeth.
You must treat the tooth's pain with every means and be thorough in every extraction, for there is no cause to hurry when extracting it, for it is a subtle substance within that must be dealt with during the extraction process.
However, when the patient requires a tooth to be extracted, you must be firmly certain which tooth is the painful one, for often the pain deceives the patient and suggests it is in a healthy tooth, and you extract that one, and the pain does not cease until the diseased and painful tooth is extracted, for we have already seen this through the experience of barbers.
Therefore, how should you be certain which tooth is painful, and that you know it well?  Then you should make incisions around the tooth with a substance possessing some virtue, until the tooth is detached from the gums on every side, and then gently remove it with your fingers, or with fine forceps, little by little, until you dislodge it.
And then, apply strong pliers for good grip or strong leverage, and place the patient's head between your knees to hold it firmly so it doesn't move, and then pull the tooth straight, in a way that doesn't break it, and doesn't cause the patient further pain.
And if it is not completely dislodged, then take one of those instruments and gently place it under it from every side, and exert the removal force in the same way as you did at first.
But if the tooth is cracked or decayed, then you should cover that crack with cloth and compress it with a thin, sharp instrument so that it is not fractured during your application of pliers.
And you must carefully make incisions around the gum on all sides, and keep a good grip to avoid breaking it and leaving a piece behind; and return to the patient, for fear of causing greater pain than the initial discomfort.
And be careful not to do what some ignorant barbers do in their rashness and audacity when removing teeth, without administering what we have described; for many times they cause great illnesses in men which are harder to treat than the original problem, and their roots remain, either all or some of them, or they pull out the tooth with some of the jawbone, as we have often witnessed.
And after the removal, rinse the mouth with warm wine or sharp wine with salt.
And if bleeding occurs, as often happens, then sprinkle some alum on the wound; and if that doesn't help, cauterize it.
The shape of the forceps and delicate pliers with which the tooth is initially grasped should be of long, slender jaws and a short, thick handle, so they do not bend when using them.
And they should be like that, with a thick handle, so that when you squeeze with them, the jaws don't bend; they should have short jaws.
And they should be made of Indian iron, with straight jaws; and on the jaws, there should be teeth that interlock and tighten with a strong constriction.  Sometimes, the jaws have the shape of a stork's beak (or similar pointed shape), and are very strongly constricting.

Chapter 32.
Of the treatment of teeth protruding above the others.
When teeth protrude beyond their natural alignment, causing a misshapen form, especially in women, and especially in young, venerable women, then you must consider: if a tooth is directly adjacent to another tooth, and separation or suture is not possible, then remove it; but if it is not adjacent to another tooth, then you should cut it using a tool of this shape, resembling a pig's snout, made of Indian iron with a very sharp tip.
And your incision should be made over several days, due to the tooth's hardness, so that it does not damage the other teeth.
And if the protruding tooth is exposed to filing, then file it using an Indian iron file, of which this is the shape:
And it should be entirely made of Indian iron, easily marked; and it should have very small points, like the files used for making needles, with which the tooth is filed little by little over many days with ease, so that no other tooth is damaged or broken. And then, finally, line it and remove its roots with small razors.
And if a part of the tooth is fractured and the tongue is injured, then it is necessary that you file it until the roughness of the fracture is removed and smoothed, and filled so that the tongue is not irritated, and the speech is not impaired.
Chapter 33.
Of restoring damaged teeth with gold or silver filings.
When teeth are damaged by impact and removal through blows or by a fall, and the patient cannot hold what is missing, and you have treated it with astringent medicines, and your treatment has not been successful, then, so that it does not decay, it is better to fill those that are broken with gold filings and not silver, because silver turns green and can become spoiled after a few days, but gold never changes.
And the filings should be so fine and thick according to the amount that can enter between the teeth; and the method of application is to take the filings and place double the amount between two healthy teeth, and then to join two twisted portions of the filings between the broken teeth, or if there is one or many, until the material reaches the sound tooth on the other side. And then repeat the process until the starting point, and gently and skillfully compress it until it is no longer broken.
And your pressure, with which you press the filings against the tooth roots, should be such that it does not escape.
And then you trim the excess filings with forceps or pliers, and place them in each place with precision, and mold them and hide them between the healthy tooth and the broken tooth, so as not to injure the tongue. And then leave it thus compressed and secure.
However, if it is loose or broken, compress it or them with other filings in such a way at all times.
And this is the method for teeth, and the manner of correcting two broken teeth and two healthy ones, as often happens in these situations:
And when one or two teeth are returned to their correct position, and they are regrown as we have described, and remain stable.
And only skilled and wise artisans can do this.
And sometimes a piece of cow bone is fashioned into a tooth shape, and placed in the gap where the tooth is missing, and is compressed as we have said, and remains in service for a long time.
Chapter 34.
Of the binding of the ligament which is in the tongue and prohibits speech.
This ligament, which is sometimes under the tongue, is either natural, present from birth, or accidental, resulting from a wound that has already healed.
And the operation on it is that you open the patient's mouth, their head resting on your own; you lift their tongue, and then cut that nervous ligament along its length until the tongue is freed from its constriction.
But if there is any hardness or knotting in that area, and this is from the healing of a wound, then you apply a cautery and cut through it along its length until you have severed the ligament and the knot has been broken.
And be careful not to create a fissure in the underlying flesh, and that you don't accidentally cut through a blood vessel and cause a flow of blood.
And then wash the site of the incision with water or sour wine or cold water; and then place a linen under the tongue; this is a medicament the patient should keep under their tongue every night so that it does not heal again.
But if you cause a flow of blood, place powdered vitriol on the area; but if the bleeding continues, then cauterize the area with a cautery appropriate for this purpose. And then treat it with the other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 35.
Of the extraction of a ranula (or cyst) under the tongue.
Sometimes, an abscess forms under the tongue, resembling a small tumor, hindering the tongue's natural function; and it grows significantly, filling the mouth.
And the procedure for it is to open the patient's mouth facing the sun and examine the abscess.
And if you see it to be dark in color, or black, hard, and the patient feels no sensation there, then do not touch, for that is a cancer.
And if it has turned whitish, indicating moisture, then you place a probe there and carefully cut it with a sharp scalpel, and divide it into sections.  If blood flows during your procedure, then you apply pressed, crushed zegi (likely referring to a type of medicinal material, possibly alum or a similar astringent), that is, vitriol, until the bleeding stops.
And then return to your work until you have completely removed it, and wash it with sharp wine and salt; and treat it with further remedies until it heals.
Chapter 36.
Of the extraction of abscesses from both ears and those that are on the neck, and of other abscesses.
When there is a boil on the throat, these are glands that are gathered together, which burst outwards and are called boils, the ones that when treated with what we have discussed earlier and do not heal, then consider if the abscess is hard, dark in color, and insensitive; then do not touch it with an iron.
And if it is reddish in color and the root is thick, then do not touch it with iron for the sake of blood flow; rather, let it mature, and then either you will lance it, or it will burst open on its own.
And if it is whitish, soft, and its root is subtle, then that is what needs to be incised.
And the operation on it is that you should consider, before your operation, whether the abscess has already been calmed by adequate rest or if it has diminished with some reduction; and then make the patient sit in front of the sun, and let their head be in your lap, and open their mouth, and the attendant holding their hands, press the patient's tongue downward with a very special instrument, the shape of which is:
And it should be made of silver or bronze, and be thin like a knife.
Therefore, as you press the tongue with it and the abscess is shown to you, and you have positioned your sight on it, then take a lancet and insert it into the swelling, and pull it outwards to the sides as much as possible. After that, if you pull out any part of the tissues, then cut it with an instrument that has this shape:
After that, if you pull out any part of the tissues, then cut it with an instrument that has this shape: and it is similar to forceps, but its ends are curved, so that the opening of each is towards the other, very sharp, which should be made of hardened iron.
However, if this instrument is not present, then cut it with a scalpel of which this is the shape: And it should be sharp on one side, and blunt on the other.
And after you have incised one wound, cut the other in the same manner of incision, and then the patient will recover from the cold water.
But if there is a flow of blood, then apply water decoction of pomegranate rind or myrtle leaves, or similar astringent remedies until the flow of blood is stopped, and then treat it until it is healed.
And sometimes other abscesses arise around the two ears, and cut those according to what I have described in the incision of two wounds as well.
And I once treated a woman with an abscess that had formed in her throat.  This abscess was dark-colored, with little sensitivity, and it had almost completely closed her throat.  She was breathing with difficulty and had been forbidden from eating or drinking water, and she was close to death if it had lasted another day or two.
And the abscess had grown so much that it had created two channels/tunnels until it emerged through two openings.
Therefore, I treated it fistularly (by creating a channel) and inserted a probe into one of the openings.  I extracted a considerable amount of [pus/matter].  Then I cut/incised the area where I understood the other opening was, and then I opened her mouth, pressed down her tongue, and inserted the probe into the abscess.
And then I excised a large portion of it, and only a little blood came from it. Therefore, the woman's throat was now clear, and she immediately began drinking water and soon ate food.  Thus, I continued to cut into that abscess many times over a long period, and another abscess formed in the same place, which I cut into until that one also healed.
So I cauterized the woman's throat, which was now healed; and after that I wandered about, not knowing what God had done to me.
Chapter 37.
Of the incision of a boil when it is abscessed.
When catarrh settles on the uvula, and it is abscessed and long, it is then called a perpendicular boil; and if it is in the lower part, thick and round, it is called a boil.
However, when it is treated with what we have said in the section, and you do not find your treatment effective, and sometimes a boil that is already gone is subtle, then you must incise the one that is round and inflamed; and if it is of that length, and it is sanguineous, of a dark colour or black, and there is no feeling there, then you must test the incision, for there is fear for the patient in that.
And when you see it according to the way I have described, by its whiteness and length, that you make the patient sit and press his tongue with an instrument that reveals its shape, and then put the lancet on the swelling, and pull it down and incise it with one of the two instruments mentioned in the incision of two ulcers.
And you should only incise what is adjusted to the natural boundary without adding more, for if you incise further, you will worsen the patient's condition.
And after the incision, use what we have described in the incision of two ulcers, and treat it until it is healed.
However, if the patient is frightened by the incision, you should use a cautery without fear, and the way to cauterize it is only with sharp water.  You should place the patient's head on your lap, then press his tongue with the instrument we mentioned, and then take the water, as described in the cauterization chapter, and apply it with a live coal. Make sure it is neither too thick nor too thin, and fill the cavity of the instrument that has this shape:
The end of the cavity where the medicine is placed should be like the cavity of a spoon, and place the instrument with the medicine on the ulcer.  The patient lies on their side so that any saliva or mucus from their mouth flows away from the medicine, to prevent anything from dripping down the patient's throat and irritating it.
Then place your hand with the medicine and press it against the uvula for half an hour until you see it blackened and calmed by the medicine's action.
And if you wish, take cotton and wrap it around the end of the spoon, and dip the cotton into the medicine.  Insert the spoon with the cotton in a tube until the cotton adheres to the tube. Do this repeatedly until you achieve the desired cauterization of the uvula. Then leave it to dry and fall off in about three or four days.
And if you need to repeat the medicine, repeat it.  Then, clean the area around the uvula with cotton soaked in broth, removing any remnants of the medicine. Wash with cool water, then apply an external ointment and internal remedies until healed.
And this type of treatment is safer than incision and less prone to fear.
Sometimes the uvula is cured with something more subtle than cautery or incision, as it is cured by fumigation in this manner: take calamint, yarrow, rue, rue, arabic scicados, chamomile, and similar herbs, and combine all, or some of them, in a pot, and simmer them in sour wine. The pot should be properly sealed, and a small hole should be made in the middle, over which a concave instrument of bronze is placed in this way; it should be made of silver or bronze.
And the tip should be inserted into the patient's mouth until the vapor rises to the uvula through the tube, until the uvula is considerably softened.
And then repeat this many times until it is dry.  Beware not to perform this treatment at the beginning of an abscess, for it would worsen the abscess, and you should not do this unless the abscess is already ripe/mature.
But if you do not have this instrument, then take a reed and place a shell of an egg at its end, so that the patient's mouth is covered, as the eggshell prevents the vapor from burning the mouth.
And this treatment is good for health.
Chapter 38.
Of the removal of thorns and that which adheres to the throat and other things.
Often thorns or fish bones or some other thing adhere to the throat.
Then you must remove whatever of those is apparent, which you observe, after pressing the tongue with an instrument in front of a light, so that you may discern what is in the throat.
And whatever is hidden and unknown in the throat, you must make the patient vomit before the food is digested in the stomach, because a thing adhering to the throat will be more easily expelled by vomiting; or have the patient swallow a piece of bread or lettuce root or a piece of dry bread; or have the patient take a piece of dry sea sponge, and tightly bind it with linen, and the patient will then swallow that, for when it reaches the place where the thorn is, it will be expelled. Or take an eel and drink it wrapped in linen, and it should be swallowed whole and quickly be drawn out; or take a leek and quickly insert it and quickly withdraw it.
But if it is not expelled by these methods we have mentioned, then use a lead instrument in this way:
And it should be a little larger than a fingernail, and at its tip there should be a cavity which you can easily insert into their throat.
And have the patient hold their head up high, and be careful not to touch their throat bone, so that it does not hurt them, and with that you will push the bone or thorn, or the doctor will do it with his hand.
But if the patient themselves can better, by their knowledge of the place of the related cause, push it down, and pull their hand with the instrument up, all according to the quantity that has been prepared until it is expelled.
Chapter 39.
Of the extraction of blood-sucking parasites attached to the throat.
When a leech is treated with what we have said in the division of treatment with medicines, and it does not work, then consider the leech on the patient's throat in the sun, after you have pressed the patient's tongue with the instrument we have described.
Therefore, if you see the leech, extract it with a small knife or with skillful, gentle hand gestures.
And if it is not possible for you to remove it with that, then take a concave cannula and insert it into the patient's throat, and then place a heated iron in the concavity of the cannula, and do this many times (until the leech is dead and detaches from the throat) and keep the patient away from water all day.
And then take a bowl full of cold water, and open the patient's mouth to it, and wash the mouth with it; and do not let the patient swallow anything and keep the water in place with their hand for an hour, because as soon as the leech feels the water, it will detach.
But if it is not dislodged by what we have described, then fumigate the patient with incense or asafoetida using the instrument we described in the fumigation of the uvula.
Do this, so that it falls.
And there is another method of fumigation for this: you take a bowl containing burning charcoal, and the bowl is covered with a cover in the middle of which there is a hole, and the tip of the instrument is placed in this hole; and then start the fumigation; and the patient should place their mouth on the end of the cannula, and close their mouth so that the fumigation does not escape until you know that the fumigation has reached the leech, for the leech will fall as soon as this occurs.
And if it does not fall, then you repeat the fumigation many times, and prevent the patient from drinking, and give them salty foods and herbs, and do not let them drink water, and with this regimen it is necessary that it be expelled.
And this is the form of the instrument with which a leech is treated from the throat when it is visible; and it is similar to forceps in some ways, except that it has some curvature in it. which is placed on the throat, and its tip is like a crane's beak, which is gloved, on which a file is applied, so that when grabbing something, it does not slip.
Chapter 40.
In which is the summary of the discourse on the appearance of abscesses and the incision of them.
Abscesses are many types, of many manners, according to their remembrance, each one in this book.
And they are diverse in their puncture and incision in two ways, of which one is through the type of abscess itself; and through what we take from the humors.
And the second way is concerning the locations on the body where they occur, for the abscess occurring in the head is different from the abscess occurring in the buttocks; and the abscess in a fleshy area is different from the abscess occurring in a joint; and for each of these there is a judgment for treatment.
And some abscesses should not be incised until the pus in them is mature or until they are fully formed.
Other abscesses should be lanced when they are inflamed and not yet fully formed, as with abscesses near joints.  For an abscess near a joint, its presence extends until what is in its surroundings can be dealt with, and the ligaments or nerves of that joint are often severely damaged, causing contraction of that limb.  Or the abscess is near a major part of the body; if you delay lancing it until it matures, it might spread to the major part. Or it is near the buttocks; in that case, lance it while still inflamed.  You should not lance an abscess that's inflamed but not fully formed, unless it is near the buttocks, lest the infection spread to other parts within the buttocks and create a fistula or an incurable condition. However, you must know the proper time for lancing an abscess, and this is when it is mature.
And you know it is mature when there is cessation of pain and fever, and a decrease in swelling and throbbing, and a clarity in the head of the abscess, and the other signs.
And therefore, the incision must be made in the pus-filled area beneath the abscess, and out of the swelling that comes from healing the prominence.
And the incision should always be made along the length of the flesh or body, if the abscesses are in the limbs or feet, and away from the tendons, nerves, and arteries; and, in short, in all such places where there is no reflection.  For in those places where there is reflection, use an incision suitable to that part of the body.
However, when the abscess is in a fleshy area, it is better to leave the incision until the digestion of the area, as we have said, is confirmed.  For if you incise it before this, you prolong the duration of the sickness and the foulness within, and it makes the edges of the wound hard and the depth worse.
However, some abscesses must be incised according to the breadth of the flesh or body, depending on the necessity, or as dictated by the affected limb.
And you must administer a small incision for small abscesses, and a large and wide incision, or many incisions, for large abscesses, according to the varying sizes of the abscesses.
And others are abscesses in which the cavity must be scooped out and incised when it is done properly, in order that it may drain, as happens with boils and buboes.
And some are incised with a three-angled cut; and some are incised in the shape of a myrtle leaf, like the abscesses on the joints.
And some are given a round or crescent-shaped incision, and similar ones.
But those which are not like that, such as superficial abscesses, you should pierce superficially, or simply cut flat.
And when the abscess is large and has collected much pus, you should pierce it and do not try to remove all the pus at once.  Having removed a portion, you should then close the abscess until the next day, and then remove a larger portion, and repeat this several times according to the degree until it is completely drained.
And especially if the patient is frail, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or an elderly, decrepit person, for the animal spirit is often weakened by the discharge of pus, and the patient often dies quickly, and you should be cautious with their treatment.
Observe this chapter with great care, and when making your incisions into these abscesses, clean the sores and then consider whether the abscess wound is small, or a flat incision; in this case, you apply liniments or old lint.  But if the abscess is large, and the incisions are numerous, then you must put a medicament into each opening until they join together.
And if, in the abscess, a portion of the skin has been separated or has been cut away around the area, then you should fill it with old lint or with cloth strips, as when you have pulled out splinters or thorns, without moisture; and compress it until the third day.  Then you should treat and heal it with appropriate ointments until it is cured.
But if a flow of blood occurs during your operation, then you should apply cold water and vinegar in which you have soaked a linen cloth, and place this over the bleeding area many times.
But if the bleeding persists, then you must apply the regimen and remedies, and powders, which are described in many places in this book and in different sections.
However, if it is wintertime, and the abscess is located near many nerves, then you should mix powders in warm wine and oil, and apply this to the affected area.
And if it is summer, and the areas are fleshy, then you should apply powders mixed with water and oil, or with wine and oil, and keep it cool until the third day, as we have said.  Then you should drain the abscess, clean it, and apply the appropriate remedies until it is healed.
And this is what is necessary for understanding the treatment of abscesses in general; however, I have already described how to treat each type of abscess in detail in separate chapters.
Chapter 41.
Of the incision of abscesses that arise on the neck of the head.
Small abscesses form on the neck of the head, and they are often like lumps that contain membranes surrounding them; and their contents are often thick.
And from them come pus, and this pus contains moisture similar to lime. And some of these contain moisture similar to dust, and others like them; and some of them are stony, hard.
And in the incision of all of them, and in the extraction of these there is no fear, as extracting them does not cause opposing archery.
And the procedure for the incision of these is that you probe them with an instrument whose shape is similar to this, which is called a probe, until you know what they contain.
If what it contains is moisture, then divide it with an incision along its length by a superficial incision in this manner:
And begin the incision along the line from B to G.  Then, when the moisture has been evacuated, you scrape off the skin that contained that moisture, and leave nothing of it, because it often returns if any part remains.
Then soak cotton in Egyptian ointment, if available, and otherwise in water and salt; and fill the wound with that and leave it until the next day, because with this you corrode any remaining skin particles.
And then apply more Egyptian ointment to the cotton a second and third time, if needed, until you are confident that no part of the skin remains; then treat the wound with ointment until it is healed.
And if the abscess contains greasy matter, then you incise it in this manner: with an incision shaped like a cross, and insert hooks between the two edges of the incision and scrape it from every side, and exert your strength in extracting the membrane containing it.
But if your efforts are unsuccessful, then do as I told you before.
And do the same for a calcified abscess in terms of incision and treatment as we have described; and incision on a calcified abscess is easier, for it has little blood and little moisture.
And sometimes there are some of these abscesses that are on the head of some men, with no moisture at all;  the reason for this is that I incised an abscess on the head of an elderly woman and found the abscess as hard, rough, white stone, which could not be broken, and if one were struck by it, it would hurt.
But in other abscesses on the head, this is not the case, as with abscesses that occur on the heads of children and near the roots of the ears, but the incision of all of these should be superficial.
And make the perforation in those from the lower part so that the body is relieved of the pus in the lower parts.
And then treat them with what is appropriate for their treatment.
Chapter 42.
Of the incision of scrofulas which occur on the neck.
Often these abscesses appear on the neck, armpits, and groin; and sometimes they occur in other parts of the body.

Chapter 52.
Of the treatment of a protrusion of the navel.
A protrusion of the navel is caused by many things; either by cracks in the fascia covering the abdomen, and then it is a section of tissue or intestine as in other ruptures; or by blood that flows from a vein or artery as previously mentioned, or by trapped wind in the area.
However, if it is due to a rupture in the fascia and a consequent protrusion of tissue, then the color of the abscess is similar to the color of the body; and it is painless, and often appears in various locations. And if it is due to the intestine's protrusion, then its location is significantly different from what we have described, and when you press it with your fingers it is hidden and then returns; and it is very light there, and it is greatly magnified near the opening of the bath and causes a great deal of struggle.
But if it is due to moisture, then it is painless and does not disappear when you press it with your hand, nor is it constricted, nor is it believed to be.
And if it is due to blood, then with these signs the abscess appears with a darkening.
But if it is due to raw flesh, then the abscess is serious, large, and hard, and it is fixed according to a certain quantity.
But if it is due to windiness, then touching it is painless.
And the treatment in such a case is to consider whether the swelling near the navel is due to blood from an artery or a vein, or windiness; then you should refrain from treating it, for there is fear and dread in that, just as you have been taught in the chapter where I discussed abscesses due to arteries and veins.
And if the swelling of the navel is due to an intestine or a hernia, then you should instruct the patient to hold their breath, and to remain standing; then probe around the entire navel with a probe, and then have the patient lean between your hands on their sides; then circumscribe the area around the navel with a wide spatula at the spot you marked with the probe; and then incise half of the abscess outwardly with a large incision; and then bind around it with strong thread or linen in a proper manner.
And then drain the center of the protruding abscess over the ligament, and insert your finger into that opening and look for the intestine; and if you have found it and secured it with the ligament, then you pull it out gently and push the intestine back into the abdominal cavity.
And if you have found the hernia, then take it with forceps; and cut away the excess tissue.
But if it is contrary to an artery or vein, then circumscribe that area, and then return to your initial treatment; and take two needles and put strong threads in them and insert the two needles into the circumference you have made around the abscess in a cross shape, pushing them in; and then press the abscess into four sections over the needles.
And if you wish, remove the needles and leave the area until the protruding flesh can fall off on its own; or incise it when it is softened and is ready to fall off, and then treat it with appropriate ointments until it is healed.
And if the protrusion at the navel is of flesh that has grown there or due to humidity, then it is necessary that you drain the abscess as we have said, and remove the blood or moisture that you find there, and then treat it with the appropriate remedies to consolidate the area.

Then separate the foreskin from the head of the penis with a thin linen cloth, which has already been soaked in cold water so that they do not stick together again.
And then it should be treated with astringent wine until it is healed.
Chapter 57.
Concerning the purification of children and the treatment of complications arising from circumcision.
Circumcision is not a simple break in the skin like other wounds, but as it is our operation and our will, and its use is primarily in children, it is necessary to devise the best and easiest way to ensure their well-being.
Therefore, I say that the ancients did not prescribe circumcision in any of their books, for they did not use it in their laws, and it is only through experience that we have come to know it.  The reason for this is that I have found most craftsmen and barbers using circumcision and purification with a scalpel and with forceps; and they use ligatures with thread and incisions with the fingernail.
And I have already tried all these methods and I find no better purification with forceps, with ligatures of thread, than with fingernail purification, for often the wound is more superficial, because the foreskin has two layers, and the lower layer remains, making further incision necessary and causing unnecessary pain. And with the purification performed by means of a rasp, a digital rasp, used by cobblers, the incision of the end of the male member is not secure, because it easily penetrates the opening.
But in the operation with a fingernail, it is very easy to damage the tissue and ruin your work. And a child's foreskin is naturally short, and many times it is born in such a manner that it doesn't need purification, and I have seen that.
However, the purification done with forceps and thread ligatures, its goodness, experience has shown me, because the forceps are for a proportionate incision, because the sharpness on top is similar to the sharpness below; and thus, when you press your hand, both sharpnesses are proportional, and it cuts in a consistent way in an instant; and a compression of the thread is made, encompassing the foreskin of the penis from all sides, with this there's no risk of error at all.
And the first method of the operation is that you should position the child, especially if it is one that understands a little, and you should only tie the thread to its penis and leave it until the next day.
And then do whatever you can to reassure him in every way possible, to accept the situation. And then, keep him still, holding him in your hands properly, and do not be careless; and hide the forceps in your hand or under your foot, so the child does not see them or any other instruments.
And then, place your hand on the penis and blow into the foreskin, pulling it back until the head of the penis is exposed, and then cleanse it of any dirt or buildup.
The shape of the forceps for cleaning, this is the shape:
One end should be blunt and the other sharp, so that the nail's root is not severed.  The length of the two cutting edges should be the same as the length of two handles combined.
But the error in cleaning is when you forcefully pull the entire, or part of, the foreskin away from the incision.
Therefore, you should extend it (the foreskin) with your finger before making the incision, and cut it evenly.
And if you cannot hold it with your finger, then pull it out with forceps and make the incision.
But if three days have passed and that which is inflamed beneath the penis remains after the abscess, then you let it be until the hot abscess subsides, and carefully peel off that area, then incise it as you have prepared.
And be careful of the head of the penis, for that will not benefit him.
And then treat that with what consolidates the wound from the powders that we have described in the treatise on powders.
And if the incision is made too deeply in the tendon, and you cut into the parts above it, that will not be beneficial to him.
And further treat it with what we have said until it is healed.

And the manner of drawing the urine with this is to take a doubled linen cloth and tie wool or cotton to one end for a good fastening; and insert the ends of the cloth into the lower part of the syringe, and cut with scissors if any excess wool is present, but insert it into the tube with force. And have the patient lie on their back, and lubricate their bladder and penis with moist oils, and olive oil and herbs.

And then pull out the cloth with the wool little by little, for the urine will moisten the wool.
Then remove it and use the urine for treatment.  Continue using the syringe until the bladder is emptied and the patient finds relief.

Chapter 60.
Of the extraction of stones.
We have already discussed in the division the types of stones and their treatment with medicines, and the difference between stones formed in the kidneys and stones formed in the bladder, and the signs of each.

And the stone formed in the bladder is properly that which is lodged within the bladder.
And I am narrating this with clear exposition and brevity.

And the cure is easier for children before they reach fourteen years of age; and the cure is difficult in the elderly. But in young people, it is easier because it is among them. (or "among their peers", depending on the nuance intended)
And of those with a larger stone, the cure is easier; and if it is small, it is the opposite.
Therefore, to reach a cure for this, it is necessary at the outset to cleanse the patient with a clyster which removes all the inflammation in their bowels; for this inflammation sometimes hinders the expulsion of the stone from the area surrounding it.
And then the patient should be raised with their feet, and be beaten and rubbed downwards so that the stone descends to the bladder, or jumps from its location, or sometimes.
Then make that incision between your hands, and place the patient's hands under their thighs, so that the whole bladder is made to slope towards the lower parts.
And then remove that and all of that outside.
Therefore, if you feel the stone in the space, then you should immediately cut into it.

And remove the stone until it is below your finger. And gently move it little by little into the neck of the bladder.
And then press on it with your finger, and expel it away from the place from which you extracted it.
And instruct the attendant to press on the bladder with his hand; and instruct another attendant to, with his right hand, pull the genitals upwards, and with his other hand hold the folds that are below the genitals, at the site of the operation.

Do not make it larger, for pressing your finger in the anus while the stone is near the incision, it will be forcefully expelled without difficulty.
And know that there is some portion of the stone to which the angles and edges adhere, which makes its removal difficult because of this.
And of that part which resembles a gland, and is round,  its removal is easy.
Therefore, if it is the part with the angles and edges that is wedged in the fissure.

But if you cannot act on it, then you widen the hole a little, and if you see blood flow, stop it by firmly applying crushed vitriol.
But if the stones are more than one, then at first you push your hand against the opening of the bladder, and then cut into it; and then push a little further in; and similarly do so if there are more than two.
And if the stone is very large, then it is foolish to cut it with a large incision, for you cause the patient one of two things: either death or a continuing dripping of urine, because the place is not completely healed.
But administer expulsion of it until it is expelled, or work on its fracture with forceps until you extract it in pieces.
So, when you have completed your operation, then fill the wound with aloe juice and dragon's blood, and compress it. And place over it a cloth soaked in oil and wine, or in oil and cool water, so that a hot abscess may be quelled.
And then lay the patient on their back, and do not loosen the bandage until the third day.
And when you have loosened that, anoint the area with water and a little oil, and then treat it with diafinicon ointment and diabazilicon ointment until it is healed.
But if in the wound a hot, burning and corrosive abscess arises, and it resembles that of blood frozen in the bladder, and urination is prevented by it (and this is recognized by blood exiting with the urine), then insert your finger into the wound and extract that blood, for if it remains, it will provoke putrefaction of the bladder and further corruption.
And then wash the wound with sour wine, water, and salt, and with any other suitable remedy, using whatever is appropriate for its healing until it is healed.

of triangular tip, sharp, fitted into a shaft.

And therefore, when you tie the linen bandage above, it is necessary for you to lift the stone upwards, or it will return to its previous position, and heal the wound as we have said.
Chapter 61.
Concerning the removal of stones in women.
Stones in women are rarely generated.

And the fourth is that the location of the incision over a stone in women is distant from the location of the stone, requiring a deep incision, and there is fear in that.
However, if necessity compels you to do this, then it is necessary that you take a knowledgeable female physician, and few are found.
But if you are deprived of such a person, then seek a skilled and subtle male physician, or present a matron well-versed in women's matters, or a woman who has considerable expertise in this art.

But if she is not a virgin, then command the matron to insert her finger into the woman's vagina and search for the stone, then have her place her left hand on the bladder and compress it gently.
Therefore, if it is found, then it is necessary to gradually pull it out of the bladder opening, downwards with the force of her strength until it reaches the root of the thigh.
And then make an incision over that, in the middle of the vagina towards the root of the thigh, wherever it is lodged, and extract the stone from that area, and the finger should not be removed from the stone while pressing down on it.
And the incision should be small at the beginning; then insert the finger into that incision, and if the stone is felt, then enlarge the incision according to the amount needed to remove the stone from that location.
And know that the types of stones are many: for some are small and large, and sharp and rough, and long and round, and having ridges.
Therefore, you know how many types there are of it, so that you may signify what you wish concerning it.

And then treat the wound.
Therefore, when the flow of blood has subsided after a few days, and the area is healed, then you return to your operation until the stone is expelled.
Chapter 62.
Of the incision for strangulated hernia.
An acute hernia is nothing but an accumulation of moisture in the white sac, which is below the scrotum that contains the testicles, and is called the sac.
And sometimes it is in the proper pouch that nature has prepared in the part of the testicle, until it is seen that there is another testicle; and the scrotum is between the white sac that we have mentioned, and this is nothing but a delay.
And the generation of this hernia is due to weakness that befalls the testicles, so that this matter is poured into them, and sometimes it is caused by a blow to the testicles.
And this moisture has many colors, for its color is either tending towards yellow or bloody, or red, or black, or foul, or watery white, and that is what is most common.
And the signs by which it is known where this accumulation of water is are these: for if it is in the white sac we mentioned, then the abscess is round, sloping slightly lengthwise like an egg, and the testicle appears, because the moisture surrounds it on all sides.
And if the moisture is in the proper pouch, then the abscess is round with a part of the testicle, and therefore one thinks it is another testicle.
And if the moisture is between the root of the testicles and the white sac, then it is felt below (i.e., deeper down).
And if you wish to know the color of the moisture, then you probe the color of the moisture in the abscess with a pointed instrument whose shape is known in advance; and according to what emerges in the trace of the instrument, judge what is inside it.
Therefore, when we come to treating it with iron, then it is necessary to instruct the patient to be bled, if possible, and to empty the patient's body.
And then lay him on his back on something slightly elevated, and place many cloths under him.
And then sit on the patient's left side, and instruct the assistant to stand on the right side, who should extend his rod to one or both sides of the root of the testicles and the area of the navel.
And then take a broad spatula and split the root of the testicles in the middle lengthwise, almost to the groin.
And the incision should be made along a straight line, equally distant from the line that you would cut the skin of the testicles, until it reaches the white fibrous tissue (scrotum) encompassing them.
And scrape that, and observe, and take care that you do not cut it; and your scraping tool, with which you scrape the area, should be suspended by the hook, and delicately examine the scraped area according to the quantity possible for you.
And then pierce the water-filled scrotum with a wide opening, and extract all the water.
And then separate the two edges of the incision with forceps, and spread the scrotum apart; and do not touch the root of the testicles, and cut the scrotum in that way that is possible for you. And cut it (the scrotum) either entirely or in pieces, and especially its thin layers, because if you do not meticulously scrutinize the incision, there is no guarantee that the water will not return.
However, if the testicle protrudes from its sheath during your procedure, then when you have completed the scrotum incision, return it inside, and then stitch the two edges of the root of the testicles together, and then treat it like other wounds until healed.
And if you find the testicle corrupted by another ailment, then you must tie the vessels that are on the supporting structure, and remove the testicle, and then treat it as we have said.
However, if the fluid is gathered in two distinct areas, understand that there are two hernias; therefore, make an incision in that area as you did in the other swelling; and if it seems to you to be a single operation, then you should do it as one.
And then cleanse the blood, and insert into the incisions wool soaked in oil or in rosined oil; and place outside other wool which you have soaked in wine and oil, and spread that over the two testicles and the area of the abdomen; and place over it folded cloths and powders, and tie them on top with a bandage that is in this form:
And then take two cloths and fill them with wadded wool according to this pattern; and have their extremities bound with cotton or damp wool, and they are attached with a tight bandage to the braguier of the breeches, and one extremity goes from the anus up to the groin, and the second goes to the area of the crotch, and two extremities over the thighs below and two others over the knees, and the entire bandage should be adjusted to the braguier of the breeches.
And sometimes this hernia is treated artificially with cautery instead of incision with an iron; and it is that you take a thin knife cautery and that you cut with it the skin of the genitals, and it is heated as we have recounted until the white pouch containing the fluid is exposed, and then you take a cautery in this form: And it resembles a Greek letter.

And then extend the pouch with hooks, and scrape it with hooks, and scrape it with a heated, thin cautery; and cut it as much as possible until you have eradicated everything; and be careful that the fire does not touch the egg.
And know that this operation is safer and further from blood flow, and better than an operation with an incision.
But if you encounter a suppurating abscess during the incision or cautery, or a blood flow, then it is necessary to treat whatever problem arises from this with the treatment that suits it, as said.
But if the patient is weak and does not endure the second operation because of its horror, then administer the piercing as I recommend; and it is when you are certain, from what we have said about the signs, that the hernia is acute.
Then it is necessary for you to have the patient sit on a raised surface, then pierce the abscess in the lowest part of it with a wide blade, and place the puncture along the length of the body.
And let the opening be wide until all the water runs out.
And then place a cloth over the area, bind it, and leave it, so that the remaining water drains out. And then treat that until the wound is healed.

Chapter 63.
Of the incision on a fleshy hernia and its treatment.
Know that in the incision of this hernia, there is a fear of causing harm in the majority of cases;  therefore, I have witnessed its release and subsequent recovery as a better outcome.

And a particularly bad abscess is a calcified one, whose colour is dark, and there is no feeling in it.

But that which is caused by inflammation of a vein does not disperse from it by any means when pressed with your fingers.

Of the treatment of a hernia (or rupture) that is of the groin.

Chapter 65.
Of the treatment of intestinal hernia.
The occurrence of this hernia is due to a section that extends onto the abdomen's surface from the groin area of the lower abdomen, causing the intestines from that rupture to slip into another part of the intestines.
And this rupture is either from the groin or from a stretching of the area, and these two types occur due to various causes: either a blow, or a jump, or a sudden effort, or the lifting of something heavy, or similar things.
The sign of this, when it is due to a stretching of the groin, is that it occurs gradually over a long period, and not suddenly; and it is a gradual swelling towards the groin because the groin compresses the intestine.
And the sign of it, when it is due to a tear in the groin, is that at the beginning it causes sudden, intense pain; and it is a diverse swelling visible below the groin area, and this is due to the protrusion of the intestine, and the body of the intestine to the parts outside the groin.
And sometimes, the intestine is accompanied by the membrane and is called this a 'circumscribed intestinal hernia'.

Chapter 67.
Of the dislocation that occurs in the ankles.
