And then tighten the white groin that is under the groin until it is uncovered.
And then take the reed and place it on the prominent area of the groin, and press it against the belly button.
And then sew the two prominent places on the reed/groin's extremity, and join one of them to the other with stitches.
And then remove the reed/groin extremity, and do not cut the groin entirely, nor extend the testicle, nor any other thing thus as I have made you aware in the healing of an intestinal hernia.
And then treat it with the treatment of ulcers, and as you cauterize the fils, cleanse it and restore the ulcer until it is consolidated; for the groin is contracted and the rupture's prominence has not been adjusted.
Cauterization is better in this location, for it is closer to health.
Chapter 68.
Of softening the swelling of the testicles.
Often the swelling of the testicles in some men is softened because the tissues around it are softened, and care is given to it.
Therefore, it is necessary for whoever is charged with the cure to lay the patient on their side, and to spread all the softened tissue over the natural tissue; then to stitch the two edges together with sutures.
And if you wish, sew the excess softened tissue at the beginning with three or four stitches, and with firm sutures.
And then you cut between the sutures; and then treat for the healing of other sores until it is healed and the pus has drained.
Chapter 69.
Of castration.
Know that castration is forbidden in our law, yet it is permitted to me to mention it in this book; and its mention is only necessary in two ways. One is so that, when a physician is questioned about it, he may know how to treat the affected person. The other is that we often need to castrate other animals for our benefit, like oxen, goats, pigs, and similar beasts.
Therefore, I say that castration is done in two ways: either by abrasion or by incision and section.
But in the method done by abrasion, the process is to immerse the beast in hot water until its genitals are softened, relaxed, and hanging. And then grasp them with your hand until they are resolved and no longer appear beneath the touch.
However, in castration performed by incision and section, you must hold the beast and press the scrotum with your left hand.
Then tie the suspensory ligament, and make an incision on each testicle until both testicles are separated.
And then cut them further, once each is skinned. And do not leave any cause on them except the subtle film that is over the vessels.
And this method of castration is better which is done by attrition, for strong attrition leaves no cause in the testicles for the beast to desire intercourse.
And then treat the wound until it is healed.
Chapter 70.
Of the treatment of hermaphroditism.
Hermaphroditism in men comes in two ways: one is when something appears in the space in the scrotum, between the testicles, shaped like a woman's vulva, in which there are hairs.
And sometimes from that area urine flows, which is in the scrotum.
But in women, it's one type: the vulva of the woman on the pubis is like two testicles of a man, a bit smaller and protruding outwardly, like the foreskin of a man, and two testicles.
And the cure is of three types: for two types in men and one type in women. And it is necessary that you cut the adhering flesh until the infection is gone, and then you treat it with the cure of other wounds.
And in the second type of men, in which urine is in the scrotum, there is no cure or operation for it.
Chapter 21.
Of the swelling and protruding flesh, due to causes affecting women.
A mild swelling is caused by natural reasons until it becomes compressed and slightly visible; and sometimes, it grows substantially in some women, spreading as widely as in men, and even impeding intercourse.
Then you should remove the excess swelling with your hand or with forceps.
And do not use incisions, especially near the root of the hair, lest you cause a flow of blood.
And then treat it with the same treatment as sores until it is healed.
But flesh born within is flesh that grows within the mother's womb until it fills it, and a mild swelling emerges at the edges, resembling a tail (and therefore some early writers called this "tailed disease").  So we must treat it as we treat swelling, and cure it until healed.
Chapter 72.
Of the treatment of a certain illness.
A certain illness is when the woman's vagina is not perforated, or the opening is small. And it is either natural or accidental.
Accidental is due to an illness that has preceded it, and that illness is caused either by poorly fitting flesh, or by a subtle or thick membrane.
And it is located either in the mother's womb, or in her ribs, or in the upper part of her body, or in the lower part, and prohibits coitus, impregnation, and childbirth, and strongly prohibits menstruation.
And the knowledge of this is determined by the examination of the midwife, if the blockage is apparent, immediately; but if it is not apparent, then you seek it with your fingers or with a probe.
But if the blockage is due to a thin membrane, and it is near the two lips, then you should cut and break it apart.
And you should place powders on the two lips, and then place your index fingers from both hands on top.
And the woman should lie on her back, her thighs extended, and then forcefully spread the two lips until that thin membrane is broken and the blockage is open.
And then take wool, soak it in oil, and place it on the area, and let the patient rest with a man by their side all day so that the incised area does not become solidified.
But if the membrane is thick and large, then you should cut it with a wide bladed knife or a myrtle leaf.
But if the blockage is due to flesh, then suspend that with hooks, and cut it.
And have with you the medicines that stop blood flow without causing bites, such as acacia, dragon's blood, and osier, mixed with egg white.
And then administer a wide lead cannula so that the wound does not heal too quickly.
And hold it for several days and administer lukewarm water.
And then let it be treated with another healing treatment until it is healed.
And sometimes the disease affects surrounding tissues, and then it is necessary to make incisions in this manner. But if this is due to a cranial abscess, no iron should be used against the cranial abscess in any way.
Chapter 73.
Concerning the treatment of hemorrhoids, and of tail and red swelling that occurs in the female's vagina.
Hemorrhoids are a collection of many veins, from which a continuous flow of blood occurs; and when these hemorrhoids have become old and are painful.
And I have already discussed the types of those and the symptoms of them, and will now state those that receive a cure and those that do not.
Therefore, I say that hemorrhoids and painful ones, when they are deep inside and do not appear on the surface, then in those no cure should be made with iron.
But those which are on the surface, where feeling is possible, are those which can be cured.
And you should place the woman in a cool place, then carefully cut away the inflamed area with scissors, or a rough cloth, and cut them away from the roots.
Then sprinkle over them a powder which stops bleeding, from among the incisive powders without biting, like acacia and similar ones.
And then you should soak her feet for an hour, and then place them in cold water.
And if the bleeding persists, then make her sit in a decoction of sumat [possibly a type of plant], and the bark of pomegranates and walnuts, and similar things.
Then take barley flour and mix it with honey and sour wine, and make a poultice with this and place it on the back of him/her.
And if the blood has stopped, it is good; and if not, apply two leeches, to the nipples without incision.
Then take wool and soak it in the juice of coriander or the juice of arnagloza or the juice of rue with diluted wine, and apply it to the area; and then treat the area with ointments until it is healed.
And treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 74.
Of the perforation of a hard swelling in the breast.
Many kinds of abscesses occur in the breast, such as cancer, stony abscess, ulcers, boils, and eruptions, fistulas, hemorrhoids, and sores, and other abscesses. And I have already described these diseases and their varieties, their symptoms, and their treatments in the division.
And it is necessary for me to discuss in this treatise the hot abscess which occurs in the breast when it collects pus, and if you wish to know how the operation with iron is performed.
Then you should consider if the pain of the abscess at its beginning is sharp with percussion, phlegm, and fever, and if it sometimes reddens, and if touching it creates a sensation, then do not proceed with a perforation, and treat it with what helps maturation until the sharpness of the symptoms ceases.
And then you should have the woman lie on a bed with two supports; and lean her back on them, and position her feet at the lowest part of the abdomen, and her thighs spread, and place her arms under her elbows, and bind each limb with suitable bandages.
And then the midwife, positioned on the right side, will administer the instrument with which the opening in the breast is made, the form of which will be evident in the actions to be taken thereafter.
And when you wish to administer this instrument, you should use its tip to insert it into the nipple of the woman's breast, and that part of the instrument which enters the breast should be no more than the nipple, and ease the patient's suffering. But if the instrument is larger than the nipple, then you should place powders on the texture of the nipple, so that the insertion of any instrument into the nipple is prevented.
And you should place the fluid (or, possibly, the lubricant) that is contained within the instrument on the upper side and have the person administering it hold the instrument, and rotate the fluid until the nipple opening is made.
And when the discharge appears, and is touched with the hand and is wet and sticky, then you should dry that area around the head with a linen cloth.
And when the discharge is evacuated, then you should place a cloth soaked in rubbed or green oil, in which there is some astringency, on the wound, and place the compress on the section near the nipple.
And place externally over the nipple and surrounding area a clean wool soaked in water in which mallows have been boiled.
And approximately two days later, apply the appropriate ointment until it is healed.
And sometimes the nipple and the wound are washed with water and honey, and then with water in which lily root or long-leafed aristolochia has been boiled, and then return to using the ointment.
And if the wound is deep inside the nipple, then you must refrain from treating it with iron, instead treating it with what we described in the section.
Chapter 75.
To teach the midwives how to treat living embryos when they are not developing naturally.
It is necessary for the midwife to understand the natural process of childbirth from its signs.  When you see the woman straining downwards and desiring fresh air, and when she is relieved by the pressure of the birth, and hastens the delivery of the embryo, then know that this birth is in accordance with nature.  The delivery of the child will be on its head, and the afterbirth (placenta) will be suspended or hanging from the navel.
Therefore, when you see these signs, the woman's abdomen should be compressed so that the embryo descends easily.  For when it descends on its head, it leaves behind the placenta, which is cleaned of excess material through proper cleansing.
However, when the birth is different from this, it is unnatural and undesirable.  For when the embryo emerges on its feet, its body emerging before its head and feet, or one hand or foot emerging, or its head and hand, or its belly protruding, and its head turned sharply away, or its posture different from these natural shapes, then it is considered unnatural.
Therefore, it is necessary for the midwife to be knowledgeable and astute in all these matters, and to guard against error and deception.
And I am showing all the forms of these things, and the ingenuity within them so that they can be understood and observed.
When the fetus emerges on its head through natural expulsion, and the process is difficult for the woman, and the woman's strength is weakened, then perform this treatment:  Let the women hold it, and evaporate its membranes with collected fenugreek and moist oils.
Then the midwife should gently insert her fingers into the small gap and fissure, and with that, detach the afterbirth, or scrape away the moisture within it. And press the woman's abdomen until the fetus descends.
But if it does not descend, then it is necessary to treat the woman with a poultice of ptilium and a poultice of fenugreek with cumin.
Then command the patient, have them cough, and induce them to sneeze with incantations; hold their mouth and nose for an hour, for the fetus will be expelled soon.
And if the two hands of the fetus are expelled at the beginning, then it is necessary that you return them gently, little by little. But if you do not succeed in bringing it back inside, then place the woman on a stool and raise her feet up, and then lower the stool to the ground; and the woman must be held so that she does not fall during the commotion.
And if the hands are not returned inside and the fetus dies, then cut them open and remove the remains of the fetus or tie its body to the woman's hand with a cloth and remove it equally, for that will be expelled.
When the fetus is on its feet, then you must push them upward, and then turn the fetus gently, and then swallow it, and then take one of its feet and extend it gently.
But when it has emerged into the thighs, then you command it to strain, and make it sneeze with a medicine, for it will be expelled if God wills it.
And if it is not expelled by what we have recounted, then you gently return the fetus to its natural position, for then it will be expelled easily.
And if it is still not expelled by all that we have described, then take marshmallow and dissolved gum, pound them well in a mortar, and then apply this mixture to the vaginal opening, to the lower part of her abdomen. And then apply a compress in lukewarm water, until the hypochondria (the lower ribs) are relieved.
And when the parts below him are calmed, then give him a fumigation of myrtle and apply it to him. And when the fumigation has been applied for an hour, then place him on a pillow, and make him cough and restrain his mouth, and remove the fumigation and gently compress the lower part of his abdomen, for the fetus will soon be expelled.
The fetus emerges lengthwise, hanging from one of its hands. Be ingenious in reducing the hand, but if you cannot reduce it, then elevate the woman and make her walk.
And if walking is not possible, then have her lie down on the bed, and forcefully compress her thighs, and then administer compresses which I have recommended of mucilaginous substances.
And if labor has begun, then have her sit on a stool, and then study the reduction of the hand, and draw the fetus in a natural way, and make the woman sneeze and command her to strain until it is expelled.
The fetus emerges on the mother's abdomen, its hands and face are on the other side of its mother. The midwife should hold the child's hand and gently and gradually turn it, facilitating its movement.
And when it is drawn out, then apply to its head what we have recommended of oil and mucilaginous substances.
And then command her to move slightly, and when you have done this, have her sit on a stool and command her to lean to her right side, and then command her to strain; and make her sneeze with the appropriate conditions. The prepared fetus is placed on its side.
When the mother is delivered of the afterbirth in this manner, consider, and then you cut the placenta with your fingernail until the moisture is expelled.
And if the fetus is suspended on its face and neck, then you cut it at the umbilical cord, so that it is not retained and dies.
And then push the fetus into the parts within until you place it as is proper, and tell the woman to strain herself several times, and it will be expelled easily, if God wills it.
Delivery of twins or multiple fetuses. Know that twins are born many times, and sometimes three or four, but this is rare and soon dies; but some are born contrary to nature, and do not live.
And count them, whether they are multiple, a second one, and separate them by cutting the ligaments at the umbilical cord of each.  And sometimes they are expelled as easily as a single fetus, in a natural way, and sometimes in an unnatural way, as we have described.
And if some of them are difficult during delivery, and reach the point we've discussed concerning unnatural forms, then treat them as we have described.
And if the midwife is skillful and does this with ease and subtlety, and with patience, and is clever enough to adapt to each unique situation to bring about recovery.
A recollection of that which is formed in the womb from embryos and those that are aborted.
Sometimes one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, or even ten are formed in the mother's womb.  I have verified that one woman miscarried six, and another ten, all fully formed.  This was determined by the measurement of the learned sages.
And all this must be within the midwife's knowledge, for it is easy to misinterpret such subtle appearances, and therefore one must proceed with caution.
Chapter 76.
Of the extraction of the fetus when it is dead.
When you treat the dead fetus using the methods we have discussed in the division of medicine, and it has not emerged naturally, and you are forced to use manual extraction, then you must consider if the woman is of healthy constitution and not suffering from any of the conditions that might cause her to be in danger.
Then the woman must be positioned on her back, with her head inclined downwards, and her thighs elevated.
And the attendant should hold her (the woman) on both sides or bind her to the bed so that her body does not move during the extraction of the fetus.
And then you should moisten the mother's mouth with soothing oils and with mucilage of marshmallow, fenugreek, and linseed.
Then the midwife should anoint her hand with those oils and mucilages, and then gently insert her hand into the mother, and then search with her hand for the location where she can hook her fingers onto the fetus.
And suitable locations for this: then consider if the fetus is positioned so that its head is presenting, and then you hook your fingers into its neck, or the head, or the mouth, or the palate, or under the chin, or in its armpits, or in the appropriate areas of the ribs, or under the flanks (hypocondria).
But if the presentation is on its feet, then in the groin, or in the middle of the ribs.
And it is necessary that the fingers of the right hand grasp the curve of the fetus in the area between the fingers of the left hand, and gently insert the hand and place the fingers in one of those locations we mentioned, until it reaches the empty space (meaning the birth canal).
And then place another finger, or a third if necessary, in contact with it, so that there is a pulling action, and not an extension according to its natural state alone.
And be moved in every direction, so that its exit is eased, as is done when a tooth is removed.
And it is necessary for the part between them that the extension be softened; and if any part of it is retained, then it is necessary for the midwife to anoint some of her fingers with oil and insert those fingers and gently manipulate what is retained.
Therefore, if the part of the fetus is expelled, then the fingers should be moved to a higher location; and do this until the entire fetus is expelled.
But if the hand emerges before the other, and it is not possible to return it by applying pressure, then it is necessary that a cloth be wrapped around it; and then pull it out until it is entirely expelled, and cut it either at the wrist joint or at the elbow.
And thus it is necessary that you do the same for the other hand and the two feet.
But if the head of the fetus is large, or if pressure is applied to it during delivery, or if water is collected around the head, then it is necessary to insert fingers between the bony parts, and break the head open and remove the water, or to pierce it with an instrument called an "alundat" (the form of which will be found with the forms of other instruments in the chapter near this one).
And similarly, do this if the fetus has a large head naturally; and then it is necessary to pierce it with an algematu, or to take it with an alunda as we have said; and then extract the bones with forceps.
And if the head has emerged and is pressed against the pubic bone, then it should be incised until the moisture contained within the feet is expelled.
But if it is not complicated, nor resists delivery, then you incise the head as soon as possible.
And if the lower part of the abdomen is swollen or hydrops, then you must pierce it until the moisture within it drains out.
But if the fetus is positioned on its feet, then the traction on it should be gentle, and the direction towards the mother's mouth should be gentle.
But if it is pressed against the abdomen and feet, then you must extract it with a cloth wrapped around your hand, and you must cut the abdomen and feet until what is inside them flows out.
But if the other limbs are out and the head is turned, then the midwife should insert her left hand; but if the mother's mouth is open, then she should insert her hand into the air space, and search for the head with it, and pull it with the fingers toward the mother's mouth, and then insert a single or two fingers, and pull it with those.
And if the mother's mouth is blocked by a hot abscess that has occurred on it, then it should not be disturbed.  Instead, a poultice of fatty, moist substances should be applied. And apply poultices; and keep the patient in waters that soften and moisturize.
And if the fetus is on its side, then if possible, it should be turned; and what we have described for a living fetus should be administered.
And if that is not possible, then it should be cut into pieces and extracted.
And it is necessary to not leave any cause within the afterbirth.
And when you have completed all your operation, then administer what we have said about the treatment of abscesses that occur in the mother.
And if bleeding occurs, then place it in styptic waters and administer the other treatment.
And I have already seen a woman in whose womb a fetus was dead, and soon after that fire another time pregnant, and soon after that fire a dead fetus was again formed. A long time ago, an abscess occurred on his navel; and it swelled until it was opened, and began to discharge pus.
And I was called to treat him, and medicated him for a long time, and the wound was not healed.
Then I applied some strongly-attracting plasters to him, and one bone came out, and after a few days, another bone came out; and I was very surprised, since there are no bones inside the belly.
And then I concluded that they were bones of the dead fetus.
Then I treated the wound, and removed many bones from him, the woman being in her usual condition, and she lived in that manner for a long time, recovering from the pus.
And I did not consider this strange, for in this there is knowledge and help for what the skilled physician administers with their hands to heal.
Of the forms of instruments necessary to extract the fetus.
Form of vertigo with which the mother is opened:
This is the form of constriction in which the books are bound, with two twists at the two ends of two strings. However, this twist should be finer than the constricting one, and should be made either of silk or of wood.
And the width of each string should be two fingers and the margin of each about one finger, and the length of each is one and a half palms; and in the middle place of the two strings are two joins of the same string, which are already fastened, of which the length is half a palm and a little more, and the width of each is about two fingers, or a little more.
And these two additions are inserted into the mother's mouth so that it may be opened with these when you rotate the two prescribed twists.
Another form similar to this, but finer and lighter:
Let it be made of silk or wood, resembling forceps. However, at its extremity are two additions, just like before, and the length of these additions is about one palm, and the width of each is two fingers.
And when you wish to open the mother with these, then you make the woman lie on the bed with her feet hanging down, opening the area between her thighs. And then open your hand with the instrument, just as you would with forceps, according to the amount you want to open the opening of the mother until the midwife does what is desired.
The form of vertigo which the ancients described:
A form of pushing by which the fetus is pushed:
A method of assistance by which the large head is contained.
And it is similar to forceps, with teeth at the end, as is sometimes the case.  And sometimes it is made long, like forceps, in this form,  similar to what happens, with teeth like saw teeth with which it is incised and contained. A method of assistance that is less involved/less difficult.
A method of grasping using a spine/spike.
A method of grasping using two spines/spikes.
A form of spatula with two ends, with which the head is severed/cut off.
Form of two wide spatulas.
All those instruments are used, and the more the kinds of those instruments are multiplied and prepared by the craftsmen, the lighter their operation is and the more valuable to men for their quantity.
And do not neglect those instruments, wherever they are or may be available to you, for the necessity of those is not excusable.
Of the extraction of the afterbirth/placenta.
When the afterbirth is retained after childbirth, then you should tell the woman to hold her breath, and then make her sneeze with a stimulant, and firmly press your hand over her mouth and nostrils, and if by this it is expelled, it is good.
And if not, then you take a bowl, pierce a hole in its cover, and place in it herbs opening the mother's mouth, such as wormwood, rue, fennel, chamomile, arabic gum, cassia wood, and holy thistle. All these herbs, or some of them, should be immersed in water in the bowl, and place that over the fire.
And then place a reed tube in the mother's mouth, and hold it in place until the vapor reaches the mother's cavity.
And then make her sneeze as we have said, and the afterbirth will be expelled quickly.
But if this treatment remains ineffective and it hasn't been expelled, then you order the midwife to immerse her left hand in sesame oil or in marshmallow mixture, and then insert it into the vagina and search for the afterbirth with it.  And when she has found it, then take it and carefully pull it out slowly.  But if it's attached to the uterine wall, then insert her hand as we described, and when she has found the afterbirth, pull it out slowly, little by little, so that the mother does not suffer from excessive pulling. Beforehand, it is necessary that it be moved easily to the right and left sides.
And if the mother's mouth is constricted, we have already described the treatment with sneezing and herbal remedies.
But if it is not expelled by all the methods we have described, then be careful not to continue pulling at it, but rather what has emerged from her mouth onto the woman's lap, and then dress it with an ointment and a four-herb medicine; for that will cause it to rot within a few days and dissolve, and it will be expelled.
But when it rots, there is a bad odor rising from the head and stomach, and this afflicts the patient; and then fumigations and appropriate remedies must be administered.
But some of the ancients already used fumigations of juniper and dried figs.
Method of the instrument with which the woman is fumigated for the retention of menses and afterbirth:
It should be made of glass or earthenware.
And the slender end should be placed in the vessel, the wide end over the fire, and the fumigation should be placed over the embers of the fire.
And hold it until the fumigation is consumed and repeat another.
For the treatment of an unperforated infant's anus.
Many infants are born with their anuses not perforated, which are then very narrow and obstructed. Then the midwife should perforate that narrowness with her finger; and if not, perforate it with a sharp instrument, and be careful not to touch the sphincter muscle.
And then place upon it wool soaked in wine and oil; and then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
But if you fear that it is obstructed, then place a tube in the opening for many days, and remove it when the infant wishes to suckle.
And sometimes anal obstruction occurs due to the consolidation of a plague or abscess; then that consolidation must be incised, and then treated with what we have mentioned regarding healing and lead.
Of the treatment of fistulas that develop afterward.
The fistulas that develop in the lower part, pus-filled and swollen, which develop near the anus externally or in the space of that part.
And it is a fistula or abscesses.
And when that swelling is old and comes from watery white moisture or subtle decay, and sometimes these fistulas are penetrating.
And those penetrating ones are known by the fact that things come out of them through discharge and by windiness; and sometimes the patient experiences discharge, and worms are frequently expelled from them.
And sometimes those fistulas penetrate into the bladder, and into the urethra; and some of those penetrating ones go into the joint of the thigh and into the bone marrow of the thigh.
And how a penetrating fistula is known from a non-penetrating one in the rectum is that you place your index finger into the rectum, and gently insert a thin probe of bronze or iron into the fistula, when there is no twisting.
But if there is twisting in that place, then insert a thin probe of lead or horsehair until you feel the probe or hair on your finger.
And if you do not feel any of that, nor does any discharge, worm, or windiness proceed from the opening, as we have said, then know that it is non-penetrating, and then work towards a cure.
And when the fistula is penetrating into the bladder or into the urethra, then the sign of that is incontinence of urine and the prohibition of it until the area is consolidated with medicine.
But if it is penetrating into the joint of the thigh or into the bone, and pain in the surrounding area, then the sign of that is that the probe goes in straight if there is no twisting into the bone, and pain in the surrounding area; and that it will always be painful; and that no medication will work there, nor will it be healed with ointment.
And in all those penetrating fistulas, there is no complete cure, and the cure for those is the work and vanity of the experienced physicians, on which one must rely.
But in that which is not penetrating and not old, healing with iron is expected, as I have told you, and this I have already tested/tried.
And it is that the patient lie between your hands on their back, raise their legs up, their thighs bent towards their belly, and then insert a lead probe, or a ram's horn if there is twisting in the fistula, until you know the place where the probe goes straight.
And if that touches the patient around the anus, then you must insert your index finger into the anus.
And if you feel the probe itself penetrating through your finger, or something else between your finger and that tissue or flesh, then know for certain that it is penetrating; and then do not work on it, for there is no cure for it, as we have said.
But nevertheless, some might say that it is cured in some men slowly.
And the treatment through which improvement is expected in him is that you heat a thin cautery according to the width of the fistula, as previously mentioned, and insert that heated cautery into the fistula until it reaches around the anus; and then repeat this two or three times until you know that all those flesh tissues that are connected and fistula-shaped, like a bird's feather quill, are burned.
And then treat it with some bandages until all the burned flesh has healed, and then treat it with medicinal ointments that cause the flesh to grow and heal, and if it is healed, it is good; and if not, then it is not curable at any time by any other treatment.
And if the probe enters, but does not penetrate your finger inside the anus, and between that and the probe is a thick layer of flesh or tissue, and the fistula extends into that area which touches the surface of the skin, then cut the skin at the beginning of the fistula, and you cut it with the probe, and keep it inside the fistula until the cut reaches the point where the probe's tip arrives; and through the probe it is freed and extracted.
And then cleanse those fistula-shaped tissues which are connected like a bird's feather quill, and leave no cause of them behind.
But if blood flow comes between you and your operation when you are burning those tissues, then the best and easiest instrument is to cauterize with fire or with a sharp medicine, because cauterization with fire has two good habits: it burns those attached tissues, and it removes blood and excess moisture.
And then treat it with bandages or strips of cloth until the wound heals, and then treat the wound with ointments that cause hard flesh to grow and heal.
And these are the medicines that involve astringency and excision; when this is done, it is already healed and you need not fear recurrence.
But if the fistula has already pierced the anus and is extended from the body's surface, then insert your finger into the anus and examine it with that.
And therefore, if you feel a resistance and there is a layer of hard tissue or flesh between you and it, and it is close to the penetration, then there is no operation on it but rather a way of trust and hope.
This condition can be treated in one of three ways: either cauterize it as we have said; or excise it until you reach its cavity, so that it is possible for you to insert remedies and proper treatment; and do not proceed much with excision, so that you do not cut the muscle containing the rectum, and that it happens to the patient in the way of a spontaneous expulsion; and then treat it with what we have said, for it will be easily healed; or extend the membrane and clean the pus that you cut close to the anus, as we have said, either with a probe or another instrument with a sharp tip, and then clean those fistulous flesh which are in the incision, until you can close it with a scab; and then strive to complete the healing of the entire wound by excision; and if the opening above is not healed, and the fistula remains open in the area of the anus, it will be easier for the patient. And sometimes the fistula is finished in this way: when you insert the probe into the fistula, and it is in the fold of the anus around the body's surface with its edge and at the margin of the anus, then use a probe with a pointed tip, which is like a needle for preparing the skin in this manner:
and insert into that a probe wrapped with five threads or around those; and then insert a probe with a thread into the fistula until it reaches the cavity.
But if it is penetrating into the margin of the anus close to the inside, then you remove the thread from that hole in the same way you put your finger into the anus; and you pull out the thread's end and bring the two ends together, tie each one, and leave it for a day or two.  And when you cut the thread into the flesh, hold it tightly until the flesh between the two ends of the thread is cut and falls away.
And then treat the wound until it is healed and sound.
However, if the opening of the fistula is not penetrating, then make it penetrate, in whatever manner you have prepared, but not too deeply, because it is not your job to do so lest you injure the muscle; and then treat it until it is healed.
The shape of the probe with which the fistula is probed: one curve is very sharp, and the other part is not sharp, so as not to cut what is unnecessary, as sometimes happens.
Of the suturing of hemorrhoids in which blood flows into the incision of those.
Hemorrhoids are of two kinds, for either they are between the buttocks, similar to bunches of grapes, or they are like the grapes of a bunch. And of those there are small and large, and whether the blood flows from them constantly or is one, or many, and they are outside the anus, and at its extremities.
However, those outside the anus are according to the measure of little humidity, from which flows yellow water or a little blood with a continuous consistency, and they are according to the color of the matter.
And the treatment for those inside the anus is that you order the patient to sit down and to press until the anus opens and the hemorrhoids appear before you. Then you grasp and suspend them with tweezers or hold them with your fingernail, and then cut them near their roots.
But if the tweezers are not holding them due to their moisture and softening, then take them with a rough cloth or cotton or wool pad, and pull them out with your fingers. Then cut them and sprinkle some warm powders onto their incision so that they stay in place instead of using a cautery; or cauterize them as is described in the chapter on cauterization. And then treat them with the other treatments until they are healed.
And if the anus does not open, then prick the patient with a prickle containing a small bite, and wash it with whatever is in the anus to gently open it when the patient is pressed.
But if the hemorrhoids are outside the anus, then the cause is slight, and you should grasp them with your fingernail or tweezers, cut them, and then treat them with what we have said until they are healed.
And for the incision you've made, you should apply a suture like this: take a thread wrapped around it and insert it into a needle. Then, pull the hemorrhoids apart and use the needle to pierce the base of the opposite side, wrapping the two ends of the thread around the area below the needle, in a transverse manner.  Tightly compress the hemorrhoid, then knot the thread and remove the needle.
Do this to all of them, and leave one unsewn so that the excess blood can drain from that one.
And then place a cloth soaked in warmed oil or a plaster over the anus, and tell the patient to rest. And then leave it until it falls off; and when it has fallen off, treat it with ointments or another treatment until it is healed.
And if they are old and the previous medicine is not working, then you should scrape them with the tip of a spatula or with your fingernail until they are soft and the skin pus above them is removed, which prevents healing; and then treat them until they are healed.
Then you scrape them with a more forceful scraping than the first until much blood comes and they are scraped; and then treat them until they are healed.
Know that "alamermar" (likely a specific type of skin condition), that is, a key point, is a reddish round spot, like a nail head, that appears throughout the body, especially under the feet and on the fingers, and it often signifies inflammation.
And then it is necessary to cut what is around the spot, and it should be held with forceps or tweezers and pulled out by its root; and then treat that.
And if you wish, cauterize it according to what is described earlier in its chapter.
However, a wart is a small, rough, fistulous, round elevation on the body's surface, and often appears on the hands of children.
And its treatment is to cut or scrape it with a knife or with a file until it closes. And if you wish, cauterize this, as we have said, with fire or with a sharp medicine.
However, a pimple is a small, scabbed or pus-filled, large elevation on the surface of the body, often found on the groin, and when the cold encounters it, a sensation similar to an ant bite is felt, and it appears around the body.
And the treatment is to take a hollow tube made from an eagle's or hawk's feather, a strong one, and place it over the pimple until it surrounds the pimple's circumference with the tube; then, turn your hand with the tube until you have scraped what's within the pimple's circle, and place it in the groin of the flesh, so that the pimple is eradicated from its root.
And if you wish, do this with a tube of bronze or iron, following this method:
The part above the tube should be thin, closed (that is, not perforated), wrapped so that the grip on it is smoothed and its rotation facilitated.
And if you wish, cauterize the pimple according to what has been previously described in the chapter on cauterization of boils.
Of the forms of instruments that are used to give treatment.
Sometimes treatment is done with silver or brass or bronze melted or hammered; and sometimes it is done with those small and large instruments, depending on those to which they are applied, for that which is used in the treatment of small children should be small; and of those whose legs are swollen or painful, the treatments for those should be small.
The form of large and small treatment:
The head of the cannula above it, to which the bag is tied, should be wide in this manner; and there should be a clear distinction where the bag is tied, just as it is usually done.
And the lower end of it, which is inserted into the buttocks, should be closed (that is, not open) very tightly, approaching thinness; and on one side of it there should be two holes, and on the other side one hole, and the size of the holes should be according to the thickness of the rays, and the largest one slightly smaller.
And the bag, in which the bladder medicines or sheepskin are placed, should be one palm in width.
And it should be perforated with many holes, just as it is folded; and if the space between the holes is the thickness of a finger.
Then insert into those holes strong, hot blood, which should be from ten bloods, or about that; and attach the bag with that.  And when the bag is placed on him, tie it tightly at the head of the bandage between two distinctions, with strong constriction, and then bandage each hole with that.  However, the quality of bandaging with that is that you fill the bag with medicine, which should be warmed to the patient's tolerance; and be careful that it is not too hot nor too cold.
Then the patient should lie on their back, raise their legs up, and place a cloth underneath them, such as linen or a sheet; and then anoint the end of the cautery with oil or egg white or fenugreek mucilage or similar; and then insert it into the buttock with ease, and then the attendant takes the bag and squeezes it with both hands and forcefully until everything within the bandage is expelled into the bowel; and then remove the bandage, and the patient lowers their legs to the wall.
And if the medicine causes such expulsion, keep it in place for as long as possible; and if he sleeps with it for a whole night, it is more beneficial in recovery.
But if you use the bandage for ailments of loose bowels and to prevent expulsion, then you repeat the cautery until it is healed.
And if there are hemorrhoids or abscesses in the buttocks, then the bandage should be very thin and delicate, like the bandages used for children.
Concerning the treatment of plagues.
I have already said in the division of the treatment of plagues what was appropriate to the book, and I say thus the treatment of them with hands and with medicines according to the requirement.
Therefore, I say that plagues are diverse depending on how the plagues are caused and on the location where the plagues appear.
And the causes why plagues are often caused are like a stone thrown, or a cut with a blade or knife, or a blow with a spear, or a stick, or an arrow, or similar to those from many causes.
But the plagues that are in the parts of the body are like a plague that falls on the head, or the neck, or the feet, or the belly, or the liver, and similar to those of the limbs.
And I am recounting the treatment of some plagues so that you can apply that reasoning and rule to other plagues.
And I will begin with the simple plagues of the head, for the remembrance of the compound ones will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
Therefore, I say that when a simple plague occurs on the head, and it is not a fracture, then consider: if it is from a thrown stone or similar, and the skull has only been slightly fractured, and it is a large wound and you fear the onset of a festering boil in the patient, then you should quickly bleed the cephalic vein, and do not delay this; and draw from it the amount of blood appropriate to the patient's strength, unless some reason prevents bloodletting, especially if the blood that has come from the wound is meager.
And let the bloodletting be on the opposite side of the afflicted part; and guard the patient from repletion and from rich foods and drinks.
But if any cause prohibits bloodletting, then reduce the patient's food intake and do not allow them to eat meat or drink strong wine.
And if a festering boil occurs on the wound, apply a poultice of roasted olive oil or wine that has been made astringent.
But if a festering boil is certain, then apply to the wound, if it is recent, its own blood, and the air hasn't changed the powder I will shortly describe.
And if the air has already changed that (i.e., if it's no longer recent), then apply various ointments until it is healed.
But if the wound is large, or is from a cut or similar injury, and its edges are not properly aligned by themselves, then draw those edges together with stitches according to my description of suturing them.
However, if the wound has already exposed the bone and tendons, and it's not a significant injury but a slight separation, then cut it open and subsequently treat the wound with medicines, and bring the flesh back together in the manner of a splint, and order the patient to consume foods that are rich in substance, such as heads of cabbage, legumes, and the like, until it is healed.
And if you find putrefaction in the splint and it's not adhering to the bone, then you cut away all the putrefied material and discard it, and subsequently treat the area.
However, if in these wounds there is an artery or vein from which blood may flow, and it has not been severed by medicine, then you must look for the artery.  And if you find it uncut, then you cut it with a scalpel, or tie it off; and if the situation demands that the methods previously described are not sufficient, then you cauterize it until the blood flow is stopped.
And if these wounds are simple and small, then the cause of them is slight; therefore, you should sprinkle this powder on them, for it will solidify them before the air alters them.
And the method for the powder is this: take one part of olive oil, two parts of dragon's blood, and three parts of unquenched lime; all must be finely ground and sifted.  Then fill the wound with it and bind it tightly until it adheres well to the area and is coagulated with the blood.
And then you bind it on top with bandages and leave it, and do not loosen it until it has become suppurated, because the medicine is not eradicated from there until it is healed.
And sometimes this alone is enough, when you don't have frankincense and dragon's blood readily available.
And you perform this same operation on large wounds when it is appropriate to apply it and bind them, especially on recent wounds with their own blood, which the air has not yet altered.
And if the air has altered them, especially in summer, then this powder is not suitable for application. Instead, treat them as we have described,  or put on some digestive ointments or dust on the wounds flour from barley mixed with water and honey until it forms a paste.  Then treat them with other methods until they are healed.
But if a small bone fracture occurs in the wound, then you remove that with care.
And I will describe the treatment of head fractures in what follows.
Of wounds of the neck.
But if a wound occurs on the neck, there is no difference in treatment between a simple wound of the neck and a simple wound of the head.
However, if the nerve or artery of the neck is cut, then if it's a nerve, there is no remedy, but rather avoid treatment with anything that causes strong astringency, such as zinc or verdigris (copper sulfate) and similar substances, for they damage the nerves and worsen the situation. And do not apply any cold substance to the wound, for the substance of the nerves is cold and their continuation is with the brain, which is the highest of all the members.
And if this is treated with mild remedies, such as washing it with fresh water, sometimes infused in olive oil (either processed or unprocessed), and turpentine when washed with fresh water, and other similar softening stones, and moist ointments.
But if the wound is large, then administer sutures, or stitch up its edges, and then treat it with powders until it heals.
However, if the wound is deep or has reached the underlying hard tissue, then drill through it at the lowest point.
But if an artery is already cut in the wound and blood is flowing, then either separate or tie off that artery, or cauterize it if necessary.
But if the vertebrae of the neck, or the entire neck, have been severed and the veins of the neck are saved, then you should join the edges of the neck wound with sutures along the spinal column, and do not touch that; first straighten it and return it to its natural position, and then bind it with appropriate bandages, and leave it for several days; then treat it with ointments that are astringent and stimulating until it is healed.
But if the wound has already affected the bone significantly, then you must treat it, and if there are bone fragments there, remove all those you can; and what you cannot remove, leave until the wound has become suppurative, and the separation and discharge have lessened.
And if the wound is recent, then sprinkle powder, as instructed, on it. But if a fistula has formed in the wound, then treat it with what is mentioned in the relevant section of this chapter.
If the wound is from a spear or knife, and you assess its depth, then consider further if the wound is creating windiness as the patient breathes; then know that the wound is fatal.
And if the wound is not deep and is fresh, with its own blood, then you place powder on the wound in the first hour, but do not press it tightly so that the blood is not retained in its depth and returns to the heart and kills the patient, but instead place an attractive ointment there, and apply it twice a day.
And if the ointment is not available, then you place old wool in the wound opening to absorb the moisture; and have the patient sleep on the wound so that anything there that is stuck to it may be released.
But if three days or more have passed since the injury, and the patient shows no spasms, tremors, or bad heart condition, or constriction of the airways, and the other bodily functions are good, then know that the wound is salvageable; and then treat it with poultices and other remedies until it is healed.
But if healing is impossible, and the wound remains open, then know that a fistula has already formed; treat it therefore according to its specific treatment.
And if the wound is from a cut with a knife or a sword, and is superficial on the foot or back, then treat it with sutures if it is large, or with powder if it is small.
But if the wound has already affected the bone, and pieces of it have already been cut out, then you should clean the wound and try to remove those pieces if they are loose. But if they are not loose, then leave them until the wound can heal, as removing them would be painful.
And for other wounds that may occur on other limbs, the treatment is similar to what we have already described.
Of wounds of the abdomen.
The ailment which occurs in the abdomen is either severe, mild, or moderate.
A small rupture is more difficult to repair in another way, and if the reduction of the affected part isn't done quickly, it becomes more difficult because the opening enlarges and the return of the organ becomes difficult.
Therefore, a moderate rupture is the best, because in that case the reduction of the bowel is not difficult, unlike in the two aforementioned types.
And know that abdominal suturing is done in four ways, but two of these suturing methods are common and appropriate for wounds/suturing in other parts of the body.
Two other suturing methods are unique/appropriate for other injuries, but are particularly appropriate in abdominal suturing.
And I will explain each one to you with explanation and clarification.
Therefore, I say that when the wound is small and some part of the bowel has protruded through it, and it is difficult to reduce, then this can be in one of two ways: either due to the small size of the rupture, as we have said, or when the bowel has become inflamed due to the coldness of the air.
And therefore, when this is the case, you should warm it by submerging a sponge or damp cloth in lukewarm water alone, or in water infused with vinegar, cyperus, and spikenard, and use this to soothe the bowel until the inflammation is resolved.
And sometimes, using this in a way that has astringency is better than plain water for resolving inflammation.
And when you see the inflammation resolved, then you should moisten the bowel with water in which elderflowers and mallows have been boiled, for this will ease its condition with little difficulty.
But if the condition of the protrusion is not eased by this treatment, then you should cut the rupture slightly with an instrument, such as used for hemorrhoids.
And the initial treatment is better when it is successful without needing a cut, and there should be no attempt to cut when it is unnecessary and potentially harmful.
Of the instrument, take note of its shape:
One part of it is sharply curved, and the other part is not sharp, and the tip is slender, but not as slender as a spatula, rather slightly blunt.
And if the protrusion is large and the intestine is outside, then its return must be according to its natural shape, to its proper place, if possible, for that is better.
But when the rupture is wide and is in the lower part of the abdomen, then you must have the patient lie on their back, and raise their legs higher than their head; and if it is in the upper part of the abdomen, then raise their head and feet higher than the lower parts of their body.
And similarly, if the protrusion is in one of the two parts of the abdomen, then constantly direct your attention and consideration to this, so that the part where the protrusion is is higher than the other.
And so you must administer this in large and moderate protrusions.
And in small protrusions, place what you have prepared, and then have the assistant place their hands underneath, the one holding the protrusion completely with their hand and joining the edges, and then determine if any other cause is needed to aid in the suture.
And this is the common method of suture: take a needle, or several needles, according to the size of the protrusion; then place the tip of the needle at the edge of the protrusion on the ring finger, and thread the needle, making sure you don't place the thread on both ends of the cord, using the two ends of the thread from under the cord of the interior parts, until you make that part penetrate; you have now joined the two ends of the cord and the two ends of the thread, and created four stitches.
And then tighten the double thread around the needle, sometimes from two sides at once, and then close the two edges and the protrusion in a neat closure; then leave the width of the finger and insert another needle, and then tie it with thread as with the first needle; and do not cease to do this if necessary with the needles until you have completed the entire closure of the protrusion.
And the needles should be of medium thickness, neither too fine nor too thick, for very thin needles easily pierce the flesh; and thick needles are difficult to insert into the cord, therefore they must be of medium fineness and thickness.
And the needle should not be inserted too close to both ends of the cord, so that the flesh is not easily pierced, and the wound breaks open before its healing.
And the sutures should not be too long, so that the wound is not hindered from healing.
And then cut the ends of the needles so that they do not disturb the patient during their sleep; and you shall place on the suture powdered substances spread out on all sides, which will hold the ends of the needles; and leave them until you know that the wound has healed.
And this type of suture with needles is most suitable for small wounds, for one or two needles (or thereabouts) suffice in their suture.
But the second suture is common: and it is that you close the four extremities of the cord and two extremities of the  slips (or strap) at once with a needle that is of faithful wrapping (or carefully wrapped), whether it is thin or thick.
And then, when you have pierced these four extremities with the needle, withdraw the needle from the same point from which you began, so that the thread retracts from the most superior part of the wound, so that the suture is like a cloth suture, with which the cloths are tightened.
And place between each suture the thickness of the little finger.
And this suture is known (or, and this suture is well known/used by men).
And with such a suture, I closed the wound that had befallen a man in his belly, which had been wounded with a knife, and the rupture of the wound was greater than a palm, and the intestines had protruded about two palms from the middle part, and the rupture was in the middle of the member; and then I reduced that, after the intestines had been outside the wound for twenty-four hours.
And the wound was consolidated in the space of fifteen days or thereabouts, and I healed him and he is healthy; and after that, he lived for many years, and he is remembered for his accomplishments.
And the other doctors had judged that he would not be healed; and by a miracle it was, for I did not treat it with ointment, for it was in a place where ointment was not found nor any medicine, but I placed old cloth over the wound twice a day and bathed it constantly with honey water until he was healed.
But I will tell you two kinds of sutures, proper according to the narration of Paraula.
And it is that you begin with a suture of the cord, and insert the needle from the outside to the inside.
And thus, as the needle penetrates the cord and in that same laceration, following its direction and length throughout the body, and leaves the end of the thread where you inserted the needle; and push the needle from the inside to the outside of the other end of the thread.
And when you have made that penetration, then you make that penetration again in that same end of the thread, from the outside to the inside; and leave the end of the thread that is in this loop, and push the needle into the other end of the thread from the inside to the outside.
And push with that your penetration, with which you make that penetration into the thread at the end of the thread which is on his side until all of it penetrates.
And then, starting from this same side, stitch it with the end of the thread on the other side, and remove the needle from the cord which it penetrated, and then return it to that cord and stitch the end of the thread which is on the other side with this end of the thread; and remove that from the cord which is on his side.
And do this once and again, and do this once more until you have sewn everything on the outside according to that example.
And the sum of this method of suturing is that you sew with a suture of hides, with which one sews hides, in such a way that you stitch the thread once from the end of the cord, and then leave the other end until you have completed it.
However, the second kind of suture, which you correctly call Gallic, and this is a description of it and its use, is that you sew it according to a similar method as some healers sew it, in such a way that they join every part to its corresponding part on him, as is natural; and they apply the one end of the thread and the end of the thread to the other end of him.  And this is done as I have described to you.
You must insert the needle into the end of the thread close to the outer part, and make it penetrate into the inner part of the wound, and leave the two ends of the thread; then turn the needle and make it penetrate from outside to inside through the two ends of the thread.
And then turn each of those, and make each penetrate from the inside part to the outside part in two other ends of the thread, on the opposite side.
And this method of suture is better than the usual, simple suture, and it is the suture of which we have previously spoken.
This is the Gallic method.
And some might say that the suture of the belly is rectified with two needles; this is to say that you insert a thread into each of them, and begin with the insertion of the needle from your side, and make it penetrate to the other side, according to the suture of those who know how to sew the skin equally.
And know that when the rupture is in the middle of the belly, then the suture of it is more difficult than in other places of the belly.
But the judgment of the treatment of plague is the judgment of the other plagues.
Which means that when you catch the plague fresh, with its blood before the air alters it, and return the intestines and sew it up properly, then place the incarnative powder on it.
And if the air has already altered the plague, then place upon it some ointments which are present with you until they form a hard stone and the threads fall out, and they are consolidated completely and watched; and then cure it thus as is the cure of other plagues until it is healed.
But if the medicines are not present with you, then place it on it immediately, as soon as it begins to shed pus, old matter, and change it twice a day, as you have been taught, until it is healed, for you do not need other medicine in many situations when the plagues are simple.
But if you fear that the plague is spreading to the main limbs, then you should soak linen in oil of a tempered quality, or in rose oil, and place it around the areas between the armpits and under the arms.
And if pain or putrefaction is felt in his intestines, for this often happens, then cauterize it with black astringent wine, especially if the pustule has broken through the intestine and created a penetrating exit into its cavity.
And know that that which is in the large intestine is of easier healing than that which is in the small intestines.

But if the plague's result is a membrane, and you find it recent, reduce it according to the method by which you reduce the intestines similarly.
But if it has spread to the surrounding space and is already green or black, then you should apply a strong pressure with a cloth on the location that is blackened by it, so that blood flow will result, for there are veins and arteries in the membrane.
And then, cut through the material next to that ligament, and place the two ends of the cloth hanging below the affected area outside it, so that the pulling away of the affected area from the binding of the membrane and release of the plague's extension is relieved.
A reminder of the plagues when they affect the intestine.
When a rupture occurs in the intestine and it is small, it is possible in some cases for it to be restored.  Because I saw one who had been afflicted in their abdomen with a lance thrust, and the plague was on the right side of the stomach, and the plague was old and had formed a fistula, and expelled gas and flatulence.
Then I considered the healing of him, though I was not confident in his recovery, but I focused on subtly treating it until it was healed and the area was consolidated.

And some experienced practitioners have said that when a rupture occurs in the intestines, and it is small, it should be treated in this manner: You should take ants with large heads; and then bring together the two edges of the rupture, and place an ant with its mouth open on the two edges of the rupture; then when it bites those edges and closes its mouth, you should cut off its head, because it adheres and does not dissolve.
And then place another ant next to the first, and do not cease doing this with ants, one next to another, according to the size of the entire rupture.
And then you compress that and close the rupture; and those heads remain attached to the intestine until the intestine is healed; and no harm comes to the patient.

And this method of suturing with ants is only according to a method of trust and hope.
But if the rupture is large and wide, especially if it is in one of the delicate intestines, because then there is only subtlety, and not a complete healing of it.
Of the treatment of ulcers and fistulas.
Know that any wound or abscess, when it becomes old and chronic, and forms an ulcer, and is not healed and discharges pus continuously and is not surgically removed, then, wherever in the body it may be, it is universally called a fistula and we call it an ulcer.
And a fistula, in truth, is a hard, white, fibrous tumor, without pain, and with a cavity similar to the cavity of a bird's nest; and for this reason, it is called a 'knotted ulcer,' that is, a nest.
And in some people it is moist, which discharges pus; and very easily the moisture is removed in a few hours. And sometimes this moisture is thick, and sometimes thin, and sometimes large, and sometimes subtle.
I have already shown all those causes, and their signs, and their cure with medicines in the division.
And it is necessary that I make you aware of the cure for a fistula, which is performed by means of surgery with the hands.
And know that fistulas occur in all parts of the body; for some are fistulas that penetrate the large veins and arteries, or the nerves, or the spinal cord, or the intestines, or the bladder, or to one of the ribs, or to one of the vertebrae of the spine, or to any of the joints composed in this way, like the hand and foot; and those that are near the main limb and similar ones.
And therefore, those fistulas that are of this type are among the difficult-to-heal illnesses, and they do not receive a cure, and to deal with them is laborious and ignorant.
And if any of the fistulas is not deep enough to reach those places I have mentioned, and you hope and trust in its cure, and you want to learn about it, then take a probe of bronze or iron, and if the fistula is in a straight line, investigate it with it.
But if the fistula is winding, then investigate it with a probe of fine lead, for lead, with the lightness of its body, is suited and bends towards the winding path.
And if the fistula has many horrors, it is not possible for you to examine it with a probe.
Then irrigate the openings with its openings, for the moisture you irrigate with goes to the other openings and runs through them.
And then carefully inquire, in whatever way possible for you, if there are bones or nerves present, and if the fistula's cavity is distant or near, or if it is a fistula with many openings, and consider all of this with the full force of your knowledge, your questioning, with which you question the patient, and the location of the pain in relation to your pressing your hand on the area, and the signs and causes similar to an abscess.
And when you have considered all of those things with careful judgment, then proceed to the cure according to your understanding: and consider whether the fistula is an obvious, nearby fistula, or in a salty place, far from a joint, or a nerve, or an artery, or a vein, or one of the places that I have told you about.
Then you should dry the fistula as previously described, and remove what is in that filtration and decaying flesh, and what is growing in the opening of the flesh, or pus and similar things, and treat it until it is healed.
However, if the fistula is of a deep, hidden nature, as determined, then it must be incised as deeply as possible for you, and then cleanse it of all its corrupt flesh; and then apply medicated dressings, and insert them into the cavity of the fistula, which you have cut open with an iron.
And do this repeatedly until the sharp medicine has consumed everything that remains in the fistula's cavity from the corruption.
And then restore it with ointments that generate healthy flesh until it is healed.
But if it is not healed by this, then you should cauterize it as we have described.
But if the cause of the fistula is bone, and you are certain of this, then you should explore and dry it as we have described, if you do not find any cause of a vein, nerve, or major limb in the way, as we have said.
But if the bone is exposed and there is any corruption or blackness on it, then you scrape it until all the corruption is removed; and then treat it with something that generates flesh until it is healed.
And if it is not healed and continues to break out as it did before, then know that you have not achieved complete eradication of the corruption; and then you must uncover it again thoroughly and carefully scrape and cleanse it with the full strength of your tools, and then restore it.
And so, if it is healed, good; but if not, then know that that corruption is in the distant depths of the body, which you have not reached, and therefore, there is no remedy for you but to leave it.
And if the corrupted bone is small, or it is possible for you to pull it out, then you pull it out with delicate forceps suitable for this purpose.
And if there are many bones, then carefully extract them all, and leave no trace of them with your strength.
But if any cause of moisture presents a difficulty, and there is no fear or mistake in their incision, then incise those ligaments or flesh, if present, and free the bones.
But if you are not prepared to remove the bones immediately, then place over the wound whatever can cover that wound, and leave it until it is approximately surrounded by those bones, flesh and ligaments, and watch that the wound does not become consolidated, and the incision you made in your operation is constricted.
However, if you fear, place on it cotton soaked in crushed oil, or cotton soaked in Egyptian ointment, or in any of the green ointments, for when those ligaments soften, and the bones are separated from them, the removal, and their extraction, is eased.
But if there is a large bone, such as a leg bone or thigh bone, or similar to those, and only the surface is corrupted, then scrape that with a rasp just behind until that blackness and corruption are removed, and then restore the wound.
But if the part of the bone that is corrupted is large, and the corruption has already reached the bone marrow, then it is necessary to close that and to cut it all the way to the point where the corruption has reached, and then treat it until it is consolidated.
And I tell you what happened to a man in his thigh, so that you may use this as an example and help in your operation.
There was a young man, about 30 years old, who suffered pain in his thigh.  And the material (or malady) spread from within the body until it reached the thigh, and it was a matter of an abscess with a large abscess.  It was not an external cause.
And then the time was prolonged by the doctors' error, until the abscess opened, and many materials flowed out, and its healing was delayed until the leg became fistula-like, and many openings formed in it that all expelled pus and bodily fluids.
And then a multitude of physicians treated him for about two years, and none of them had skill in the art, until I was called.
And I saw his leg and the materials that flowed from those openings, a great deal; and already the man's body was all wasted, and his complexion was yellowish.
And then I inserted a probe into one of those openings, and the probe reached the bone.
And then I probed all the openings and found them extending to various parts of the leg.
And then I probed and incised one of those openings until I uncovered a part of the bone, and found it decayed, already brittle and blackened; and a part of the leg was already decayed, having penetrated to the marrow.
And then I sealed what I had uncovered and prepared of the exposed bone.
And I judged that it was not in the bone but that decay which I had cut out and sealed, and I had already drained it.
And then I applied restorative medicine to the wound for a long time, but it did not heal.
And then I again probed and uncovered the bone a second time over the first discovery, and found continuing decay with the bone.
And I sealed what appeared to me of that decay, and then I was forced to make its cure and restoration, and it was not restored.
And then I discovered it again and did not stop cutting the small bone piece by piece, and forced its restoration, but it was not restored until I cut around a palm's width of it, and extracted its marrow, and then restored that with medicines, and it was slightly consolidated and healed.
And no further repetition in the operation or section of it is necessary except for the weakness of it and its little tolerance and fear of death, for it suffered at all hours from a bad fainting due to an excess of evacuation.
And therefore healed entirely, and nothing is in place of bone but hard flesh; and its arrangement within its body is good, and its virtue has returned; and it has been restored to its proper condition, and it caused no hindrance in walking nor harm in any way.

And place a stick or board under the bone properly, for when you do this, the cut is not forbidden from not cutting the corrupted flesh.
And the cutting should be done over the corrupted area little by little, for if the corruption is in the hollow of the bone and does not appear in its surface to the touch, then you should cut it again.

And the cutting should be done precisely over the area of the corruption, a little at a time, as we have said.
And if the corruption is in the joint, cut that corrupted joint, and saw the bone where it continues from the other part.

Of cutting edges and the sawing of bones.
The extremities can be affected either by an internal or external cause, or if your treatment with those remedies does not improve the condition, or if the disease has advanced to a part of the limb that has not been affected by any cause, then you must cut that part of the limb where the illness has reached, in order to prevent the patient from dying or suffering worse damage to the limb.

And if the illness or injury is in the extremity of the finger, then you cut the finger and do not allow the illness to spread to the hand.
And similarly, if the illness reaches the hand, then you cut it from the base of the forearm, and do not allow the illness to spread so that it reaches the arm's forearm.
And if instead of that, then cut the arm up to the elbow at the joint.
And if the illness passes beyond the elbow, then in that case, it is the patient's death; and to administer any other treatment for this is a waste of effort.
And similarly, do the same for the foot. When the illness reaches the toe of the foot, then you cut it up to one of the ankles.
And if it takes hold in the arch of the foot, then you cut off the entire foot. But if it ascends up to the knee, then you cut the leg up to the knee joint.
And if the illness has already reached the knee, then there is no remedy but resignation, and to leave the patient to death.
And the way to cut or sever the limb is to tighten the ligament in the area just below where you want to cut, and tighten another ligament above the spot; and the attendant should pull a ligament downwards and another upwards; and you separate the flesh between the two ligaments, widening the space until all the flesh is exposed; and then it should be cut or severed.
And it is necessary to place a cloth on all sides so that it does not touch the affected area, and that the patient receives a regulated and carefully applied wound.
But if blood flows when you are working, then you cauterize the spot carefully, or place some blood-stopping powders on it; and then return to your procedure until it is complete.
And then bind the wounded limb with a suitable bandage, and treat it until it is healed.
And I relate to you what happened to a man on his foot, the same accident I and you have recounted, which is that a blackening of his foot occurred from a burn that was similar to a fire's combustion.

For a long time, that same corruption happened to him in the finger of his index hand.
