And I have related this story not only to be helpful in understanding this illness, but also to serve as a sign, by which it is recognized and by which it is treated.
Of the treatment of sinuses and the quality of enemas with medicinal remedies.
When an abscess occurs on fleshy parts of the body, and the abscess cavity becomes prolonged until it collects pus, and then it bursts and is pierced, and pus comes out, leaving an empty space like a vessel, and the surrounding skin is like a thin cloth, and it hasn't progressed to a deeper corruption, and it doesn't affect the bone, nerves, or ligaments, it is called a sinus, and not a fistula.
However, if the cavity is prolonged until the corruption reaches any part of the body, then it is called a fistula and not a sinus.
And the treatment for it is to cut through that surrounding skin, just as it is rolled, especially when it is thin and like a cloth, and making sure that it does not adhere to the affected area because of the corruption.
But if you suspect that the skin adheres and that the corruption hasn't yet penetrated deeper, and there is a gap in the skin, then treat it with an enema.
And if you consider it a sinus, and if it is large, and the discharge has a foul odor, then inject it with an Egyptian medicament, which is olive oil, sour wine, and vinegar taken in equal parts, and combined in a vessel, and heated over a fire until the mixture becomes reddish and takes on the consistency of honey.
Then, take what is necessary from this mixture, dissolve it with water and honey; use this to inject the sinus, and compress the opening, leaving the medicine there for about two hours.
Then, remove it with care; and do this for so many days until the sinus is cleansed and the bad odor is gone.
And sometimes, if this medicament is unavailable, it is injected with ash water; and you should take the ashes of wood, and mix them with water; and then strain it and inject the sinus with it until you are confident that the sinus is then closed off.
And if the patient cannot tolerate the Egyptian medicament or the ash water, then inject with water and honey, mixed with crushed vinegar, and inject with wine and honey mixed, for the nature of honey is to cleanse and wash, and wine helps to constrict the sinus, especially when the wine contains an excess of astringency and dryness.
And when you have done this several times and are confident that the sinus is now healed, then inject it with something that promotes flesh growth in that area.
And you should take diafinicon ointment and dissolve it in rose oil and astringent wine; or inject with other medicines and ointments that have the properties that I have already described in the treatise on ointments.
And if the opening of the sinus is narrow, so that the enema tube cannot be inserted, then you should enlarge it gradually with a small iron, and apply heat wrapped in Egyptian or basil ointment until it is widened.
And similarly, if the opening is wide, then you should bring the lips together with sutures, leaving a quantity in which the enema tube enters without additional space.
And if the opening from which the protrusion is elevated to the upper parts, then dry that area in the lowest location possible so that the protrusion descends, for when the protrusion is cut into the depths of the sinus, flesh will be generated there.
And if it is not possible for you to dry the sinus to the lowest bone as desired, then you must prepare the limb with a preparation through which the protrusion flows easily according to your equipment, and so that no cause retains the protrusion.
But the one over the sinus opening, which remains all the time to prevent what is adhering to the protrusion from coming out. And so that you may know if what comes out of the protrusion is much or little, or digested or undigested.
And with this, consider the location of the sinus; if the patient feels pain there, and if it is an abscess.
And when your treatment continues in this manner, and you see the material covering the sinus opening has adhered or is becoming attached, then on the third day, carefully remove the adhesion.  When you remove the plaster, change the cloth and renew the ointment.
But if from the sinus a subtle virus spreads, then do not be discouraged by the adhesion, for healing in such cases takes time; for some individuals, flesh generation takes longer in their wounds, while in others, the opposite occurs.
And if from the sinus undigested matter has been discharging for many days, then know that the sinus has not adhered; and if the adherence of the sinus is delayed and the cause prolonged, then adjust the drying properties of the medicines.  If the medicines are moist in their substance and potency, they should be dried, like diaphanous ointment, when superfluous additions of zegi (possibly a type of medicinal ingredient) are adjusted in them.
The final remedy, with which to treat this, is to take diaphanous ointment, dissolve it in rubbed oil, and add aged wine that is viscous in its substance to it.  Mix these thoroughly and use the resulting mixture.
And similarly, in cases where there is no discharge, use diaphanous ointment, honey that has been boiled until thickened, and apply it; or take myrrh, aloes, and incense, grind them all into powder, sprinkle it onto the heated honey, then spread this mixture on a cloth and bind it to the sinus.  Or use the thickened boiled honey, sprinkled onto a cloth, and apply that.
Sometimes, long-stemmed aristolochia and the roots of the lily of the valley (and it is the lily of the valley), and the flour of eryngo and centaury, are used. Grind these simple ingredients, sift, and sprinkle the powder onto the honey previously mentioned; use this mixture.
And if the sinus is prolonged and not healed by this treatment, then know that the corruption is in the underlying bone, or in other hard members or ligaments.  Then treat it with a fistular cure, as described above.
Of the treatment of whitlow, and of the afflicted nail, and of the incision of the added finger, and of the sectioning (or division).
Whitlow is an abscess that arises beneath the nail of a finger of the hand or foot, and frequently arises on other fingers.  If it is prolonged, and its treatment is less successful, or if it is an abscess from a hot abscess, or if it is corrupted, and puts forth pus until the root of the nail is eroded, and often entirely corrupts it, and often carries its corruption to the bone until it has a foul odor, and the fingertip is made gangrenous, and its color becomes dark.
And therefore, when you have done as we have said in the division, and your treatment does not succeed, then you must cut away all the remaining superfluity of the nail with an iron, and then cauterize the wound with this, for cautery is very helpful in this.
However, if the bone is sound and the nail is healthy, and the nail's additions have already compressed the flesh to the surrounding parts, and are beginning to pierce and tear it, then you must place a fine needle under the angle of the nail that is piercing the flesh, and lift that flesh up and cut that flesh easily, and apply to what remains of the flesh burning and corrosive medicines until it is completely removed; and then treat it with salves until it is healed.
But if the corruption has already penetrated the bone, then you must cut into that bone and remove it, for a bone corrupted in such a place is never healed.
And if the corruption and decay have spread to the finger, then you cut into it at one of the joints according to the means of its recovery, and then treat it until it is healed.
But if the nail has suffered a blow or friction, and there is severe pain there, then you must first draw blood from the patient, and then cut the nail with a sharp instrument, making a transverse cut from above downward, and be careful that the cut does not reach the flesh underneath the nail, for this will cause the patient extreme pain and lead to a growth of unnecessary flesh in the area; and then treat that area with whatever relieves the pain.
But a finger that has grown abnormally large in some people's hands, is often fleshy, and often has excess bone, or extra nail growth.
And some of these growths are born at the base of the joint, or are born in some of the joints of the fingers. But that which grows in some of the finger joints is not harmful, and that which grows in the joints is often painful.
And for those that are fleshy growths, the cutting is easy, and you should slice it away from its base with a broad blade.
But for that which grows at the base of the joint, the cure is difficult; therefore, be careful of cutting it off.
But for that which grows on the finger near the joints, you must first cut away the flesh around it, making a circular incision down to the bone, then use one of those appropriate saws to sever it, and then treat it until it is healed.
But if the fingers stick together, either due to birth defects, or through a wound, burn, or similar, then you must sever that adhesion until the fingers return to their natural shape.

And similarly, if the fingers adhere to the palm, you must sever that adhesion according to the appropriate preparation and the shape of the limb.
Concerning the incisions of varicose veins.
Varicose veins are swollen, thick veins filled with melancholic superfluities that appear in various parts of the body, but most commonly in the legs, especially in travelers, laborers, and those who carry heavy loads.
Then it is necessary that you administer a purge of the body of black bile several times with strong expulsion, and then bleed the patient from the basilica vein.
But the treatment of them with iron is according to two methods: one is that they should be incised and the black blood drawn out, and the other method is that the vein should be raised and completely extracted.
The incision is done in this way: first, soak the leg with very hot water until the thick, phlegmy blood in the leg is resolved.  Then, tightly bind the patient's leg from the highest part of the thigh down to just below the knee with a bandage. Then, cut the vein in one, two, or three places with a wide incision.  Next, elevate the black blood with your hand from the lower part of the leg upward, and then downward, until the desired amount of blood has been drawn, which will lessen the patient's illness.
And then bandage it, and instruct him to abstain from foods that generate black bile.
And repeat the treatment by phlebotomy when the veins are filled again and this is noted in the patient.
But the raising of them is done this way: you take the patient's leg, if it is very hairy, then place it in a bath and soak the leg with hot water until it turns red and the veins are prominent; or, if a bath is unavailable, you apply a vigorous exercise until the limb is heated.
And then cut the vein on the right side of the vein, by incision either along the back of it from the knee down, or lower on the back of the foot.
And then open the vein with hooks and scrape the vein from all sides until it appears sensitive, and you see the reddish colour turning to black.
And then, as you evacuate, you see a white cord-like substance from the vein.
And then you place a rag under it until it is raised and pulled out of the vein, and suspend it with a hook or a lion's claw.
And then make another incision near that incision, after three days, and scrape the vein on the upper side until it appears.
And then raise that [part of the vein] and cut it in a posterior incision towards the heel.  Then pull that out and lift it until it emerges through the second incision, and then pull it out of the incision that is above it.
Do this until you extract it from the incision that is highest or most superior of all the incisions, until it is all out; and then cut that [the remaining part].
And if you are unable to pull and raise it, then insert a needle with a strong double hook and pull it out, or place a rag under it and rotate your hand around until it is expelled. Be careful that it is not cut; for if it is broken, it will be difficult for you to raise that vein, and it will cause harm to the patient.
And when you have completely removed it, then place on the wound area wool soaked in olive oil (both plain and medicated), and treat it until healed.
And if the vein is twisted, having contortions and folds in its parts, and is not straight as we have said, then you must cut on those contorted parts and folds in it, and then grasp it with hooks until you have lifted the whole vein.
Be careful with your incision not to cut the vein and cause it to be damaged, for it will be difficult for you to raise it; and use your caution in this.
Shape of the incision with which the vein is cut:
It is not too sharp, like hooks, nor does it have a sharp point, so as not to damage the vein; and it should be of a thick and flexible material.  For if it were thin, it would cut the vein due to its thinness;  and therefore it has the necessary, but not excessive thickness, as we have said.
Of the extraction of the vein of the leg.

And it is easily generated in other parts of the body besides the legs.
And its formation is from putrefaction that arises from beneath the skin, like serpents, worms, and maggots that breed between the skin and the flesh.

Therefore, it must be treated gently and extracted little by little, until it is expelled from behind it and no trace of it remains within the body.
And from this vein, some men have had it grow to a length of five palms and ten fingers, and it has come to my attention that one has extended to twenty palms.

Of the cutting of worms generated beneath the skin, and it is called the bovine illness.
This illness is called bovine disease in some lands near us, because it often affects cattle, and is a small worm that is born between the skin and the flesh, and travels throughout the body up and down, and is felt by its movement from one limb to another until it bursts through the skin and exits.
And its generation is due to the putrefaction of certain humors, as occurs with worms, snakes, earthworms, and ascarids in the belly.

Of the incision upon the ailment.
This illness is called nafir in our land, and it is a pain that comes upon certain limbs, and then moves from one limb to another.
And I have already seen that, as I have told you. I was called to a sick woman in a town, and I examined her arm and saw a small inflammation in the vein of the arm's forearm.  After an hour, I saw that inflammation moving like a worm ascending the arm, but more quickly than one might expect, like living silver moving from place to place.
And then the pain moved to that area and settled into the muscle, and then I remained there for an hour.
And then it moved to the other arm until it reached the other arm.
And then I observed it moving throughout her entire body as I watched; and I was greatly amazed by its movement from limb to limb and its speed. And I had previously seen something like this with my own eyes, but I have often observed pain shifting from limb to limb. But I did not see it with my own eyes in this way in the woman.
And I did not consider it to be anything other than that because the woman was old, with dry body and exposed veins, and this apparent shifting of the air/wind was felt in this way.
And it is necessary that this "backward-healing" (or "reverse healing") not appear in those who are delicate and with moist bodies and hidden veins.
And if you want to cure him, and the patient feels that pain, then it is not apparent to your eye as we have said, so you gently and lightly press on the area until that trapped wind/air is released, and cauterize the affected area.
But if you don't see it with your eyes, then you cure it with body cleansing and with things that expel wind/air, and use aromatic pills and pills of [herbal remedy "serapi"] and similar remedies.
Of extracting arrows.
Arrows are diverse according to their type and the body parts they are aimed at, for their diversity lies in those with large points, small points, concave shapes, blunt shapes, solid shapes; and those with three angles, and those with tongues; and those with feathers.
And the signs of brain injury are when an arrow hits it and pierces the skull, and exits the cranium above the brain, for it causes sudden severe pain, dizziness, vertigo, and redness in the eyes, inflammation and redness of the tongue, spasms and confusion of mind, and vomiting of yellow bile; and the ability to speak is severely impaired, and the voice is lost; and from the wound site, a white, dust-like moisture exits, and the area around the wound is similar to the watery consistency of flesh.
Therefore, if you see these signs, then you refrain from treatment and from extracting the arrow, unless it is protruding.
But the signs of an arrow striking the heart, if it is near the left nipple, are that it feels as though it has struck a solid object, and not an empty space; and the arrow's movement is very similar to the pulse, and black blood flows from the wound; and the patient feels coldness in the extremities, and cold sweat, and fainting; and therefore know that death is certain without any doubt.
And the signs when the arrow has struck the lung are profuse frothy blood from the wound, and the vessels near the neck become inflamed, the patient's complexion changes, they exhale heavily, and they crave a rush of cool air.
And if the arrow strikes the membrane that is in the foot, the pain is then near the small ribs, and great pain and gasping are caused, and all the muscles of the limbs are affected.
And if the arrow strikes the stomach, then immediately undigested food will come from the wound.
And if the arrow strikes the abdomen, and is embedded, there is a visible tear in the wound, or the intestines or flesh are ruptured, and there is no hope for a cure beyond removing the arrow.
And if the arrow strikes the bladder, and urine flows from the wound, and some cause of harm spreads to the exterior parts, and severe pain affects the patient, then know that it is fatal.
But the other limbs, such as the face, neck, throat, shoulders, and limbs adjacent to them, the vertebrae of the back, the collarbone, the thigh, and the leg, and their similar parts, are safe according to the greatest dispositions when the arrow does not hit a vein or artery and is not a poisoned arrow.
And I tell you this, what I have seen of the nature of these arrows, so that you may consider this in your treatment.

And if you are bloodletting on the surface of the foot, then be careful of the nerves beneath that surface, and make your incision across the vein, as if you were intending to cut it through.
And let the bloodletting be skillful.
But if the release of blood is prohibited, then you immerse the patient's foot in warm water and allow the blood to flow out until it is evacuated.
And if the blood-letter errs the first time, then repeat the bloodletting slightly higher, for the spot is healthy; and do not fear harming them, but rather be careful of the nerves as we have said.
And similarly do the same on the sole of the other foot also.
And for the sciatic vein, the spot, as we have said, is below the heel, under the bone of the outer side.
And the benefit of bloodletting him is for hip pain when it is caused by hot blood.
And the procedure for bloodletting him is that the patient enters a bath, and quickly and tightly bind the thigh, from the wide part, up to above the heel with a long, thin bandage for four days, for it appears only through this.
And therefore, when it appears, then you should perform phlebotomy on that spot using any possible and suitable instrument; either cut it with an incision or make a section cut, for the site is good/suitable for the treatment.
And it is often hidden in many men; and if you find it, and it doesn't appear on touch, then lance a branch of it.  This is the one that appears on the back of the foot near the aforementioned auricular and annular (joints/areas).
And be careful of the nerves, and let the blood flow out in the quantity you desire.
And then, release the constriction and place a compress on the site of the phlebotomy and tightly bind the area, for it will heal quickly.
Of cupping and the qualities of their use.
Cupping is of horns, or of reeds, or of glass.
And cupping is done in two ways: one of them is with incision and extraction of blood, and the other is cupping without incision.
And this cupping that is without incision is in two ways, for it is either with fire or without fire.
And to the cuppings that are administered with incision and blood extraction are fourteen places on the body, of which the first place where cupping is placed is the occiput, and it is the back part of the head; and the middle place of it is of two parts of the neck together; and cuppings of the chin below the jaw and below the mouth; and cuppings of the two shoulder blades; and cuppings of the elbow, and are above the end of the forearm; and cuppings of the two biceps; and cuppings of the two elbows; and cuppings of the two thighs.
And cupping does not extract blood except from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and therefore it does not diminish the virtue of those veins, as happens with phlebotomy; nor is it necessary that cupping be done with or without fire in any illness caused by repletion until the whole body is evacuated.
However, if necessity prompts cupping for illness or for a continuous condition, we administer it at any time at the beginning of the month and at the end of it, and in the middle of it, and at any time.
And therefore, when in any of these cases, and specifically if it is in the third or middle part of the month, then we order cupping to be done after two or three hours have passed.
And the benefit of cupping the nape of the neck is that when it relieves heaviness in the head and what is affecting the eyes; but it is necessary that this be done after the evacuation of the whole body.
And this cupping is in the location of the cephalic vein's bloodletting.
And it is not permissible for one who is of a cold constitution, or in whom there is a cold, to use it, for it gives them great harm.
And therefore, it is not appropriate for the elderly, or those with cold diseases of the head, to use it.
But for whoever is continuously affected by it, it causes oblivion; and therefore, you must command the cupper to gently move their hand a little below the cup, fearing the generation of oblivion.
However, cupping with alcohol is in the place of bloodletting from the vena fusca and basilica veins: and therefore, it is beneficial for asthma, constriction of the breath, splitting of the breath's instrument, coughs, and congestion.
And it is necessary that the alcohol cupping be slightly raised, for if it is placed lower, it engenders weakness in the body and stomach.
And cupping with *alhagdahan* is beneficial for accidental pains in the head, and for ophthalmia, migraine, and fevers, and for pains in the roots of the teeth; and it is a substitute for bloodletting from the basilica vein.
And you must instruct the cupper not to press their hand with incision, so that they do not cut the artery and cause a flow of blood.
And cupping below the chin is beneficial for ulcers in the mouth, and for corruption of the gums, and for similar mouth ailments, and it is in place of bloodletting from the vessels (or blood vessels) along the two lips.
But cupping done above the two shoulder blades is beneficial for heatstroke, which is caused by an excess of heat.
And cupping on the two sides within the armpits is beneficial as much as bloodletting from three veins, namely the basilica, cephalic, and brachial veins, because it draws blood from all the smaller veins in the flesh, and those small veins draw from the larger veins until the drawing is accomplished in the three veins.
And instruct the cupper not to overdo the incision, for the area is lacking flesh, and underneath are nerves and arteries.
And a cupping treatment applied to the buttocks is beneficial for hemorrhoids and ulcers located lower down.
And you should instruct the cupper to use a large, strong cupping glass, because the area needs a strong suction; and weak, flimsy cupping glasses are easily broken and are better avoided.
However, cupping on the legs should be done sparingly, for they draw blood from the entire body, and it benefits old pains in the kidneys, liver, and bladder; it also brings on menstruation, and relieves heaviness and swelling; it is similar in effect to bloodletting from two main veins, and then, afterward, from two lesser veins.
However, it greatly weakens the whole body and can cause fainting in many people.
And the benefit of cupping in this way is similar to the benefit of cupping both legs.
And the quality of applying cupping is to place the cupping glass empty at first and to suck gently, and do not prolong the cupping, but place them lightly, and remove them promptly, so that the humors come to the area by flowing in. And do not stop repeating this and continuing until you see the area reddening and swelling and the redness of the blood appears.
And then you make incisions and repeat the suction gradually, and then consider the disposition of the body.
For that person with delicate, easily injured skin, you should make incisions only once, lest the area become ulcerated.
And instruct the cupper to enlarge the incision slightly, and to moderate the suction gently.
And if the blood is thickened, then you should make incisions twice, because on the first occasion, the path for the blood and its watery elements is opened. But on the second, the scrutiny gained from the extraction of the thick blood.
And if the blood is very foul, then a third incision should be made to achieve the final result.
And finally, when we want to extract little by little or a small amount of blood, a single incision is sufficient.
And if we want to extract a lot of blood, we make many incisions; and if we know that the blood is thick, then it is necessary to make deep incisions.
And the term for incision is one in which the depth is only the width of a fingernail.
And if the area where the fingernail is thick, hard, dark, and its pores are tight, then the places for the cupping vessels should be rubbed with remedies that are soothing, opening, and resolving; if it is summer, such as almond oil or violet oil, or the oil of soft almonds, or the oil of poppy seed; and if it is winter, then narcissus oil, or lily oil, or chamomile oil, or blood oil, and similar ones.
And if the superfluity is thick and cold, then the oil should be of amaranth, or balm, or almond, or dill, and their similar ones.
But if the person being cupped has wide pores in tender flesh, then it is necessary to prohibit the use of oil, and it is necessary that the areas around the cupping jars be washed with water or with cool water from solanum, or poppy water, or water of wood sorrel, and similar ones.
And for someone whose blood is of moist and heavy nature, wash their cupping jars with sour wine, or water of myrtle and sumac, and similar things.
And for those whose superfluities are thick, wash their cupping jars with old wine, or with water of balm (majorana), or with a decoction of dill, or chamomile, and the like.
And the cupping should be guarded before a bath; having been administered shortly after the bath, for one or two hours, and it is necessary that they rest after the cupping.
What is that which should be governed (or controlled) in the person being cupped (or bled) before the cupping (or bloodletting)?
It is necessary to consider at the beginning: if the person being cupped, or the one about to be bled, is choleric and has blood that is sharp and inflamed, then it is necessary that they take things that cool it, such as  milfoil (or similar astringents), endive in sour wine, lettuce, and secanabin, and juleps, and the like.
And give them foods like pomegranates, and the flesh of small birds, and foods seasoned with acids, and the like.
And for someone whose constitution is cold, it is necessary that they drink almiva syrup, or alsecanabin seed syrup, and sumac, and a moderately sweet wine, neither too old nor too young; and they should be advised to eat little and drink little, and give them foods like pomegranates, hempseed, pigeons, and small birds.
And it is necessary that on the day of cupping and bloodletting, the drink should be more than the food.
And it is necessary to give some people theriac alpharac, or musk-based medicines, that is to say musk, or alchelite, before the cupping or bloodletting or shortly thereafter, so that the main parts of the body are strengthened and the blood is refined;  and it is not necessary for them to take it heated.
But the cupping that is done without incision should be applied to the liver, spleen, gout, and navel, on the knee joints, and the groin area, because these parts do not tolerate incision on them.
And it is not sought for them, but rather the drawing of blood from a limb, just as we place a cupping glass on the breast in a sickness of blood flowing from the nose; and we use this so that cold, trapped wind in the limb is dissolved, as we apply to the stomach and navel; for this action rarifies the limb, warms it, and lessens the pain, because it resolves the wind.
And it is already placed on the lungs when they are congested or full of phlegm, for by the power of strong suction it readily opens congestion and eradicates the phlegm from its location.
And similarly, it works when placed on the liver and spleen to draw out wind trapped within them.
And these cupping glasses are administered empty, with suction only; and sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered full of lukewarm water in illnesses of the spleen.
And this is so that the cupping glass is filled and becomes larger; this is either just hot water or water in which suitable herbs have been boiled.
The form of a cupping glass administered with fire:  Let the opening's width be two fingers, as we have shaped it; and let the amount of the heated portion be half a palm; and let it be on his side, in the area that is within the curve, half of it; the hole should be small enough that only a needle's thickness could enter it.
It should be made of a reddish or citrus wood, with thick margins, lion, oak, and elm for it to not harm the limb, maintaining its position on it.
And in its middle should be a transverse rod of wood or iron, upon which a candle with fire is placed.
And this cupping glass should be significantly larger than the one we previously described; and the smaller one should be adjusted depending on the illnesses and age of the person using it.
The cupping glasses for children and the weak are different from those for young adults and those with strong bodies.
And the method of applying this cupping glass with fire to the limb is to heat the charcoal with decent wood fire before a small beeswax candle, and place that on the middle of the transverse rod in the middle of the cupping glass, so the fire is lit around the lower portion, so that the patient's body is not burned.
Then, it should be placed on the patient's limb, and the finger should be placed on the hole we mentioned, until when we have the cupping glasses and don't need them to be shown, and the vapor escapes through the hole, then the cupping glass will quickly dissolve.
And ignite the charcoal according to its method, and repeat it if you need it.
But a cupping glass used for pleurisy, with water, in which there is no transverse rod nor hole, and is not administered unless filled with water, and is placed directly onto the limb.
And this is its form:  And the larger this cupping glass is, the better, because it can receive a larger quantity of water.
Chapter 97.
Leeches are not administered to many limbs, but only to those limbs where cupping is not possible, due to their small size, such as the foot and the gums, and similar parts; or to a limb without flesh, such as the finger and the nose, and their similar parts.
And the quality to use of those leeches, if you have found leeches, is in the still, clear waters of muddy areas.
And they should be left in fresh water day and night until they are hungry, and no food remains in their stomach; and then the body should be emptied initially with bloodletting and cupping; and then the patient's limb should be rubbed until it turns red; and then they should be placed on it.
And when they are full and loosened.
And if suction from the area with a cupping device is possible, it is better for the improvement.
And if not, then wash the area with vinegar and then with clean water; and it should be rubbed and squeezed.
And if the flow of blood is prolonged, when the leeches are drawing blood, and it is oozing, then you immerse a cloth in cold water and place it over it until the oozing is stopped.
And if the blood is excessive, then sprinkle powdered vitriol, or other similar styptic substances, onto the area until the bleeding is stopped; or place roasted bean halves over the area and leave them until the beans adhere to the spot, and the bleeding is stopped.
And if necessary, repeat the application of leeches, when it's possible to change them.
And if the leeches are prohibited from being applied, then the area should be rubbed with fresh blood, or a needle should be inserted into the area until blood flows; and then applied, for those, when they sense any blood, quickly adhere.
And when you want them to drop off, sprinkle a little powdered aloes, or salt, or ashes on them, and they will fall off quickly.
Thus begins the treatment of the third part: of restoration. This chapter is very often necessary in the art of medicine; and it is the restoration of fractures and dislocations of accidental injuries to the bones.
O, son, know that foolish and vulgar doctors, and those who have never consulted the books of the Ancients or read a single letter from them, boast about this chapter.
And for this reason, this method of learned men in our land is private; and I have not forgotten this at all in any way; and I have not adopted this from what I have learned, but rather through the length of my reading of the Ancients' books and my study of their understanding until I extracted the knowledge from them.
And I will henceforth dedicate myself to experimentation and study throughout the length of my life.
And I have already described in this chapter everything that contains my knowledge and upon which my experiment has been performed; since I have summarised this for you and freed it from unnecessary length; and I have shortened it in conclusion; and have shown it in its final explanation; and have presented in it many forms of instruments that are used in it, as it is done by adding an explanation, just as I have done in the two previous chapters.
First Chapter.
Summaries and collections of bone fractures, and of those things that are necessary to be addressed before we begin to discuss the healing of broken bones and various types of fractures. Therefore, let us begin this chapter with a summary of the subject matter; and the topics that must be understood at the outset by artisans and those who seek to understand the truth of these matters; that is, you, and those who are eager to learn or to teach this noble art, either from you or others.
And so I say that when an accident occurs involving a fracture, dislocation, twisting, or a fall, then it is necessary that you begin with bloodletting or to lessen the inflammation, or both, if no reason prevents it, such as weakness of the patient's constitution, or if the person who has been injured is a child, or old and decrepit, or if the time is one of intense heat or intense cold.
And afterward, the patient should consume cool herbs and the flesh of birds and rabbits; and we forbid fatty meats and foods that fill the veins with blood, until you are sure of a hot abscess, and if you do not fear the effusion of matter onto the limb.
And then return the patient to their original regimen, with which they are accustomed.
And so, when the broken bone begins to be restored, then it is necessary that the patient eat nutritious foods, substantial and heavy foods in which there is viscosity, such as foods made from cooked wheat with water, and rice, and barley, and calf's feet and entrails, and eggs, and fresh fish, and thick wine and similar things to them, and with this regimen, the fracture is made to coagulate more easily and better.
And know that broken bones in the elderly and decrepit, in those in the initial stages of their frailty, cannot be joined and consolidated according to their natural process because of the drying up of their limbs.
But nonetheless, nature brings together and binds around the broken bone from every side a substance somewhat like glue, thick and adhering, which holds it together until it is completely conjoined and tightly bound, until it reaches its full strength and firmness, just as fire at the beginning, until nothing hinders it in its work.
And for this reason, it is necessary that the patient's diet consist of foods that are heavy, viscous, and substantial, as we have said.
And know that the types of fractures are diverse according to the diversity of the limbs, for a fracture of the leg bone is different from a fracture of the head bone; and a fracture of the foot bone is different from a fracture of the arm bone; and similarly, all the other limbs have different fractures.
And I come with a reminder of every type of fracture, explained in its own distinct chapter.
There is a great variety of fractures within themselves. (or:  Many different kinds of fractures exist.) And the types of bone fractures themselves are diverse; for a fracture is sometimes arched, sometimes splintered; and sometimes a fracture runs along the length of the bone: and these are fractures of the bone in the shape of stars, and splinters, and pieces that are either a result of, or not a result of, the injury, just as happens in a pen when it is prepared to write, and there is a fracture with a cut and a breaking in the head, and there is a small fissure fracture.
And for each type there is a specific remedy or treatment in restoring it, as will be a separate reminder in its proper place.
Clear sign of a bone fracture. And by this is a fracture of the bone known: it is the twisting of it, and the prominence and appearance of it to the touch, and that of it when you press it with your hand.
And therefore, when there is no apparent twisting in the location, nor is it felt upon touching the bone with pressure, and the patient does not experience significant pain, then there is no fracture here.
However, it is possible that there is a twist or a small crack, therefore it is not necessary to manipulate that with your hand and with a total compression; instead, place upon it the medicines of which there is a reminder at the appropriate time, suited to the location; and allow it to be tightened by a subtle constriction.
And know that when the location is bent but not split in two or otherwise damaged, such that the entire part of its structure remains, then it is necessary for you, at once, to begin rectifying it, lest a hot abscess occurs there.
And if a hot abscess occurs there, then leave it for a few days until the abscess subsides; and then heal it by any means you can, through gentleness and ingenuity.
And know that the restoration and healing of the soft tissues is easier than that of the bone on which damage has occurred.
And then strengthen it according to the subsequent remedy.
But if the bone is damaged, then an extension of the broken limb (whether the arm or the leg) is necessary, if it is a limb; or with your hand if the limb is small; or with two ropes or with one rope and your hand.
And your position, in which you place the limb in its natural shape while the parts of the broken limb are extended, then demands that you return those additions to their places with skill and by any method you can.
And take care, with all your strength, that your treatment does not cause further pain or injury to the patient; and, with all your ability, ensure that you place one bone with its matching partner in the best possible position.
And it is fitting that at that time you carefully touch it with your hand, and if there is any other cause, correct it and set it straight to the extent of your ability.
Avoid excessive extension and compression, as some foolish doctors often cause abscesses or contractions in the limb through their treatment, as we have seen in their many instances of treatment.
The proper treatment for a quiet limb is what I am recommending; so that you understand that broken limbs vary in their smallness and largeness, and in the form of those.
And for those that are small, such as the arm, finger, and foot, and similar parts, then the wrapping should be of a soft, thin cloth.
However, for those that are large, such as the thigh or leg, there should be a firm and strong wrapping, for the firm bandage connects the large limb and holds it tightly on all sides, so no gaps or spaces are present.
And then, begin, with proper alignment, placing a suitable bandage over the fracture site, using three or four wraps, depending on the limb's needs.  Hold the bandage firmly with your hand.
And now, move to the highest part of the fracture site, and hold the bandage less tightly than you did the fracture area. And now, gently loosen the wrapping around the fracture site little by little, until a considerable amount of the fracture site is free.
And then take another bandage and wrap it again over the broken area, and now move the wrapping towards the lower part of the fracture.  Your procedure should involve the tightness of the bandage and its loosening, as was described in the first wrapping above.
And then place linen or cloth between the wraps, if the fracture has a twist or irregularity; otherwise, do not place anything there.
Then place another bandage over this, and quickly secure these properly made wraps if there is no inflammation or painful swelling at the site.  If there is, place something that relieves the swelling and reduces the inflammation. Leave it on for a few days; then you tighten the bandages.
Let the splints be made from half-lengths of straight wood, and be skillfully formed; or from pinewood; or from palm branches; and let the width of each splint be according to this shape and this figure.
However, the splint placed over the fracture should be slightly thicker and wider than the other splints, and the length of the splint according to the size and smallness of the limb; and then tighten the splints with another bandage according to your initial tightening; and then tie it securely with a bandage, as described previously for tightening, and let the tightness be greatest over the fracture site itself; and the further away from the fracture, the less tight the bandage should be.
And the threads should be medium in thickness and fineness, and made of linen; and if the threads are thick, as I have seen with some fools using twisted cords for their bandages, and that is a great mistake, as the tightening loses its uniformity.
Likewise, extremely thin threads are not suitable, for you will not be able to apply the desired tightening with them, and there should be at least a finger's width between splint and splint.
And if the patient is irritated by the edges of the splints touching the affected areas, then place some soft material like linen or processed wool beneath them so that it does not cause him injury.
Sometimes the fracture is with a wound, and an accidental rupture occurs in the bone, and I will address that in the second chapter, in detail.
And know that not every broken limb should be splinted on the first day; this is because, when the injury is severe, you should not apply splints until after about 5 or 7 days, or even longer, depending on your judgment regarding the possibility of a painful abscess forming.
And this is the method of plasters used by the ancients for restoring fractures, dislocations, and sprains.
A common method of plaster for fracture repair, suitable for many conditions, especially women and children, because it does not generate heat or cold: take powdered flour, specifically the middlings of flour, which are the parts that remain on the walls of the flour mill during the grinding process.
And prepare it in this way, but sift it with egg white, and make the mixture neither thick nor thin; and then use it.
Another method of plaster for repairing fractures, dislocations, and sprains:  a recipe using asafoetida, ladanum, acacia, enula, and wild chamomile root (all of these in equal amounts); myrrh and aloes (each in half the amount of the previous group); and aloes (twice the amount of the previous group).  All should be ground and sifted, mixed with rosewater or egg white if the patient's constitution is hot; and then use this plaster, as it is a good compound for repairing slightly broken bones and is beneficial for many people.
Another method of plaster beneficial for fractures and sprains:  a recipe using wild chamomile and white marshmallow root (each in an amount of 10 drams); myrrh and aloes (each in 5 drams); acacia (6 drams); and Armenian bole (20 drams).
All should be ground in a mortar with thorough grinding, sifted, mixed with water or egg white, and then applied.
A method of plaster for joints and bones separated from their places, and for pain, cracks, and fractures:  take wool, soak it in sour wine and cooked oil, and place it over the affected area.
However, this plaster does not have the virtue of repair; it is good for suppurating wounds and expelling pain effectively.
Another method of plaster for a broken bone: take leaves of wild fig and leaves of wild poppy; grind them together and make a plaster from them.
A concise method of plaster for bone fracture repair and for resolving remaining pus: take roots of marshmallow, chamomile flowers, violet flowers, and powdered elder; grind them all together and mix them with wine, if the affected limb is not inflamed.
But if it is inflamed, then treat it with moistened coriander water or water and its use.
Another, stronger plaster for resolving stubborn pus, assisting in the repair of the limb: take marshmallow root, linseed, balms, melilot, marjoram, violet flowers, chamomile;  all in equal parts. Grind them together and mix with willow water, or fresh water, or wine, depending on the limb's condition and the color's tone.
However, Hippocrates in his book does not say that it should be placed on a broken limb during repair, but rather a cerot (plaster) made from wax and oil, and not anything else; and recommends that it should be of medium thickness/consistency.
However, in Gaul, it is seen that they are placed on the broken limb during repair, and the ingredients used for drying with a little warmth, such as myrrh, frankincense, and similar things to those we have mentioned.
A plaster for injury and pain: take equal parts of crushed chickpeas, human skin (or bird skin), marshmallow root, and salt; grind and sift them, then mix to form a plaster.
However, the length of time the bandage should remain, and when it should be removed, depends on whether pain, swelling, or a fracture or dislocation is present. If none of these are present, you do not remove it for several days. And if there is severe pain, swelling, or inflammation at the site, then you should remove or loosen it immediately, and remove the plaster.
And take a clean cloth and a dampened piece of sea sponge, immerse it in lukewarm water, and use it to wash the area until the swelling subsides and the pain is relieved. Then allow the limb to rest for an hour; afterward, place a piece of wool soaked in sour wine and oil or olive oil on the area, and bandage it for one night until you are certain the inflammation is gone and that a hot abscess has not formed and the limb's pain is gone.
And then repeat this subtle compression with a small plaster, and don't constrict it as tightly as the first time, and use it in that way until it is healed.
And if the abscess, soreness, pain, inflammation, and all the symptoms have completely disappeared, and you no longer need a plaster and compression, then you repeat the process in the same way as you did at the beginning.
And if no condition of those we have mentioned occurs on the limb, do not remove the dressing for three, four, five, or seven days; and sometimes it is left for twenty days, depending on the condition of the limb, as we have said, until the fracture adheres and the flesh coagulates around it.
Then increase the compression more than in your initial compression, and increase the patient's food intake, according to the patient's recovery.
But if you find the fracture site already too dry and weakened, then know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it.  Therefore, moisten it with warm water every time you remove the dressing, for three days.  Slightly loosen the compression, and through this action, nourishment flows to the limb, and it heals quickly.
However, what foolish bone setters do, restoring the limb fracture a second time when it was not properly restored initially, and is restored incorrectly, is an error in their practice, a great fear; and if this were correct, the Ancients in their books would have magnified it and would have operated in this way.
And I have not seen anyone of them do this, which makes it more reasonable that it should not be done.
Second Chapter: Of fractures when occurring to the head.
The types of fractures are many, and their shapes are diverse, and the causes are distinct, for some fractures are caused by a blow of a blunt object, and the blow crushes the entire bone down to the marrow, just as it does to a piece of wood, and because of this, this type is called comminuted; or the blunt object cuts a portion of the bone, and cuts the flesh around it, but does not pierce through to the back; and this type of fracture is called a simple, complete fracture.
And the severity of these two fractures is either great or small, and the fracture is either a crushing or a tearing; and the cause of this is a blow with a stone or a fall on a rock, and similar impacts.
And this fracture is either penetrating, approaching the marrow beneath the bone; or it is on the surface of the bone, and the severity of these two fractures is either great or small.
And a fracture is also one that is hidden within the bone, in the fine detail of small hairs, and because of this, this type is called a hairline fracture.
And a fracture is caused either by a fall or a blow with a stone or similar, which forces a portion of the bone inward, and occurs in a hollow area, just as happens to the ribs when struck, and is a separation of the bone parts inwards.
This is more likely to happen in the bones of young, moist bodies, such as the heads of children.
And for all these types of fractures, the pieces, are separated, like scattered stars. And I will return everything to its proper place.
And we recognize all these types of fracture by probing those areas and by examining them with probes and looking for signs of rotting flesh on top of them.
A fractured area is recognized because it is exposed on the bone, cleaned, and probed with a probe, for the fracture appears black.
However, in treating a fracture, first consider the patient's symptoms, and if you see symptoms that signify imminent danger, such as vomiting, diarrhea, loss of consciousness, loss of voice, fainting, high fever, swelling and inflammation of the affected areas, and similar symptoms in more serious conditions, without a doubt, the patient is not going to recover.
And if you see symptoms that are not serious, you hope for recovery, then you begin the treatment.
This is because if the patient has come to you in the early stages of illness, especially during the winter months, then you must examine the bone's condition within fourteen days according to all the circumstances.
And if it is during the summer months, then you should try to set the bone within seven days, being careful that the tissue below the bone is not corrupted by the pannicle, and that the symptoms we have mentioned do not occur.
And if the fracture has already reached the membranes covering the brain, and is fragmented and crushed, then you must cut away the shattered and crushed parts, as I will explain to you.
And you must shave the head of the injured patient, and examine the bone according to the custom and method possible for you, following the nature of the injury, and being gentle with the patient.
And if you encounter a bone protruding, blood flow, or a hot abscess, then you must treat it accordingly, filling the area with cloths soaked in warmed wine and oil, and letting the injury rest until the abscess subsides, and the blood flow stops; then begin working on resetting the bone and removing it.
And this will be done according to one of two methods of operation.
And one method is that you cut the bone with a narrow scalpel, and this is its form: And then you apply another scalpel, a little further out than the first; and this is its form:
And then you apply another scalpel further out than the second one.
And it is necessary that you have several different scalpels, some wider than others; and some of them are shorter than others, and have sharpened edges at their ends, and are made of iron or steel.
And use them with ease in striking the scalpel so that you do not strike the head and damage it.
And if the bone is strong, hard, then it is necessary to pierce around it before you apply the scalpels with the wedges, which are called non-deepening wedges.
And they are not called that (non-deepening wedges) because they do not penetrate the bone surface adjacent to the bone, because the wedge has a rounded end or edge on the part below the head of it, sharp like an edge or a small circle, which prevents it from sinking into and penetrating the thickness of the bone.
And it is appropriate that you take a large number of these wedges, each suited to the thickness of each bone, so that you have available in all the skull's bones, the ends of which are sharp, the length and shortness are according to the thickness of each bone.
And this is the way to use three types of wedges:  The method of piercing around the broken bone is to place the wedge on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know the bone is penetrated; then replace the wedge with another in a different location, and place that which is between all the holes to the amount of the thickness of the ray or around it.
And then, with scalpels, cut what is between each hole in the bone, making it as thin as possible in the end, as we have said, until you remove the bone, either with your hand, or with some other tool, such as pliers and fine forceps.
And you must take great care that the scalpel does not touch anything in the skull.
And therefore, when you have encircled the bone and separated it from the skull if it is continuous with it, and you have extracted it, you must scrape and smooth the rough edges of the bone using another tool similar to a scalpel, but it must be finer and more delicate than the other scalpels.
And if any small and jagged pieces of bone remain, then remove them easily with one of your tools; and then treat the wound with liniments and ointments, as we have mentioned.
And the other method of operation is light, free from fear, which Gualia describes and praises greatly; and this is his teaching: It is necessary for you at the beginning to start uncovering the part of the bone in the place where the fracture is most severe and most evident, until when you have uncovered that part and have placed under it the tip of this gentle and cheerful scalpel, of which this is the form:
And it is so that the lenticular part is smooth and does not cut anything, and the pointed part is on two sides along its length, in the manner as you see; so that the lenticular part is fitted to the skull with its pointed, cutting face on the bone.
Then strike the scalpel on one part with a small mallet until you cut the whole bone with ease as it is turned, and you are certain that the bone will not cause any accident that you fear in any way. And if the doctor were the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant of them; certainly, and if he were asleep...
Then if any cause remains attached to the bone of the pannicol in some places of the bone, cut that from it with the lenticular end of the scalpel, and free it with ease, for it is freed from it without harm and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate to the pannicol, and what is cut or removed from the bone is its surface, and roughness and subtle irregularities remain there, then you must scrape away that roughness and eradicate those irregularities with the fine razors you have prepared, in various sizes so that you can apply the most suitable ones to every area and the most correct ones according to where you are in your operation on that broken bone and its shape.
And you must use in your scraping of the bone at first the widest of those razors, and then use the finest ones, and do not cease doing this successively until you reach the finest and most subtle of all of them.
But in each of the other small, capillary fractures and subtle fractures, you must administer treatment according to what you observe for its repair.
And it is a cause that is not hidden from all those who have studied this art. And what we have written here applies to large fractures.
And if the skin is retracted when you expose the bone, then you must take a cloth equal to the size of the wound, soak it in heated oil, and place it over the wound's opening; then take another cloth, doubled or tripled, and soak it in wine and heated oil and place that over the first cloth, doing this as gently as possible so as not to press on the fractured area; and then apply a wide bandage, but not so tightly that it binds the wound too much.
And leave it with this treatment for a day or two until you are sure of a hot abscess; then remove it and administer medicines that counter excessive dryness, such as flaxseed, barley flour, ashes, and similar herbs.
And you should make a powder from these medicines, ground into a fine powder, and apply it to the wound; generally, administer any medicine that has the property of cleansing without being caustic.
And be careful, by virtue of your skill, that the wound remains clean in your treatment; avoid applying ointments or other things that would cause it to fester.  Do not allow virulent matter to accumulate, for when it gets to the skin surrounding the brain, it will corrupt and rot it, and this will bring about great harm to the patient.
And the fracture of the skull, if the bone is damaged, especially when the treatment is neglected and there is blackness on the surface,  and if you observe in the patient the symptoms we have described, know then that it is perilous without a doubt.
If the blackness is not from the wound itself, but from the medicine applied, and the medicine is causing this blackness, then you should take one part honey and three parts of rubbed olive oil, and with those, moisten a cloth, then place that over the fracture; and then treat it with appropriate healing spices until it heals.
Chapter 3.
Concerning the restoration of a broken nose.
Know that the nose is not broken in two pieces at once, usually, but just one sometimes, because there are two bones in it, for the lower part is cartilaginous, and thus it is not broken, but only abraded, twisted, and bent.
But if one of the two parts is broken, then you should insert your little finger into their nose, and manipulate the fracture inwards with your index finger, and outwards with your thumb, until the nose returns to its natural shape. And do this easily, and be careful that your action does not cause further pain to the patient.
And if the fracture is in the most important part of the nose, and the finger cannot reach it, then a small, thickened end of a probe should be used.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then do similarly.
And strive for a restoration within the first day of the fracture, if possible; and if not, then approximately on the seventh or tenth day you should provide rest for the inflamed area (or boil).
And insert a small piece of linen cloth into the end of the nose, if the fracture is in one part, or two small, thick pieces if the fracture is in two parts, the thickness of each filling the nasal opening.
And some of the ancient healers said that the pieces should be soaked in broth and changed daily, but I do not see this as necessary.  It is better that the cloth be soaked in a mixture of cooked egg white and powdered [ground] spice; and leave it in place until the bone is set and the cartilage is hardened.
And sometimes, instead of the pieces of cloth, a bird's feather wrapped in linen is inserted into the nose; this is done if the nasal fracture is severe and breathing is restricted.
And this method is not necessary for you; and if you wish, do it this way, and if you wish, use the cloth pieces.
And if an abscess develops later, plaster the nose with a cerate, or with cotton soaked in sour wine and roasted oil, or with diachylon.
But if no hot abscess develops, then you should cover the external one with barley flour and powdered herbs mixed with egg white; then place linen strips over it, and do not bind the nose with anything.
However, if the bones of the nose are fractured or broken into small pieces, then you should cut away the damaged areas and remove them with instruments appropriate for that; afterward, close the wound and heal it using things that promote flesh growth and healing, and with appropriate plasters.
And if a sore develops inside the nose, you should treat it with cloths and administer lead cannulae until it is completely healed.
Chapter 4.
Of the restoration of the jaw when it is broken.
When the lower jaw is broken and the fracture is not accompanied by a wound, observe whether the fracture is only slightly displaced and not broken in two but bent inward; in that case, its correction is simple.
And thus, if the fracture is on the right side, you should insert your left index finger into the patient's mouth; and similarly, if the fracture is on the left jaw, then insert the said index finger of the right hand; and push the protrusion of the fracture inward with ease, outward with your other hand on the outside of the bone; thereby gently repositioning it.
And if the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two pieces, then it is necessary to apply traction on both sides according to the straight line until its re-alignment is possible.
And if you encounter a displacement of teeth with fracture, then secure those you expect to remain with gold, silver, or silk thread; and then place a poultice on the broken jaw, and then place a folded cloth over it, and place a large, properly-made bandage, or a piece of cloth the length of the jaw, over that; and then tie it as you are prepared to bandage it and the application is appropriate, until it is not damaged.
And order the patient to use rest and tranquility, and provide him with easily digestible foods.
And if you estimate that the shape has been altered in any way, then you should remove and loosen the bandage on the third day; and then rectify what has been altered, and plaster it with powdered lime, egg white, or flour of millet, after you have removed the poultice, and place a soft wool bandage over the plaster.
And then, when that plaster is attached and the bone position is not altered, leave it, and do not remove it until the fracture is healed and tightened; for often this fracture tightens within three weeks.
And if another time a purulent abscess occurs, then administer what we have often said in its treatment until it is healed, and the abscess is resolved.
And if the fracture is with a fragment, then consider whether it is a detached piece of bone or splinters; then you must carefully remove those splinters with what is appropriate for removing them from the instruments.
And if the opening of the wound is narrow, then you should enlarge it with leeches according to your need.
And then, when you have removed those splinters, and nothing remains of them, then close the wound opening if it is wide; and if not, place one of the appropriate plasters on it and let it heal until it is cured.
Chapter 5.
Of the restoration of the collarbone when it is broken.
Often the collarbone is fractured near the front side, close to the muscle, and this fracture occurs in one of three ways.
When it is broken, or separated, or separated into two parts without splinters; and it is easy to restore it; or it is splintered, and its restoration is difficult; or the fracture is accompanied by a wound.
And the operation in this case, when the fracture is without a wound, is that you have two assistants present, so that one holds and steadies the broken collarbone, and the other extends the shoulder towards that area.
Then gently hold the fracture with your fingers until the proper shape is achieved, and it is neither overly prominent nor concave.
And if you need more extension, then you should place under the patient's armpit a pad of cloth or wool, of a size appropriate to your needs; and extend and elevate the collarbone and compress the pad with your hand until you have properly aligned the fracture.
And if you cannot pull the collarbone's extremity outward, because it has become caught, then the patient must be placed on their back, and you should place a cushion of moderate size on their muscles.
And the assistant should press on the patient's muscles below until the collarbone's bone, which is caught above, is elevated, and then you should realign the fracture and hold it in place with your fingers.
But if you feel splinters of the collarbone, and they are loose, then you should break them apart and remove those splinters easily.
And if it is lodged in the bone, then you should create an incision with one of the scalpels you have prepared for this, after placing the tool that supports the collarbone beneath the collarbone; and this tool is made of wood or iron, of which this is the shape:
And it is similar to a scoop, of which this is the concave part; and its width should be according to what you need, according to the size of the bone or its smallness.
However, its length should be according to what is possible for you in the operation; it should have two ends just like a pair of pincers, and one end should be wide and the other narrow.
And if the disruption of the wound that you have incised when you have extracted the splinter from the bone is wide, and you are certain of a pus-filled swelling, then you should bring together the two edges of the wound with stitches.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear a pus-filled swelling, then you should fill the wound with cloths and powders according to the extent of the wound's incision.
And if a pus-filled swelling occurs, then soak a cloth in sour wine and rubbed oil and wine, and place it over that.
And be sure that the splint does not move from the broken bone; and therefore, you must check the patient daily, and when you see the ligament has softened or the splint has already moved, then you must correct it and tighten the bandage.
Then place the patient's sleeping position on their neck, and place a small cushion under their armpit beneath their sleeping position for the night; so that with that, the patient's arm is raised from the side, and thus the fracture of the collarbone is elevated by the muscle; or the patient's arm is tied to their neck.
And do not loosen the bandage unless there is a suppurating or pus-filled swelling at the site within twelve days.
And then renew the plaster if you see the patient's skin is suitable for it, and adjust the tightening and restore it, and leave it until the fracture of the collarbone is restored and solidified.
And it is strengthened and comforted according to the month in 28 days, and sometimes in some men, it is done in less time than that.
Chapter 6.
Of the restoration of a shoulder fracture.
The shoulder blade is fractured in its lateral location, and it is not broken through, but only the margins.
And therefore, when the middle portion of it is fractured—and it is only known by touch—then, according to the appearance of the fracture, prepare it and reposition it according to its natural form by any means possible.
Then place powder of ground chalk mixed with egg white and linen bandages over the area; then place a folded cloth; and then place a thin wooden board—corresponding to the size of the entire shoulder blade, or a bit more—over it.
But if beneath the board there is a hollow in any part of the shoulder blade, then you fill that hollow with linen bandages, and then elevate the board to an even level.
And then bind it firmly with long strips, with appropriate constriction and firmness to the board, until it remains in place.
And check the bandage every day; and whenever it is loosened, bandage it again, and re-position the board if it has moved from its place.
And have the patient lie on their healthy side.
And the shoulder blade is restored in 20 or 25 days.
And therefore, when this number of days is complete, then you remove the bandage, and you will know for sure if the injury is of the bones, where the connection is not feared or their separation.
And if you proceed from the site of the heavy and painful bone, and place a splint under it, then you cut over it, remove it, and do as we have said for the fracture of the clavicle, for the healing of an abscess if this occurs here.
Chapter 7.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the foot.
The foot is sometimes broken in the middle, and this is often serious; but its extremities are more often damaged and broken into fragments.
And the symptoms when the middle of the foot is broken are that it inclines inward to the parts within, causing great pain, and difficulty in breathing, coughing, and a severe feeling and vomiting of blood; and the cavity in the broken bone is evident, not hidden from the sense of touch.
And the restoration of it is that the patient be laid on their back, and a cushion be placed between their shoulders; and then press on their muscles, and adjust their sides with the hands on both sides, and be diligent in the operation and in the joining according to every possible and suitable manner, until the bone returns to its proper shape.
Then place a plaster and bandage over it, and place a thin sheet of willow or rush, or similar lightweight material, over that, and then wrap it in cloth; and then be diligent in binding it tightly around the broken bone so that it does not move; and sometimes bind it in a circular manner to the back, and firmly tie that with proper tightening; and constantly observe the bandage, and when softened, tighten it.
And if necessity arises due to loosening, or suppuration, or pain, or an abscess that occurs in the area, then you must undo it and remove the plaster and correct whatever occurred by the treatment; and then you reapply the plaster, if necessary, to the area; and apply the bandage until it heals.
Chapter 8.
Of the restoration of broken ribs.
Know that a fracture of the ribs occurs only in the thicker parts, which are the middle sections; and on their extremities and forward parts, injury occurs only by this, because they are cartilaginous. And its knowledge is not hidden from the senses through investigation with words.
And the restoration should be such that the fracture is aligned with the fingers in the best possible manner, until it is aligned as necessary, and then apply a plaster and bind the broken bone with splints, if needed.
And if the rib fracture is towards the interior parts, then the patient experiences intense pain and a stabbing sensation, similar to the stabbing pain one with pleurisy experiences, and this is because the bone pierces the membrane; and it causes difficulty breathing, coughing, and vomiting of blood; and this is a difficult condition to treat.
And the ancients were ingenious in that regard with many inventions.
And of what they say:  it is necessary to withhold foods from the patient that engender inflation and flatulence, so that the belly is inflated and stretched, and the fracture is driven outwards.
But we would avoid this, for it is not a light cause to generate a hot abscess, if it doesn't occur; and if it already occurs, then it is helpful for that.
And some of them would say that it should be placed over a cupping glass and subsequently that it should suck with force; and it seems like a reasonable approach, but nonetheless, it is feared that the cupping glass will draw the superfluities to the area due to its weakness.
And some of them would say:  it is necessary to cover the area with wool already soaked in warm oil, and place powders between the ribs until it is full, so that the dressing is tight when wrapped around the body in a circular fashion; and then the patient should be treated with pleurisy cures, both dietary and medicinal.
And if a severe cause afflicts the patient, whom it cannot withstand, and the bone pierces the membrane with a harmful puncture, we fear for the patient, then it is necessary to make an incision over the affected area, expose the fractured rib, and then place an instrument over it that will hold the fractured segment in place, the preparation for which is beforehand, and we will carefully cut the bone and remove it.
And then, we should join the two sides of the wound, if it is large, with stitches, and treat it with plasters until it is healed.
And if an abscess occurs, then you should mix powders in rubbed oil and apply it to the area, and the patient should be treated with whatever is appropriate for an internal abscess.
And let him lie on his side on which he sleeps, until he is healed.
Chapter 9.
Of the restoration of a fractured vertebra of the back and neck.
Sometimes there is a fracture in the bones of the neck, and sometimes there is a wearing away of them, and similarly in the vertebrae of the back. And if this happens to someone, and you want to know if they will be healed or not, then consider this: if you find their muscles already softened, numb, dead, and cannot be moved, extended, or squeezed, and when you press them with your fingers or prick them with a needle they feel no pain, then know that they are not healed, and in this condition it is fatal.
And if they are moved and feel pressure from your fingers and pricking, then know that the bone's marrow is saved and the patient will recover with treatment.
And if you see similar symptoms in the vertebrae of the neck and want to know if they will be healed or not, then consider their feet.
Then, if you see that the feet are also softened, similar to what happens in the muscles, and when they lie on their backs, they experience involuntary movements and evacuations, and sometimes they are lying on their back and want to urinate but cannot, then know that it is fatal, and therefore do not bother with their treatment.
But if you do not observe any of those symptoms, the cause is better and less severe.
And the treatment for this is to apply a sedative to the inflamed abscess that you place over the affected vertebra, rubbed with oil or using crushed eggs; apply it to that spot several times a day until the inflamed abscess is soothed.
And then apply a comforting and stimulating plaster, and bind it tightly; and instruct the patient to rest and remain calm, and not sleep on the affected area until it is healed.
And if you observe signs of bone fracture or any other separated part, then it is necessary to dissect the area, and remove that bone; and then bring the two edges of the wound together, with sutures if it is a large wound; and then treat it with some flesh-inducing ointment until healed.
But if the back of the hip bone is fractured, and the hip socket is damaged, then you must insert the index finger of your left hand into the anus, and with the other hand, align the fractured bone as best as possible, using the correct anatomical alignment; and then apply a plaster and a bandage, if needed, and then secure it tightly.
And if you feel broken pieces within it, then you must excise those pieces, and treat the wound with the previously described treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
Of the restoration of the [broken] width/breadth/span.
The bones of the hips are often fractured, and if they break, the fracture is usually only a separation at the extremities and they are cut lengthwise and deviate in different directions; and the patient experiences pain and a puncture-like sensation at the site, and the leg becomes numb when fractured.
And the restoration of it is that you extend your hand over it, so that it is above the fracture, matching its shape.
And if the fracture is only at its extremity, then you align it according to what is available to you, fitting it in until it resembles its natural shape.
But if the fracture is lengthwise or has already shifted to the lower parts, then you have the patient lie on their stomach until the restoration of the fracture is ready for you. And then, when you have aligned it, place a plaster over it, and afterwards place a wooden or splint over that, and compress firmly so that you fear neither further piercing of the fracture nor movement of the splint.
And align the hollow with whatever fills it, until it holds the compression equally, and instruct the patient to sleep on their back or on their sound side.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then you cease the extension and restoration until the hot abscess is calmed, and apply what calms it as previously indicated; and then return to the restoration, and compress it as appropriate.
And if the bones are shattered or comminuted, then you set them.
Chapter 11.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the assisting bone.
Adjutori" is that which is between the elbow and the head of the shoulder.
And if it is broken, then its restoration is in one of two ways, of which one is that you take a splint of medium thickness, according to this form:
And two ligaments be tied to its two ends, and it should then be suspended from a raised position.
And have the patient lie on a bed; then place the patient's broken arm on the splint until it reaches the point on the middle of the splint where the curve of the arm should be attached.
And then place some heavy object on top of that, or have the attendant extend it further downwards; then the doctor should hold the fracture with their hands together, until the fracture returns to its proper position.
The other method is that the patient be lying on their back and tie the arm to their neck with bandages; then command two attendants, one of whom holds what is above the fracture with their hands, and the other holds what is below it; and each of them should extend it towards their respective side.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then tie a bandage on the fracture, and another bandage over that, and each of the two attendants should pull in their respective directions.
And if the fracture is close to the end of the muscle, then it is necessary that half of the bandage be placed on the point and the other half below the fracture near the elbow.
