And similarly, if the fracture is near the elbow, then the bandage should be placed over that area and over the elbow itself.
Apply this with ease and without effort; and then, once it is tied securely and properly applied, tighten it if there is no risk of a painful abscess forming.
However, if a painful abscess develops, then leave the tightening until the seventh day, and place some wool soaked in sour wine and crushed olive oil on it until the abscess subsides, and then tighten it.
And the way to tighten it is to place a poultice on the fracture, then wrap a fresh cloth around the poultice; then support the arm with this aid, and place the patient's open hand on their own muscle.
And place the cloth and tighten the support for the arm so that the arm is in the correct position, unless any aspect of the broken bone prevents this.
And then use splints.
And you should place the splint that is wider and stronger than the others over the fracture; and place the width of a finger between each splint, and the length of the splints should be according to the break, with an addition of three fingers on each side.
And then tighten the splints with the constriction which I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And make sure your constriction over the fracture site is tighter. And the longer the fracture, the less tight the constriction should be.
And therefore, adjust the placement of the splints and the constriction, as we have described, at the moment when you return the limb to its proper position, then do as follows.
And if you fear a suppurating abscess, then leave the constriction and splints on for seven days, as we have said.
And afterwards, consider the limb in all its parts for three days so that no swelling or inflammation occurs in the area; or the provision of food to the limb is prohibited to prevent excess from the constriction; and therefore, correct all this according to what we have stated.
But if you are confident that no cause for this will arise, then do not loosen the bandage until after three days or more.
And let the patient's sleep be on their back, and their hands be on their stomach.
And place a full, wool-filled poultice on the affected area, and be mindful at all times of the night or day that the shape of the broken limb is not distorted or that the bandage is not loosened; therefore, rectify all of this with your skill.
And provide the patient with food according to the order we previously mentioned, so that the food is light at the beginning until the strengthening has started; and then you must increase the amount of food for them.
Because it is customary for the poultice and the leg to be strengthened in 40 days; and then they must be loosened, a bath administered, and medicated with ointments that rectify that.
And if the fracture is badly bruised, then you do not loosen the bandages and splints for a day or up to two months.
Chapter 12.
Of the restoration of a fractured arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called the two forearm bones; of which one is small, and it is the one that sits beside the aforementioned wrist, and the other is large and is the one located beneath the smaller one, in the lower part.
And frequently the larger forearm bone is broken alone, or the smaller one, and frequently both are broken together.
And when the smaller forearm bone is broken above, then the restoration of it is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the bone of it is broken below, the fracture is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And worse than that is when both bones are broken together.
Therefore, if the bone that is broken is the smaller forearm bone above, then it is fitting for the physician, to ensure its restoration, that a small extension is placed with ease until it is healed.
But if the larger bone is what is broken, then it is necessary that a more forceful extension be applied.
And if both bones are broken together, then it is necessary that you apply the constriction much more forcefully.
And it is necessary that you apply the form of the hand to ensure its restoration and the extension of it, placed on a cushion, with the palm of the hand on the upper parts, higher than all the other parts mentioned, and the little finger lower than the other fingers, and the patient lie on a smooth, hard bed.
And there should be a cushion in front of him, at his height or level, so that the patient does not suffer further distress.
And then one attendant extends the lower arm with their hand or a bandage, and another attendant extends the upper arm similarly.
And then the physician manipulates the bone until it is returned to the best possible position.
And if there are fractures in the bone, then you must use your skill to reduce every fragment to its proper place.
And if a separated fracture appears in the bone that is causing a disruption and is not amenable to your restorative efforts, then you should operate on it, and remove it using the method previously described.
But if the fracture is with pus, I have already made a separate chapter on this, and therefore take care of it accordingly.
And if you encounter an abscess at the beginning of your restoration, then you should apply a cloth with a plaster made of warmed oil and white wax, which should be moderate in its thickness and subtlety, and firmly wrap the cloth around it until the abscess subsides; then remove the plaster and apply a prepared poultice of ground powder mixed with egg white, then place a bandage; and let the bandage that is placed on that pus-filled fracture be firm and strong.
And know that the name of the bandages for the arm is given in the major cases, that is, a fracture in one or two segments.
Then apply your bandage to the fracture area firmly and forcefully, and if the bandage is applied to the areas above the fracture or to the areas below, apply the bandage slightly more loosely in accordance with the previous instructions in the beginning of the chapter.
And the cloths wrapped around the fracture should be soft and moist, not hard or rough, and the bandage with which it is wrapped should be of a medium thickness, neither too subtle nor too coarse, just as we have described.
And consider the limb and its ligaments for a few days. And if any cause arises requiring correction, such as swelling occurring on the limb, then you should soak the limb in warm water until the swelling subsides.
And leave the limb unconstrained until it rests, then reapply the bandage.
And if the bandage has already softened, and the bone has already healed and resembles those around it, then correct all of this with your skill.
And further consider if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive tightness of the bandage, because then you must loosen it slightly and leave it for several days until nourishment flows to the limb, and then tighten it again.
But if the patient does not experience any of the causes we have mentioned, then it should not be loosened until approximately 20 days or thereabouts.
And then the patient's hand should be suspended by its power of active movement, and let him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is restored in 30 or 32 days, or more quickly in 28 days, and all of this is according to the disposition of the humours and the strength of the healing.
Chapter 13.
Of the restoration of fractures of the wrist and fingers.
Know that the wrist and the joints of the fingers are frequently fractured, and not only by impact but often by friction.
And when the hand is fractured or injured by friction, then the patient must be laid down, and a bandage should be applied to the area evenly, and then the patient's hand should be placed on that bandage.
Then the attendant extends the broken bones, and the physician binds them together until they are properly rejoined.
And then a poultice and a bandage should be applied, otherwise an abscess may form; and then a splint is placed over it according to the size of the area, and you have already wrapped it in linen cloth.
However, if the fracture is in the parts within the palm of the hand, then make a bandage shaped like a sphere of cloth, and instruct the patient to hold it with their fractured palm; then it should be bound with a long bandage.
And let the splint be of leather with cloth in which it is supported, so that the leather is applied to the palm; and bind that as necessary.
But if the fracture is in the outer parts, then it is necessary to place a splint over it and another splint on the inner part of the palm, so that the hand is open and straight; and then apply the binding, just as the hand is wrapped around the fingers with bandages.
And if a fracture occurs in any of the finger joints, if it is the thumb, it should be bandaged as necessary and then bound with the palm; and if you want, put a small, straight splint on it so that the fracture is elevated and not moved.
But if the fracture is in the other fingers, such as in the middle finger, or the index finger, or the ring finger, or the pinky finger, then it should be bandaged and tied with the adjacent finger; or all the fingers should be tied together in sequence, and that is better; or place a small splint over it, as we said for the thumb.
And consider, in your restoration, and subsequently, the disposition of a hot abscess and its opposition – with your operation – with what is necessary when any of these causes occur, according to how the narration of it has been repeated.
Chapter 14.
Of the restoration of a fractured thigh bone.
The thigh bone is often fractured and declared to be broken according to how it has been displaced forward and most severely.
And the restoration of it is done so that a ligament is tightened over the fracture and another ligament below the fracture, and the patient is laid prone on their belly and on their face.
And then the attendant stretches the whole ligament to its proper position, when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
But if it is close to the root of the thigh, then let the ligament be close to the knee, towards the hip joint, so that the extension lies on top of it, and the other ligament below the fracture.
And similarly, if the break is near the knee, then let the ligament be placed close to the knee, and the extension be towards the lower parts.
And then the physician aligns the bone with both their hands until it returns to its natural shape and the bone is joined with a good union; then it is necessary to apply a poultice and a bandage, lest the limb be afflicted by an abscess. But if an abscess occurs, then you leave it for a few days until the hot abscess subsides, and then return to your treatment.
The bandage should be applied like this: you must wrap a hard bandage around the fracture, folding it two or three times and removing any excess; then wrap the leg until it reaches the ankle and the root of the thigh.
And insert a long splint between the thigh and the leg near the knee, towards the lower parts, and let the ends of the splint extend from the highest point of both parts. Then, wrap the excess bandage around the leg and thigh.
And then place splints on the thigh, over the fracture site, and place one splint over the bone of the leg.
And then fill the spaces between the leg and thigh with soft cloths, so that the fracture is stabilized.
And then begin with a strong bandage in the middle over the fracture site, with three or four wraps.  The further you move away from the fracture site with your bandage, the weaker, lighter, and more loose it should become.
And then extend the two ends of the splint downwards until they reach the ankle, and firmly hold the ends of the splints on the other side to ensure the bandage stays in place.
And then leave the bandage on it, ensuring that no swelling, abscess, or inflammation or similar issues occur to the limb; and if any of these occur, then remove the bandage and correct the problem immediately, as we have often stated.
But if there is a protruding piece of bone, then you should reposition it as best as possible.  If not, cover it and leave it, and treat the area with what preceded the remedy until it is healed.
And sometimes this thigh fracture, or whatever is similar, is held together by splints, as we have said in the section on the arm.
However, in the repair of the limb done in such a way, the patient does not experience lameness; and if it is repaired alone, or another leg is applied to it, then it is necessary that the affected limb remains lame for a while.
And know that the thigh is splinted for 40 days, or a little more, or a little less, and all this depends on the diversity of the constitutions and other predispositions.
Chapter 15.
Of the repair of a fractured kneecap.
Know that a fractured kneecap often heals, but it frequently causes a lot of attrition (wear and tear/damage).
However, if it is a fracture, it is merely a fissure or hole in its parts, with or without inflammation, and everything has happened according to the patient's feeling.
And its repair is to unite what has been broken with the aforementioned means until it is joined together as much as possible, through alignment, ease, and construction.
Then apply a poultice and place a round bandage over it, if necessary; and bind tightly with a suitable bandage.
And now consider all the methods we have described for other fractures, such as an abscess and similar conditions,  in order to oppose any accident with what is suitable for the patient until healed.
Chapter 16.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured leg.
Know that the leg has two bones, one of which is thick, and is called the thigh bone; and the other is slender, and is called the shinbone.
And the treatment for some types of leg fractures is similar to that for arm fractures, and therefore the restoration is done in the same way, and the procedure is similar.
And if both bones are broken together, the leg is completely dislocated.
And if the thinner bone is broken, the leg is dislocated forward.
And if the thicker bone is broken, you find it below, and it is one of those things that is not hidden from you.
And then administer extension, reduction, and ligation of the splints accordingly.
However, if the fracture of the leg is extensive with many fragments, the extension should be less and lighter, and alleviate it with your skill/virtue.
And if the leg requires an operation similar to that on the arm, and when you have adjusted the splints and completed your operation, then you should take pieces of willow wood, placed underneath the parts that are placed on cushions, and these should be made of palm branches, thin ones, not thick ones, and their length should be in proportion to the length of the leg, from the ankle to the bottom of the foot.
And then wrap each part in two layers of cloth according to its length, and place one on the leg on one side and the other on the other side, from the ankle to the lowest part of the foot.
And then tie the two splints in three places: at the two ends and the middle.  By this arrangement, the leg is prevented from bending to the right or left, and remains in a proper position.
And now a small wooden brace, the length of the leg, has been placed on it, to prevent movement of the leg.
And this is especially necessary for fractures, and when it is properly called a fracture.
And thereafter, examine the leg daily and carefully monitor it for any abscesses, inflammation, or other similar issues. If any such problems arise, apply appropriate remedies until it heals.
Chapter 17.
Of the fracture of the ankle and bones of the foot and toes.
Fractures of the ankle itself are not common; however, fractures of the foot bones occur sometimes; and fractures of the toes are rare, and they are usually only bruised or injured during a disruption of position.
And if the bones of the foot are fractured, and sometimes those bones are displaced from their proper positions, then the patient should place their foot on the ground in a way that is prepared as if they were walking.
Then place one foot on whatever is elevating those bones, and then press down, and press and squeeze them until they return to their proper places.
And then apply a plaster and bandage on top, and place a small table underneath the sole of the foot with two flat, wide ends on it.
Then bind that tightly with a downward-stretching bandage, after you have wrapped it in cloths and other things as you need.
And after three or four days have passed, then you untie the bandage, for you will find the bones squeezed together, or broken, or dislocated, and the operation on them is as you know.
But if any bones are broken, then restore them, and fit them together just as I have shown you and recounted to you in the restoration of the fingers of the hand. And then place a splint along the length of the broken finger, and slightly wider than it; and underneath the foot place this table, which I have already described to you, and bind it with a downward-stretching bandage.
But if the broken bones are two, three, or more, then you place a splint on each finger, of its own size, wrapped in linen; and bind the board at the lowest part of the foot, and let one end of the board be placed over the belly of the foot so that it is well supported.
And it is important for you not to forget that you should consider all the things which I have described to you in the other fractures and accidents that we have discussed; and be prepared for any accident with what is appropriate.
Chapter 18.
Of the fracture of the thigh bone, the hip bone, and the penis of the male.
When the thigh bone is fractured, then make a splint in the shape of a curved cylinder, and then incline it slightly towards the back, and let it be held behind.
Then the midwife fills the thigh with wool, and it is placed on the thigh like a bandage; and then the woman should be comforted, and slowly raise her buttocks, because all of this is for the fractured area to return to the alignment of the thigh bone, and the broken bone to return to its proper place.
And then place pillows under her back, and when she wants to urinate, the wool comes out easily and she urinates; and you reduce and return it in the manner in which you originally fixed it, and then return to her original pillow.
And do this for seven days or thereabouts, for then it is restored.
And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder and insert a reed cannula into the wound; then put the entire bladder into the thigh, and then blow into the cannula with force until the bladder is inflated within the thigh, for the fracture will return.
And then it should be filled with wool and left for several days as we have recounted, until it is healed.
And when the hip bone of a man or woman is broken, then administer in its restoration and setting what we have recounted for the hip bone.
And that which is proper is not hidden in these nearby fractures which heal slowly, especially for him who studies and uses and understands these words of my book; for that fracture signifies to you in the most effective ways the correct procedure in its restoration and reduction; thus understand well.
But in the case of a broken penis, take a bird's nest and place it over the penis; then place a cloth bandage over it, wrap it up, and leave it for three days or thereabouts, and it will be healed.
Chapter 19.
Of the restoration of fractures of the bones when they are with a plague.
It is convenient for one who has suffered a fracture with a plague, and especially if the bone is large, such as the bone of the thigh or the upper arm, or similar to them, that you apply cupping and bloodletting as soon as the conditions for bloodletting indicate it, as we have mentioned above.
And if the plague flows blood, then you should apply cupping to contain it, in such a way that you powder it with crushed brick, if no other cause is present.
And then begin restoring the fracture on that same day, and do not delay this, unless an abscess arises.
However, if a serious abscess arises, then you leave the restoration until the ninth day, until the abscess has subsided, and do not approach it on the third and fourth days, for you will cause harm.
But if the broken bone is prominent above the skin, then you must carefully reduce it and secure it with your hands with ease and minimal force.
However, if the force of your hands is not sufficient to align it, then you reduce it with your hands using this instrument; and this instrument is made of iron, its length is seven or eight inches, and its width depends on the size of the injury; and therefore, it is necessary for the physician to take three or four of them, depending on the need for the treatment of any fracture.
And it should be round, and have little thickness so that it is not doubled under the pressure during the operation.
And it should have a sharp tip, with a bevel on its edges; and the top should be thickened, tapering down from the middle to the ends.
And this is its shape:
And it is called in Greek "labre volentz atelarem", which means a small spatula.
Therefore, it is necessary that the sharp end of it be raised above the prominent end of the bone.
And you push that in one go until the bone returns, and it is secured with some bandage.
And therefore, strive to bind the broken ends of the fracture together; and if the broken end is small, and does not accept the instrument well, then it is necessary that you cut the end of that bone until the instrument has the power to do so.
However, if you cannot completely reduce the bone with what we have recounted, then cut it with whatever surgical instruments are appropriate for it, or saw it with a saw in any way that is possible for you.
And then remove any roughness or small splinters that remain in the bone.
And when the bone is reduced, and shortly after the reduction the patient experiences intense pain, know that the bone has not been returned to its natural place; and then do what you deem best to greatly benefit the patient.
When your bone restoration is complete, then soak a cloth in black astringent wine, and especially if it's summer, and do not apply a plaster or anything containing oil to the wound, for this will cause suppuration and corruption.
And then administer styptics at the time of your completion of the bone restoration, and leave the wound uncovered so that you cut the wound with scissors and use dressings according to the size of the wound.
And be very careful not to compress the wound with the fracture, for many foolish doctors have done this, and caused death or a consuming illness, or festering sores, to their patients. And if your fracture is slight, soft, different from the severity of other fractures.
And if the wound is large or severe, and you fear any of the bad accidents we have described, and you find pain in the area, then you should not apply styptics, but make a dressing of hard cloths instead of styptics and bind it tightly.
And when after a day or two the wound begins to fester, you remove the cloth you had placed over it with wine, and then administer poultices,  medicaments commonly used to treat wounds, such as tetrapharmacon and similar remedies.
And you must loosen the bandage and examine the wound each day, morning and evening, until it is healed and closed.  The limb should be prepared in such a way that the blood flow runs freely to the lower parts.
If the wound persists for many days and is neither healed nor has the pus been drained, then know that there are hidden dangers, and you must probe the wound with a probe. And of those dangers, those that can be separated, remove and extract them. Those that cannot be separated and cause pain to the limb, force you to cut them, if possible.
And if you find pus or ichor, signs of corruption or putrefaction, then you must apply a remedy appropriate to that, as described in the relevant chapter on its treatment.
And concerning this, where my discourse and your expertise are needed: when a large bone is fractured, treat it as you would a fractured thigh bone or similar fractures of large limb bones. Do not attempt to pull or extract it, for often death results. Leave it undisturbed until it can knit together, as in roughly 20 to 30 days it will mend on its own; then treat the wound if it appears necessary, and if not, leave it alone.
Chapter 20.
Of the treatment of inflammation that occurs at the site of any fracture.
Often this inflammation occurs at the site of healing from a fracture, especially if it is near a joint, and it disfigures the shape of the limb; it greatly hinders the limb's natural function.
Then consider if the inflammation is recent, and apply medicines that have a styptic effect, such as alum, vinegar, myrrh, cerussa, and similar substances; use some or all of these, mixed with styptic wine, egg white, or vinegar, and place this mixture on the inflammation over a bandage, firmly bandaging it.
And leave the bandage in place for several days without loosening it; then remove it and apply another, until the inflammation is gone; or apply a lead poultice directly to it, as lead has the property of reducing swelling in the limbs.
However, if the inflammation has hardened and become a festering sore, and it is necessary to remove it forcefully, then cut into it from the deepest part, and excise the excess or scrape it away with some raspatory until it is removed; and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 21.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture, when it is restored, and subsequently the limb remains weak and frail, not according to its natural state.
When a broken bone is set, and afterward the limb remains weak and frail, this is due to several causes: one is that the ligaments were not properly tied and bound; or due to excessive tightness of the ligaments hindering nutrient flow to the limb; or due to excessive application of remedies; or due to inappropriate movement; or due to a lack of blood in the patient's body, and their general weakness.
And the treatment for this is to give the patient nourishment and to make their body gentle until the blood is replenished; and administer baths; and introduce joy and cheerfulness.
And afterward, place a poultice on the limb, so that the poultice draws more nourishment to it, or continue applying warm water compresses, until the nourishment flows and returns to its natural form.
Chapter 22.
Of the treatment of broken bones when they are improperly set and the operation is not performed as it should be.
When, after the limb has been repaired, a deformity, prominence, or knot appears on the broken bone, and this affects the limb's shape, but nevertheless the limb is not prevented from its natural operation, then you should not accept the word of anyone who claims that the limb has been broken again.
But there have been many foolish doctors and bone setters who have done this in our land, and this practice is highly reprehensible, leading to great fear, the sign of which is death.
However, if the lameness and swelling are recent, then they should be bathed in lukewarm water in which softening herbs are soaked, such as the leaves of marshmallow and its roots, and melilot, and similar ones; and a softening poultice should be made, such as well-prepared diachylon; or a mucilage of marshmallow root should be made, mixed with a dove's fat or with cedar oil, and applied as a poultice; or fatty figs should be crushed with pigeon dung, and similar medicines known as consolidants.
And sometimes it is dissolved by diligent, gentle friction with moist hands, and movement of the limb should be administered in all directions at all hours.
However, if the deformity has progressed and become strong, hardened, and difficult to treat, then it is necessary to cease the pus, and the bone's continuity should be resolved.  That which is superfluous or swollen on the bone itself should be incised with fine incisions, and the process should be managed with care and effort.
Chapter 23.
Concerning the treatment of dislocation.
Dislocation is the displacement of any joint from its proper place, thereby hindering movement and distorting the shape of the limb, causing significant pain and injury to the patient.
Therefore, when an accident causes dislocation, it is necessary to immediately attempt reduction, and delay is not advisable, for if delayed, the area becomes inflamed and firmly bound, making the reduction of the dislocation difficult.
And therefore, it is not advisable to move or stretch the affected area during inflammation, for it often causes spasms and prolonged pain.
But when this occurs, you should attend to the patient's inflamed area, and let it rest until the inflammation subsides; then the limb should be bathed with warm water and oil, and the dislocation should be reduced easily.
And every limb is cured by this, so that the displaced part returns to its proper place.
And I have already ordered the chapters on dislocation, following the precedent for fractures; from the highest part of the body down to the lowest.
Chapter 24.
Of the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
So that the two jaws are dislocated if not promptly; and the dislocation of both is done in one of two ways: either they are slightly displaced from their positions and slightly softened; or they are completely dislocated and fully dislocated, until they are softened at the joint, such that the patient is drooling and cannot hold or close their mouth, and the patient's tongue is swollen.
However, when the dislocation is slight, it often returns on its own with little effort.
But if the dislocation is complete and full, then a gentle reduction of it must be administered, and it must not be delayed at all.
And the minister must hold the patient's head, and the physician must insert the index finger of one hand into the site of the jaw dislocation within the patient's mouth, if the dislocation is on one side; or insert both thumbs together, if the dislocation is on both sides, and the other fingers of their hands should be outside, holding and steadying it; and the physician commands the patient to close their jaw and to move each part to and fro; and the physician straightens the jaw and pushes it until it returns to its place.
And if the reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are dislocated, then apply a vaporization with hot water and oil, until the reduction of both is easy and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
And thus, when they are returned and are straightened, and the patient's mouth is closed and not swollen, then place over them powders of cloth with a plaster, and it should be made from beeswax and oil rubbed together; and then it should be bound with soft bandages easily.
And let the patient sleep on their back, and let their head be placed between two pillows, so that it does not tilt to the right or left side; and do not force them to chew anything; let their food be soft, until when the pains are gone and the jaws are healed, then let them eat whatever they want.
And this should be administered easily, and do not force the opening of the mouth for eating, drinking, and breathing, until the jaw is healed and sound.
And if the reduction of the two jaws, when dislocated at any time and not returned to their places, then often fevers and persistent sweating occur, and the patient's abdomen is easily agitated, and easily vomits pure bile.
And when you see this, then know that it is perilous; and often the person who experiences this dies within ten days.
Chapter 25.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the collarbone and the extremity of the muscle.
The collarbone is not dislocated from the inside by its connection with the foot; and sometimes it is dislocated outwards, and this is evident to the touch.
And the restoration is that the patient lie on their back and extend their arms. And then firmly press the area with your palm, for it will return.
And then place a poultice and powders on that area and compress it.
However, the extremity of the one that is sitting on the elbow and that continues with it, is not dislocated in any day; therefore, it must be reduced and bound according to what we have said and according to what is appropriate for you.
And then apply a poultice, powders, and a bandage to it, and instruct the patient to adhere to quietness and rest until healed.
And with this same treatment, the extremity of the muscle is reduced when it is separated from its place.
Chapter 26.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the muscle.
Know that the muscle is not dislocated in only one way, but in three, one of which is that it is dislocated from the top part to the lower part.
And the second is that it is dislocated from the upper part of the muscle, and this is done slowly; and it is not dislocated behind, towards the shoulder; nor is it dislocated in front, towards the nerves.
And it is mostly dislocated and comes out from the lower parts towards the top, and especially in those with little flesh, it is easily dislocated; but in those with a lot of flesh, it is expelled with difficulty.
And often accidents befall some men through blows or falls, and their muscle is inflamed with a hot abscess, and is swollen because it is already dislocated.
Therefore, you must examine this until you are completely sure of it, and then proceed with its treatment.
And the dislocation is recognized when it is in the lower parts near the neck; so that you distinguish between the dislocated and healthy muscle, for you find a clear difference between them; and you find a hollow at the head of the muscle, and below the neck, towards the contact of the head of the muscle, just like an egg, and the patient cannot bring their hand to their ear, nor to any other movement.
If it is dislocated towards the foot or upwards, and you then find a clear touch, and it is not hidden.
And a slight reduction of this dislocation is done when it is fresh and the patient is young.
And the reduction of it is that the attendant raises the patient's hand to the upper parts; and then places your fingers under the patient's neck, and raises the joint with force back to its place, and the attendant raises the patient's hand, and extends it upward; and then places it down, so that it returns easily.
But if, despite what we have said, it does not return, and it has been a significant time since the dislocation occurred, then the patient should be bathed in warm water and a poultice should be administered that softens and smooths, like if it were concoctions of rose root, fenugreek, and melilot in water, and should be administered.
And then the patient should be placed on their back, with a wool pad placed under their neck, that is neither too loose nor too tight, and then the doctor places their heel on the pad, and raises the head of the muscle with force, and pulls the patient's hand to prevent it from being moved downward, and another attendant holds the patient's head, preventing it from being moved downward, so that it will quickly return to its place.
And if you wish, reduce it in this manner: you have a man present who is taller than the patient, standing on the affected side, and place their arm under the patient's neck and raise the neck upward until the patient is suspended in the air, and another attendant pulls the patient's hand from below their stomach. But if the patient is light, then something else should be suspended with the patient, to weigh it down, so that the dislocation returns quickly.
And it is easily reduced by another method: a long wooden post, round at the top like a mortar pestle, neither thick nor thin, is placed under the patient.  Then, thick blankets are placed on top of the post's head, and the patient is positioned along the length of the post, and then the patient's hand is extended downward on one side, and the patient's body is pulled forcefully to the opposite side, so the joint returns to its place quickly.
And if the reduction is difficult by all the methods we have described, then use this treatment: take a post that is the length of two arms and a width of four fingers, and its thickness should be lightened to the level of the neck in this way:
And on the round head, thick cloths should be tied so that the post does not injure the patient.
And then place the post under the patient's armpit, and extend the hand or arm down the post, and tie the post to the armpit, arm, and hand's extremity.
Then the arm should be placed on a ladder-like support, the width of which is appropriate, and the hand extended downwards, and the other side of the body should be pulled to the other side, so that the joint will enter quickly.
And when its insertion is complete, according to whatever way is possible for you, then place a layer of wool under the armpit; and then apply a prepared plaster of ground powder with vinegar and egg white over the entire muscle, just as it is wrapped around; and then tightly bandage under the armpit, and the bandage should be wrapped around the plaster, and the patient's hand should be tied to their neck, and left undisturbed, and the hand should not be moved for seven days.
And the patient's food should be restricted, until the limb is strengthened, for this is more conducive to its healing.
And subsequently, the food should be increased, after seven or five [days], and movement should be encouraged; and if it is then firm and not softened, then it is healed.
But if the joint is repeatedly dislocated due to humidity affecting it or another cause, then a cautery with three openings should be applied, as previously described in the chapter on cauteries.
And if all this is done, and after seven days the bandage and joint are loosened, and the plaster and bandage are reapplied several times, and it is still not fixed, and the arm cannot be raised to its normal position, then know that the nerves, which are at the end of the muscle, are severed or stretched or softened, and know that the joint will never be properly placed back in its socket.
For dislocations of the foot and nipple or posterior parts, reduction is performed by pushing and pulling with the hands until it returns to its place.
And another bandage should be applied to that area, and treat it until it is healed.
And if after healing there is heaviness in the limb and slowness in its movement, then the patient should use baths frequently until that heaviness is relieved and it returns to its original natural state.
Chapter 27.
Concerning the treatment of elbow dislocations.
Know that the elbow joint is dislocated with difficulty and similarly returns with difficulty; and it is dislocated in all directions, and mostly in the front to the back, and its dislocation is not hidden from you, for it is evident from the pressure or from the touch, depending on what figure it is dislocated, and when you adjust the dislocated elbow with your hand, this is clearly evident to you, for the joint is recessed and cannot bend the arm, nor touch its muscle.
And you should act quickly, and reduce the dislocation before it becomes severely inflamed, for if it becomes severely inflamed, its reduction is difficult, and the limb is not completely healed; and especially when it is in the back parts, for it is worse than all other types of dislocation, and causes more pain, and often results in death.
And the restoration of it, when it is possible to reduce it, is that the patient's hand is extended with two hands, and the patient's arm is extended, and the physician's hands are on the elbow, and below it, and he presses the joint with the thumbs of his hands together, or with the heel of his palm until it returns to its place.
However, if the dislocation is in the front parts, then it returns easily by bending the hand with his hand, until he strikes the elbow with the heel of his palm.
And if the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer vigorous extension and strong force.
And it is that two assistants extend the arm, and hold that extension so that it is not withdrawn from the extension.
And then the arm should be rotated in every direction, since it is wrapped in a doubled cloth long over his hand, or a bandage.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, anoint it with some oil so that those things which aid in the lubrication of the joint will be done with ease; and then press the joint with forceful pressure until it returns.
And after the reduction, it is necessary that it be placed on a poultice in which there is astringency and binding with egg white, and be bound with a descending bandage, and the arm be suspended from the neck, and left for several days.
And then it should be unbound; and therefore if the joint is formed in its place, then you untie the bandage from it and leave it.
And if you see the joint is not well tightened, then you repeat the poultice and bandage, and leave it for several days until it is tightened; and then unbind it.
But if it happens that there is great difficulty during the reduction of the joint, and the condition is moving, then administer moistening baths and gentle friction and pressure, until it is relieved; or place a soothing poultice over the joint, perhaps a fatty substance; and then bandage it and leave it over the wound day and night. And then remove that, and put it in a bath; and when it is soaked, then you rub the joint with moderate friction, and then repeat the soothing poultice, perhaps a warmed substance, one time, and a second, and a third, with intervals of bathing, until it is healed.
And if you wish, place over the wound a warm, damp cow's hide, and press firmly on it; and do this several times, for this is moist and will restore it to its proper position.
Chapter 28.
Concerning the treatment of dislocations of the arm, that is, of the joint between the arm and the hand.
A dislocation of the arm sometimes occurs, and the reduction of its dislocation is relatively easy, unlike other joints. However, speed is essential in reducing it at the time of the dislocation, before the area becomes suppurated or a harmful abscess develops.
And the reduction of its dislocation is done by placing the patient's hand joint on a table, while an assistant extends the hand, and the physician places their hand or palm on the area of the joint, pressing until it returns to its proper place.
However, it's necessary to consider if the palm has already moved to the back of the hand. If so, the patient should place the back of their hand on a table in front of the extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is towards the back of the hand, then the patient's hand should be positioned on the table, so that the physician's hand can be placed over the joint area.
And so if it returns quickly, it's good; but if not, then gently restrain it with a bandage to prevent an abscess, and leave it.  Do not repeat this, for otherwise it will not tolerate nor will it be possible to complete the reduction after a few days have passed.
However, if the joint remains in its twisted position, and the patient feels no pain, but only that the hand is weakened and cannot grip anything, then you should know that the nerves are severed or bruised.  In this case, there is no remedy other than to cauterize the area near the injury, applying heat in order to alleviate the problem.
Therefore, once the injury has been reduced, apply the bandage we previously described and leave it on and fastened for five days; then, it should be untied and left, and the patient's hand should be exercised.
But if the movement is hindered and some heaviness occurs, then alleviate it with warm water and some rubbing several times until it is relieved.
Chapter 29.
Of the treatment of dislocation of the fingers.
Sometimes the fingers are dislocated in various ways.
And when any of these fingers are dislocated towards the palm side or the back of the hand, then straighten the finger and push the dislocation back with your fingers until it returns to its place.
And then support the head of the finger, and suspend it over the part to which it is dislocated, and leave it like that for two days.
And then suspend it and extend it until it is properly aligned in its socket.
And when night comes, bind it according to that narrative, and do not cease to unbind and exercise it with movement during the day, and bind it at night; and do this for several days until it is straight.
And do likewise if it is dislocated towards the back of the hand, and bind it to that part and do your initial procedure on it, until it is healed.
And similarly do this when it is dislocated in other parts of the body.
Chapter 30.
Of the treatment of dislocation of the vertebrae of the spine.
When an accident causes a complete dislocation of any of the vertebrae of the spine or neck, and the affected vertebrae remain significantly displaced from their proper positions, then in that case there is no cure, for death awaits the patient.
And a sign of this is that the patient's urination is involuntary, and they cannot control it; and often, some of the patient's limbs or both legs or both arms, or one of them, are paralyzed, or a vertebra is displaced from its place; for often one is displaced, and the removal of such is often done in four parts.
Therefore, when it has been displaced to the rear parts, it is called a posterior dislocation.
But the treatment of this is that you must consider if the posterior dislocation occurred from childhood, for in that case there is no cure or complete recovery.
However, for those caused by accident or impact or similar incidents, the Ancients described various methods of treatment in lengthy accounts, of which the most useful return; but I will summarize that, omitting the excessive detail, for the sake of brevity in my explanation and exposition.  I have prepared this using various instruments, by means of which I will demonstrate and explain it.
And only those dislocations that occur in the back, specifically as I describe, can be cured.
And the patient should be laid on their face, close to a wall, and something soft should be placed beneath them, so that their foot is not injured.
And then a straight pole should be placed, inserted into the ground near the patient's head, and another pole similarly placed near their feet, at the other end of the raised surface.
And one attendant should hold the pole, and it shouldn't be anchored in the ground; and another attendant should hold the other pole in the same manner.
And then wrap a bandage around the patient's head, and underneath their temples securely; and extend the end of the bandage to the pole near the patient's head, and tie it there; and then tightly bind the other bandage around their hips, their buttocks, and down their legs; and finally adjust all the bandages and tie them to the other pole near their feet.
And now each attendant should extend the bandage to the part of the pole, and the two poles and bandages should not be moved from the positions in which they are placed, but still not fixed as we have described.
And the physician should place his palms on the spine with strength until it returns to its place, or should place a table upon it, and then carefully adjust the patient's feet onto the table until they return to a normal position.
But if it does not return in this way, then take a table whose width or length is approximately three arms' lengths, and hollow out a space in the wall, which we mentioned is near the patient, where the end of the table can be placed. And then place half of the table on the spine, and the physician places his feet on the other end, and presses firmly until the spine is compressed and returns to its proper position.
And if you wish, open with the wheel that is turned by hand; and you place in the ground, at the patient's head, two poles, each one arm's length long, and at the patient's feet, also two poles; and the space between each pole is one palm wide.  Each pole has a hole through which the wheel turns, and each is firmly fixed to the ground so it does not move.
And a round post, on which the wheel is mounted, is placed in the two holes of the two poles.
And at the end of this post (the one with the wheel) is a hole into which the post is fitted, the length of which is a palm's width including what is turned around; and the other two poles have a similar arrangement.
And now tighten the bandages you have placed on the patient's feet around the hole near their head, and the bandages on their thighs around the wheel near their feet.
And near each wheel, there should be an attendant who turns the wheel with their hand, while the physician adjusts the spine as we have described.
And after the spine has been repositioned and the area is aligned, you must apply a dry poultice made with egg white and then some cloth; then place over the poultice a piece of wooden board whose width is roughly three fingers and whose length covers the affected area of the joint, and over this board place the spine.
And now it should be bound with the bandage we have mentioned, and the patient should use subtle nourishment until they are healed.
And if any lingering inflammation remains in the area at the end of the healing process, then medicines must be administered which soften and soothe with prolonged application of the plate, as we have described; and a lead plate is to be applied to that area.
And sometimes inflammation occurs in the lower part of the spinal column, caused by that dislocation.  That bone, if inflamed, is not to be treated with this medicine, because forcing it will cause you to incur death.
Chapter 31.
Of the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the joint of the muscle can only be dislocated, and the problems affecting them are not like those affecting other joints with minor displacement and concavity.
And the hip joint is dislocated in four ways, for it is dislocated inwards, or outwards, or forwards, or backwards.
And it is mostly dislocated inwards, and less so forwards or backwards.
A sign of the hip's dislocation inwards is that when you align the patient's healthy leg with the affected leg, the affected leg is longer and the knee is more prominent than on the healthy side, and the patient cannot bend their foot toward the groin, and the area where the groin would be is inflamed with a hot abscess, because the head of the thigh bone has likely migrated there.
And the sign of dislocation outwards is that the symptoms are the opposite of this inwards dislocation.
The sign of a forward dislocation is that the patient can extend their leg fully, but cannot bend it without pain in the knee, and if they try to move it forward, they cannot; and the patient's urine is retained, and the groin is swollen, and the patient walks on the outside of their foot.
The sign of a backward dislocation is that the knee does not extend, nor can the patient bend the knee forward to touch the groin, and the patient's leg is shorter than the other, and the groin is soft, and the head of the thigh bone is towards that area.
However, the reduction of these types of dislocations is that you must consider, at that time, if the dislocation is old and chronic, and has already been present for a time.  If you attempt to reduce it then you should expect only partial success; and, therefore, no presumption of complete healing should be made in this case.
But in the case of a recent dislocation, one of the four types, then you should try to return the joint to its proper position, extending it both inwards and outwards, and moving it to the right and left, because it will easily return and needs no further treatment.
However, if it does not return in this manner, then you must prepare strong assistants and extend the patient's leg from the lower part, either by using their own hands, or by using a bandage tied to the leg above the knee; and another assistant should hold the patient's hand above so that they place their hands beneath the patient's armpits; and then hold tightly with a strong bandage over the root of the thigh, and let a third assistant hold the other end of the bandage.
And this extension should be either from the inside of the knee to the groin, or from the back to the buttock.
And this extension should be done all at once until the patient is raised off the ground by their body weight and hangs suspended.
And this type of extension is a common procedure for all four types.
And so, if the dislocation returns with what we have described, it is good; and if not, then for every type, it is necessary to proceed to the specific treatment.
In the specific reduction of a dislocation, when the dislocation is inward, the patient should be laid on their healthy side; then apply bandages around the thigh's root, in the area between the thigh's head and the space below the groin; and then extend the bandage upward from the groin to the highest part of the pubic bone.
And then another strong assistant should take the patient's arms, and firmly hold the affected thigh's large part and extend it outward forcefully, for this forceful extension will return it to its proper place.
And this type is easier to treat, with which this limb is reduced than the other types.
But if you are hesitant and do not obey the entire procedure with this type of treatment, then it is necessary to tie the patient's two feet together with a strong, light bandage, around both ankles and both heels.
And let the length of each be about four fingers, and let the affected leg be extended more than the other by about two fingers.
And then let the patient be suspended over the head of the bed, and let them be in the house; and let their distance from the ground be about two arms' length.
And then command a strong young man to grasp the top of the thigh, and let another young man support the patient, and the other young man, pressing the thigh with strength, will propel it, so that the joint returns to its place quickly.
But in the proper reduction, when it is a dislocation to the outside, the patient should lie on their back, as we have described in the previous mention of the flexed knee.  The bandage should be tightened tightly around the affected leg and the patient's feet.
And then two staffs should be placed, one at their feet, and the other at their head; and then staffs fitted together in the middle of the hill should be placed, wrapped with damp cloths, so the patient isn't harmed because the staff is between their thighs, so that the extension towards the lower parts isn't pulled.
And then each attendant should extend in their respective direction, and the physician with their hand guides the reduction according to the art.
And when the dislocation is in the front parts, then it is necessary that the leg be slightly extended, and it is according to this same method on a hill; and the physician places the palm of their right hand on the injured limb, and then grasps it with the other hand, and with that grasping, positions the extended part towards the lower part of the affected area.
And if the dislocation is in the rear parts, then it is not necessary for the patient to be extended downwards, and they are elevated from the ground; instead, they should be placed on a hard surface, as is appropriate for the repositioning if the leg is dislocated outwards, according to what we have said about the patient lying on two supports; and they should be on their face, and the ligaments extended, as we have said, not very long.
And pressure should be applied with a board over the area where the joint is.
Therefore, when the repositioning is complete—and the sign of its completion is clear to you, which is that you extend the patient's legs, and when you see them equal and the patient tightens their leg and stretches it without hesitation, then know that the limb has been correctly returned—and then you close the thighs and apply a bandage, and bind tightly so that the leg is not moved in any direction; and keep the patient at rest for three or four days.
And then loosen the bandage and compare one leg with the other.
Now, if you see them equal in appearance, then know that the dislocation is now fixed, and then let the patient be.
And if you see any cause of softening, then you reposition and bandage it, and bind it as you did initially, and leave it for three days.
And then loosen it, and have the patient walk slowly for several days, until they are better.
Chapter 32.
Of the treatment of a dislocated knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways, for it is dislocated outwards, inwards, and downwards, and backwards; and it is not dislocated forwards.
And the sign of a dislocated knee is that you tell the patient to apply their leg to their thigh; then you will know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration in all these ways is that you have the patient lie down straight, so that they extend their leg, if possible; place your arm on their side, and put their foot between your feet; and then apply your palms to their knee, and hold them between your fingers firmly on their knee, and another assistant extends their foot until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of its reduction is that their leg adheres to their thigh with effortless ease.
And then bandage that and adhere the leg to the thigh, and then bind them together with a bandage for three or four days, and then unbind them.
And do not allow the patient to walk for several days, until they are healed and strengthened.
But if the reduction is not possible for you, then administer strong extension with the ligaments, which in the treatment of the hip were previously discussed.
Chapter 33.
Of the treatment of the dislocation of the ankle.
The ankle is sometimes displaced by a small movement, and sometimes dislocated completely, and its dislocation is either inward or outward.
And the sign of its dislocation is that sometimes the ankle swells on the side to which it is dislocated.
But the treatment for a slight displacement is a gentle return, and it is that it be extended easily with the hands and held until it returns to its place.
However, in the treatment of a complete dislocation, it is necessary to have the patient lie down straight, and have a strong and capable assistant on the back of the patient, in the middle of the affected area.
And then, you, with your right hand, take hold of the foot at the highest point, and with your left hand, the lower part of the foot, at the location of the ankle.
And then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, and then with your left hand, twice, and then extend with the left hand; and push the foot of the leg with the right hand towards the leg without difficulty.
And do this twice, as we have counted.
And then push the foot of the leg a third time, and you are at the ankle.
Therefore, if it returns in one or two attempts according to this method, and if you see the foot swelling, it is good; and if not, then repeat the operation, for it (the foot) returns.
But if you are prohibited from reducing it with what we have recounted, then have the patient lie on their back on the ground, and firmly fix a thick plank on the ground, and place it between the patient's thighs, and you have already covered them with a cloth so as not to harm the patient.
And then the minister of the patient should hold the patient's thighs, and another minister should extend the leg with their hands or with a bandage tied to the back of the leg; and each minister should extend against the extension of their partner, and the plank should be placed between the patient's thighs to hold it, so that the body is not pulled down toward its extension.
And then the physician grasps the dislocation with their hands, and another minister holds the healthy thigh from below, for the dislocation returns with ease.
And thus, when the dislocation returns and the success of the reduction is clear, then apply a plaster and firmly bind it with bandages, and bandage the foot to the leg.
And it is necessary to be careful of the nerves which are behind the heel, so that the bandage placed there is not forceful and harms them severely.
And then leave it for two or three days; and if the bandage has softened, tighten it.
And then loosen it on the third or fourth day, and prohibit the patient from movement for forty days. But if forced to move before that period, there is no certainty that the dislocation will not be dissolved and corrupted, and subsequently will not receive a cure.
And if a boil happens, then it is necessary that you apply to it the treatment described in our earlier discussion, a different location for care and application, until it is gone.
Chapter 34.
Concerning the treatment of dislocations of the toes of the feet.
It is necessary that the dislocated part be gently manipulated without effort; and this is not difficult, rather easy.
However, if the dislocation is in some of the joints of the foot, then it is necessary to make the patient place their foot on a soft, level surface, like the ground or a table, and to be positioned as if walking.
And then you stand, placing your foot over the area where the joints are affected.  Then firmly press down on the affected area with your foot, until it returns to its proper place and you see it is now aligned, and no longer appears to be out of position.
Then place a board beneath the sole of the foot that encompasses the entire foot, having two ends.  Secure it with appropriate and firm bandages for three days; then loosen the bandages and allow rest from walking for several days, until the area is fully healed and the restoration is assured.
Chapter 35.
Of the types of dislocation that occur with a bruise, or a fracture, or both together.
When any of these causes occur, and you exert yourself excessively in the treatment and restoration of the patient, death often results.
Therefore, it is not appropriate for anyone to presume to treat such a patient, but only one who is knowledgeable in the art, experienced, compassionate, and patient, not hasty or presumptuous.  In the beginning, let them only use remedies that reduce intense inflammation, and leave the patient to divine judgment, only hoping for a return to health and a lessening of the illness, if there appears any hope at all. Then, strive to perform the reduction as soon as possible, before the inflammation becomes severe.
And if the limb returns as desired, then administer a regimen that reduces inflammation and treats the bruise with appropriate desiccant ointments.
And if a dislocation is accompanied by a fracture and the bones are separated, then you must treat the separation, and do in this regard what we have described in the simple ailments of similar cases previously mentioned.
And, according to your ability, avoid harm to the internal organs by following the instructions I have given you; for this is best for you before your Creator, most lasting for your glory, and most healthful for your body in your time. Thus ends the words of Albucasis, may God be blessed by them. Amen.
